The Objection of Threat and the Responses on Breach and Lying
Taftazani presents the scriptural objection and multiple responses regarding khulf and lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural objection that God's forgiveness would involve breach and lying, and the multiple responses that resolve this issue.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is not a lie – breaking a threat is generosity, not falsehood.

The apparent tension between God's threats and His mercy troubled theologians. If God threatens sinners with punishment, then forgives them, does that imply breach (khulf) and lying (kadhib)? Taftazani presents the objection and several responses from Sunni scholars, showing the problem is not as simple as it seems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses indicating missing words – these are OCR artifacts.
2
Identify the objection and the main responses
The objector claims that forgiving sinners after threats would involve breach and lying. The responses offer several distinctions: promise vs. threat, exclusion from general meaning, and counterarguments about lying and eternity.
3
Analyze the distinction between promise and threat
Understand that breaking a promise (wa'd) is invalid by consensus, but breaking a threat (wa'id) is considered generosity (karm) by some scholars. This distinction is central to the response.
Promise = wa'd, threat = wa'id.
4
Consider the issue of lying and the Zayd analogy
The analogy of a person who says 'I will kill Zayd tomorrow' and then spares him illustrates that a change in a threat is not necessarily a lie. Reflect on how this supports the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by warning that permitting lying in divine information leads to many evils. Consider why this final point is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وسمعاً بالنصوص الواردة في وعيد الفساق ، فإن الخلف والكذب نقص بالاتفاق (۱). ورد بأنهم داخلون في عمومات الوعد والخلف في الوعد باطل بالإجماع، بخلاف الخلف في الوعيد فإنه كرم جوزه البعض. نعم حديث لزوم الكذب وتبديل القول مشكل ( ) فالأولى القول بإخراجهم من عموم اللفظ وبأنه ليس نسخاً ليمتنع في الخير، وأما القول بأن الكذب يجري في المستقبل فضعيف جداً ) وكذا القول بأن صدق كلامه عندنا أزلي، فلا يتغير. والكذب عندكم إنما امتنع لقبحه ولا قبح ههنا لتوقف العفو عليه. كمن أخبر أنه يقتل زيداً غداً، فلم يقتله . وذلك لأن أزلية الصدق تقتضي ترك العفو وجواز الكذب في إخباره يفضي إلى مفاسد لا تحصى
He said: And as for scriptural [evidence], by the texts concerning the threat against sinners, for [fulfilling] the threat and lying are deficiency by consensus. And it was responded that they are included in the generalities of [God's] promise, and breaking a promise is invalid by consensus, unlike breaking a threat, for it is generosity, as some allowed. Yes, the matter of the necessity of lying and changing the statement is problematic. So the better [view] is to say that they are excluded from the general meaning of the text, and that it is not abrogation to prevent [change] in good. As for the saying that lying occurs in the future, it is very weak. Likewise the saying that the truthfulness of His speech is eternal in our view, so it does not change. And lying according to you is only prevented due to its evil, and there is no evil here because forgiveness depends on it. Like someone who informs that he will kill Zayd tomorrow and then does not kill him. And that is because the eternity of truthfulness necessitates withholding forgiveness, and the permissibility of lying in His information leads to incalculable evils.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural (sam'i) objection and several responses. The objector argues that the texts threatening sinners imply that forgiving them would involve khulf (breach) and lying (kadhib), which are deficiencies. The response offers multiple points: 1) Sinners are included in general promises of mercy, and breaking a promise (wa'd) is invalid, but breaking a threat (wa'id) is generosity, as some scholars allowed. 2) The issue of lying and changing a statement is problematic, so it is better to say that sinners are excluded from the general meaning of the threat, not that the text is abrogated. 3) The claim that lying is future is weak. 4) The argument that God's speech is eternal and unchangeable is countered: lying is prevented due to its evil, but in this context there is no evil because forgiveness depends on it. 5) The analogy: someone who says he will kill Zayd tomorrow and then does not kill him—this would be a lie, but the eternal truthfulness would require the threat to be carried out, so forgiveness would not occur. The passage concludes that permitting lying in divine information leads to many evils.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any khulf (breach of a threat) is necessarily a lie and therefore evil.
Correction: Breaking a threat is not the same as breaking a promise; it can be an act of generosity, and the distinction is recognized by scholars.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is always wrong
B) Because it is considered generosity
C) Because God can do anything

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that divine forgiveness does not entail falsehood because threats can be broken by generosity, and promises are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage warns that insisting God must fulfill every threat leads to 'incalculable evils.' In your own life, are there grudges or predictions you hold onto because you feel they must come true? Are you willing to let go of one of these, trusting that mercy is greater than consistency?

Basran Mu'tazila: Scriptural Impossibility of Forgiveness
Taftazani presents the Basran Mu'tazila argument that forgiveness is scripturally impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basran Mu'tazila argument that forgiveness is scripturally impossible despite being rationally possible, based on definitive Quranic threats.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is rationally possible but scripturally impossible, according to the Basran Mu'tazila.

This lesson examines the position of the Basran Mu'tazila and some Baghdadis. They argue that while reason does not rule out divine forgiveness, scripture contains definitive threats of punishment for major sins, making forgiveness impossible in practice. Taftazani presents their case through specific Quranic verses and general texts. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the sectarian debates on divine forgiveness and justice.

Guided Path
1
Locate the central claim
Read the passage and identify the two-part claim about forgiveness: rational possibility and scriptural impossibility.
Note that the Basran Mu'tazila do not deny rational possibility.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
List the specific Quranic verses cited and note that they are about major sins. Also note the reference to general texts about immortality.
The verses are from Surah An-Nisa (4:30, 4:14) and Surah Al-Anfal (8:16).
3
Understand the logical consequence
Trace the argument: if the threat (وعيد) is certain, then forgiveness would entail a breach (خلف) and lying (كذب).
This is a conditional argument based on the truthfulness of God.
4
Reflect on the position's uniqueness
Consider why the Basran Mu'tazila accept rational possibility but reject actual forgiveness. This distinguishes them from other groups.
This will be important for comparing with Ash'ari and Maturidi views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك القائلون بجواز العفو عقلاً، وامتناعه سمعاً، وهم البصريون من المعتزلة، وبعض البغدادية بالنصوص الواردة في وعيد الفساق وأصحاب الكبائر إما بالخصوص كقوله تعالى في أكل أموال الناس : ومن يفعل ذلك عدواناً وظلماً فسوف نصليه نارا (5) وفي التولي عن الزحف ومأواه جهنم وبئس المصير (6) وفي تعدي حدود المواريث ويدخله ناراً خالداً فيها (7) وإما بالدخول في العمومات المذكورة في بحث الخلود. وإذا تحقق الوعيد . فلو تحقق العفو، وترك العقوبة بالنار لزم الخلف في الوعيد والكذب
Those who hold the permissibility of forgiveness rationally and its impossibility scripturally, and they are the Basrans of the Mu'tazila and some of the Baghdadis, adhere to the texts concerning the threat against sinners and perpetrators of major sins, either specifically like His statement concerning consuming people's wealth: 'Whoever does that aggressively and unjustly, We will burn him in a Fire' (5), and concerning turning away from battle: 'his abode is Hell, and an evil destination' (6), and concerning transgressing the limits of inheritance: 'He will enter him into a Fire, abiding therein' (7), or by entering under the generalities mentioned in the discussion of immortality. And if the threat is certain, then if forgiveness and withholding of punishment by fire were to occur, a breach in the threat and lying would follow.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the Basran Mu'tazila and some Baghdadis. They hold that forgiveness is rationally possible (ja'iz 'aqlan) but scripturally impossible (mumtani' sam'an). Their evidence consists of specific scriptural texts that threaten sinners and perpetrators of major sins with punishment. For example, verses about consuming people's wealth unjustly (4:30), fleeing from battle (3:162/8:1), and violating inheritance laws (4:14) explicitly threaten Hellfire. Additionally, general texts about immortality in Hell apply. Since these threats are certain (mutaḥaqqiq), if forgiveness were to occur and punishment withheld, it would result in khulf (breach) in God's threat and lying (kadhib). Therefore, even though reason allows forgiveness, scripture precludes it.

Correction: They actually affirm rational possibility but deny scriptural possibility due to definitive threats.

Key Takeaway

The Nature of Intercession: Removing Harm, Not Seeking Benefits
Author defines intercession as removing harm, not seeking benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'aris define intercession as removing harm (isqat al-madar) rather than seeking benefits, and how this definition supports intercession for major sinners.

Core Idea
Intercession removes punishment, not grants extra rewards.

In this lesson, we explore the definitional foundation of intercession (shafa'a). The Ash'aris argue that if intercession were about seeking benefits, we would be intercessors for the Prophet when we ask for his honor. Instead, it is about removing harm, which opens the door for intercession for major sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the core definition
Read the Arabic passage where the author defines intercession as removing harm (isqat al-madar) and contrasts with the Mu'tazila view that it is for seeking benefits.
2
Understand the logical implication
Notice how the author uses the Mu'tazila's own principles (no punishment after repentance, no minor sin when avoiding major) to argue that intercession must be for removing major sins.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this definition is crucial for the debate about intercession for sinners.

Source Text

المبحث الثالث عشر - يجوز عندنا الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر في حقها ، لما سبق من دلائل العفو، وما تواتر معنى من ادخار الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر. وقد يستدل بعموم قوله تعالى : ( واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين ) وبأن أصل الشفاعة ثابت بالنص والإجماع، وليست حقيقة لطلب المنافع على ما يراه المعتزلة، وإلا لكنا شافعين للنبي الحين نسأل الله تعالى زيادة كرامته . بل لإسقاط المضاد. وعندكم لا عقاب مع التوبة، ولا صغيرة مع اجتناب الكبيرة، فتعين كونها لإسقاط الكبائر، تمسكت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - عمومات نفي الشفاعة مثل قوله تعالى : لا يقبل منها شفاعة فما تنفعهم شفاعة الشافعين من قبل أن يأتي يوم لا بيع فيه ولا خلة ولا شفاعة وما للظالمين من حميم ولا شفيع يطاع وما للظالمين من أنصار والجواب - بعد تسليم عموم الأزمان والأحوال التخصيص بالكبائر جمعاً بين الأدلة على أن الظلم المطلق هو الكفر، ونفي الناصر لا ينفي الشفيع . الثاني - آيات تنفي شفاعة صاحب الكبيرة ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضی فاغفر للذين تابوا . والجواب - أن الفاسق مرتضى من جهة الإيمان. والمراد تابوا عن الشرك، لأن من تاب عن المعاصي وعمل صالحاً فطلب مغفرته ،عبث أو طلب لترك الظلام الثالث - آيات خلود الفساق وقد مر الرابع - الإجماع على صحة اللهم اجعلنا من أهل شفاعة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم . والجواب - إن أهلية شفاعته على تقدير العصيان إنما هو بالطاعة والإيمان . في الشفاعة يدل على ثبوتها النص والإجماع . إلا أن المعتزلة قصروها على المطيعين والتائبين لرفع الدرجات وزيادة المثوبات . وعندنا يجوز لأهل الكبائر أيضاً في حط السيئات، إما في العرصات، وإما بعد دخول النار لما سبق من دلائل العفو عن الكبيرة، ولما اشتهر بل تواتر معنى من ادخار الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر، كقوله ( عليه السلام): «ادخرت شفاعتي لأهل الكبائر من أمتي » وترك العقاب بعد التوبة واجب عندهم، فليس للعفو والشفاعة كثير معنى . وقد يستدل بقوله تعالى : واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين . أي لذنوب المؤمنين، فيعم الكبائر وبقوله تعالى في حق الكفار : فما تنفعهم شفاعة الشافعين فإن مثل هذا الكلام إنما يساق حيث تنفع الشفاعة غيرهم ، فيقصد تقبيح حال الكفرة. وتخييب رجائهم، بأنهم ليسوا كذلك إذ لو لم تنفع الشفاعة أحداً لما كان في تخصيصهم زيادة تخييب وتوبيخ لهم. لكنه مع هذا التكلف لا يفيد إلا ثبوت أصل الشفاعة ولا نزاع فيه. نعم لو تم ما ذكره بعض أصحابنا من أن الشفاعة لا يجوز أن تكون حقيقية لزيادة المنافع، بل لإسقاط المضار فقط. والصغائر مكفرة عندكم باجتناب الكبائر، فتعين أن تكون لإسقاط الكبائر، لكان في إثبات أصل الشفاعة إثبات المطلوب إلا أن غاية متشبثهم في ذلك هو أن الشفاعة لو كانت حقيقة في طلب زيادة المنافع لكنا شافعين في حق النبي (عليه السلام) حين نسأل
The thirteenth section - It is permissible in our view intercession for the people of major sins in its [proper] place, due to what preceded of the proofs of pardon, and the recurrent meaning of storing up intercession for the people of major sins. It may be argued by the generality of His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believers' and because the basis of intercession is established by text and consensus, and it is not literally for seeking benefits as the Mu'tazila hold, otherwise we would be intercessors for the Prophet when we ask Allah for increase of his honor. Rather it is for removing harm. And according to you there is no punishment with repentance, and no minor sin with avoidance of major sins, so it is determined that it is for removing major sins. The Mu'tazila clung to several points: First - general verses negating intercession such as His saying: 'There will not be accepted from it any intercession', 'So the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them', 'Before the coming of a day in which there is no trading, no friendship, and no intercession', 'For the wrongdoers there is no friend and no intercessor to be obeyed', 'For the wrongdoers there are no helpers.' The answer - after conceding the generality of times and conditions, specification is made to major sins to combine the evidences, because absolute injustice is disbelief, and negation of a helper does not negate an intercessor. Second - verses negating intercession for the committer of a major sin: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased with', 'So forgive those who repent.' The answer - that the immoral person is pleasing from the aspect of faith, and the meaning is 'they repented from polytheism', because whoever repents from sins and does righteous deeds, then asking for his forgiveness is futile or a request for leaving injustice. Third - verses on eternal punishment of the immoral have passed. Fourth - consensus on the validity of 'O Allah, make us among the people of the intercession of Muhammad.' The answer - that eligibility for his intercession on the condition of disobedience is only through obedience and faith. Regarding intercession, text and consensus indicate its establishment. However, the Mu'tazila restricted it to the obedient and the repentant for raising ranks and increasing rewards. And in our view it is permissible for the people of major sins also for removing evil deeds, either in the plains [of judgment] or after entering the Fire, due to what preceded of the proofs of pardon for the major sin, and due to what is well-known, rather recurrent, of the meaning of storing up intercession for the people of major sins, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'I have stored up my intercession for the people of major sins of my community.' And leaving punishment after repentance is obligatory according to them, so there is not much meaning to pardon and intercession. It may be argued by His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believers' - i.e., for the sins of the believers, so it includes major sins. And by His saying regarding the disbelievers: 'So the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them' - for such speech is only uttered where intercession benefits others, intending to make the condition of the disbelievers ugly and to disappoint their hope, that they are not like that, because if intercession did not benefit anyone, there would be no increased disappointment and rebuke in specifying them. However, with this elaboration it only proves the establishment of intercession, about which there is no dispute. Yes, if what some of our companions mentioned were complete - that intercession cannot be literal for increasing benefits, but only for removing harms, and minor sins are expiated according to you by avoiding major sins, so it is determined that it is for removing major sins - then in proving the establishment of intercession there would be proof of the desired outcome. However, the utmost of their clinging in that is that if intercession were literal in seeking increase of benefits, we would be intercessors for the Prophet (peace be upon him) when we ask
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the permissibility of intercession (shafa'a) for major sinners (ahl al-kaba'ir) in Ash'ari theology, along with a refutation of the Mu'tazila position. The author begins by stating the Ash'ari view: intercession is permissible for major sinners, based on previous proofs of divine pardon and the tawatur (mass transmission) of the meaning that intercession is stored up for them. He then provides a rational argument: the basis of intercession is established by text and consensus, and intercession is not literally for seeking benefits (as the Mu'tazila claim), because if it were, we would be intercessors for the Prophet when we ask Allah to increase his honor. Rather, intercession is for removing harm (isqat al-madar). The author then points out an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position: according to them, there is no punishment after repentance and no minor sin if one avoids major sins, so intercession would have no function unless it removes major sins. The author then lists four objections raised by the Mu'tazila, each with an answer. The first objection cites general verses negating intercession (e.g., 'no intercession will be accepted', 'the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them'). The response: while granting the generality of time and conditions, the verses are specified to major sins to reconcile all evidences; moreover, 'absolute injustice' (zulm) is disbelief, and negating a helper does not negate an intercessor. The second objection cites verses that limit intercession to those whom Allah is pleased with (e.g., 'they intercede only for whom He approves'). The response: the immoral person (fasiq) is still pleasing from the aspect of faith, and the phrase 'those who repent' refers to repenting from polytheism, not from sins, because asking forgiveness for someone who repented from sins is pointless. The third objection about verses on eternal punishment is noted as already addressed. The fourth objection cites the consensus on supplicating 'Make us among the people of Muhammad's intercession.' The response: eligibility for his intercession, even if one is disobedient, is through obedience and faith (i.e., one must still be a believer). The author then reiterates the main point: text and consensus prove intercession, but the Mu'tazila restrict it to the obedient and repentant for raising degrees, while Ash'aris extend it to major sinners for removing evil deeds (hatt al-sayyi'at), either in the plains of judgment or after entering Hellfire. This is supported by the famous hadith 'I have stored my intercession for the major sinners of my community.' The author notes that the Mu'tazila consider punishment after repentance impossible, so intercession would be meaningless for them. He then presents a verse argument: the Quranic statement that intercession does not benefit disbelievers implies that it does benefit others (believers), otherwise specifying disbelievers would not increase their disappointment. Finally, the author discusses the definition of intercession: if it is defined as removing harm only (not increasing benefits), and since minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins, then intercession must be for removing major sins, which directly supports the Ash'ari position. The author notes that the Mu'tazila's counterargument (that intercession as seeking benefits would make us intercessors for the Prophet) is weak because it is based on a misunderstanding of the nature of intercession. The passage is cut off mid-sentence at 'when we ask' (hina nas'al).

Key Terms
الشفاعة
الإسقاط
المضار
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession is about asking for extra rewards for the pious.
Correction: The author argues intercession is for removing harm (punishment), not seeking benefits. Otherwise, we would be intercessors for the Prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be a consequence if intercession were for seeking benefits?
A) We would be intercessors for the Prophet.
B) Intercession would be impossible.
C) Only prophets could intercede.
D) Major sinners would gain paradise.

Key Takeaway
Intercession is defined as removing harm (punishment), not as seeking benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the Prophet's intercession on the Day of Judgment, do you imagine it as a reward for your deeds or as Allah's mercy to cover your failures? How does this lesson's definition of intercession as removing harm change your expectation?
?
The Mu'tazila thought intercession was for seeking benefits. Have you ever acted as if you are entitled to intercession because of your good works? How does the correct definition—intercession as removing harm—humble you and shift your reliance to Allah's mercy alone?
?
This lesson says intercession is specifically for major sinners. Are you willing to admit that you are a major sinner in need of intercession, or do you assume you are safe? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's mercy versus your own righteousness?

Refuting Mu'tazila Objections to Intercession
Author refutes four Mu'tazila objections against intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and respond to the four Mu'tazila objections against intercession for major sinners using the Ash'ari replies.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila objections fail because they ignore the specification of verses and the reality of faith in the sinner.

The Mu'tazila raised several scriptural and rational objections to the idea that intercession can benefit major sinners. In this lesson, we examine each objection and the Ash'ari response, showing how careful tafsir and theological principles resolve the apparent contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the four objections
Go through each of the four Mu'tazila arguments: general verses negating intercession, verses limiting intercession to those approved, verses on eternal punishment, and the consensus on supplication.
2
Study the Ash'ari responses
For each objection, note the specific reply: specification to major sins, interpretation of 'pleased' as referring to faith, and the meaning of the supplication.
3
Synthesize the overall refutation
See how the responses collectively defend intercession for major sinners without contradicting scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث عشر - يجوز عندنا الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر في حقها ، لما سبق من دلائل العفو، وما تواتر معنى من ادخار الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر. وقد يستدل بعموم قوله تعالى : ( واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين ) وبأن أصل الشفاعة ثابت بالنص والإجماع، وليست حقيقة لطلب المنافع على ما يراه المعتزلة، وإلا لكنا شافعين للنبي الحين نسأل الله تعالى زيادة كرامته . بل لإسقاط المضاد. وعندكم لا عقاب مع التوبة، ولا صغيرة مع اجتناب الكبيرة، فتعين كونها لإسقاط الكبائر، تمسكت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - عمومات نفي الشفاعة مثل قوله تعالى : لا يقبل منها شفاعة فما تنفعهم شفاعة الشافعين من قبل أن يأتي يوم لا بيع فيه ولا خلة ولا شفاعة وما للظالمين من حميم ولا شفيع يطاع وما للظالمين من أنصار والجواب - بعد تسليم عموم الأزمان والأحوال التخصيص بالكبائر جمعاً بين الأدلة على أن الظلم المطلق هو الكفر، ونفي الناصر لا ينفي الشفيع . الثاني - آيات تنفي شفاعة صاحب الكبيرة ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضی فاغفر للذين تابوا . والجواب - أن الفاسق مرتضى من جهة الإيمان. والمراد تابوا عن الشرك، لأن من تاب عن المعاصي وعمل صالحاً فطلب مغفرته ،عبث أو طلب لترك الظلام الثالث - آيات خلود الفساق وقد مر الرابع - الإجماع على صحة اللهم اجعلنا من أهل شفاعة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم . والجواب - إن أهلية شفاعته على تقدير العصيان إنما هو بالطاعة والإيمان . في الشفاعة يدل على ثبوتها النص والإجماع . إلا أن المعتزلة قصروها على المطيعين والتائبين لرفع الدرجات وزيادة المثوبات . وعندنا يجوز لأهل الكبائر أيضاً في حط السيئات، إما في العرصات، وإما بعد دخول النار لما سبق من دلائل العفو عن الكبيرة، ولما اشتهر بل تواتر معنى من ادخار الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر، كقوله ( عليه السلام): «ادخرت شفاعتي لأهل الكبائر من أمتي » وترك العقاب بعد التوبة واجب عندهم، فليس للعفو والشفاعة كثير معنى . وقد يستدل بقوله تعالى : واستغفر لذنبك وللمؤمنين . أي لذنوب المؤمنين، فيعم الكبائر وبقوله تعالى في حق الكفار : فما تنفعهم شفاعة الشافعين فإن مثل هذا الكلام إنما يساق حيث تنفع الشفاعة غيرهم ، فيقصد تقبيح حال الكفرة. وتخييب رجائهم، بأنهم ليسوا كذلك إذ لو لم تنفع الشفاعة أحداً لما كان في تخصيصهم زيادة تخييب وتوبيخ لهم. لكنه مع هذا التكلف لا يفيد إلا ثبوت أصل الشفاعة ولا نزاع فيه. نعم لو تم ما ذكره بعض أصحابنا من أن الشفاعة لا يجوز أن تكون حقيقية لزيادة المنافع، بل لإسقاط المضار فقط. والصغائر مكفرة عندكم باجتناب الكبائر، فتعين أن تكون لإسقاط الكبائر، لكان في إثبات أصل الشفاعة إثبات المطلوب إلا أن غاية متشبثهم في ذلك هو أن الشفاعة لو كانت حقيقة في طلب زيادة المنافع لكنا شافعين في حق النبي (عليه السلام) حين نسأل
The thirteenth section - It is permissible in our view intercession for the people of major sins in its [proper] place, due to what preceded of the proofs of pardon, and the recurrent meaning of storing up intercession for the people of major sins. It may be argued by the generality of His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believers' and because the basis of intercession is established by text and consensus, and it is not literally for seeking benefits as the Mu'tazila hold, otherwise we would be intercessors for the Prophet when we ask Allah for increase of his honor. Rather it is for removing harm. And according to you there is no punishment with repentance, and no minor sin with avoidance of major sins, so it is determined that it is for removing major sins. The Mu'tazila clung to several points: First - general verses negating intercession such as His saying: 'There will not be accepted from it any intercession', 'So the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them', 'Before the coming of a day in which there is no trading, no friendship, and no intercession', 'For the wrongdoers there is no friend and no intercessor to be obeyed', 'For the wrongdoers there are no helpers.' The answer - after conceding the generality of times and conditions, specification is made to major sins to combine the evidences, because absolute injustice is disbelief, and negation of a helper does not negate an intercessor. Second - verses negating intercession for the committer of a major sin: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased with', 'So forgive those who repent.' The answer - that the immoral person is pleasing from the aspect of faith, and the meaning is 'they repented from polytheism', because whoever repents from sins and does righteous deeds, then asking for his forgiveness is futile or a request for leaving injustice. Third - verses on eternal punishment of the immoral have passed. Fourth - consensus on the validity of 'O Allah, make us among the people of the intercession of Muhammad.' The answer - that eligibility for his intercession on the condition of disobedience is only through obedience and faith. Regarding intercession, text and consensus indicate its establishment. However, the Mu'tazila restricted it to the obedient and the repentant for raising ranks and increasing rewards. And in our view it is permissible for the people of major sins also for removing evil deeds, either in the plains [of judgment] or after entering the Fire, due to what preceded of the proofs of pardon for the major sin, and due to what is well-known, rather recurrent, of the meaning of storing up intercession for the people of major sins, such as his saying (peace be upon him): 'I have stored up my intercession for the people of major sins of my community.' And leaving punishment after repentance is obligatory according to them, so there is not much meaning to pardon and intercession. It may be argued by His saying: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believers' - i.e., for the sins of the believers, so it includes major sins. And by His saying regarding the disbelievers: 'So the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them' - for such speech is only uttered where intercession benefits others, intending to make the condition of the disbelievers ugly and to disappoint their hope, that they are not like that, because if intercession did not benefit anyone, there would be no increased disappointment and rebuke in specifying them. However, with this elaboration it only proves the establishment of intercession, about which there is no dispute. Yes, if what some of our companions mentioned were complete - that intercession cannot be literal for increasing benefits, but only for removing harms, and minor sins are expiated according to you by avoiding major sins, so it is determined that it is for removing major sins - then in proving the establishment of intercession there would be proof of the desired outcome. However, the utmost of their clinging in that is that if intercession were literal in seeking increase of benefits, we would be intercessors for the Prophet (peace be upon him) when we ask
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the permissibility of intercession (shafa'a) for major sinners (ahl al-kaba'ir) in Ash'ari theology, along with a refutation of the Mu'tazila position. The author begins by stating the Ash'ari view: intercession is permissible for major sinners, based on previous proofs of divine pardon and the tawatur (mass transmission) of the meaning that intercession is stored up for them. He then provides a rational argument: the basis of intercession is established by text and consensus, and intercession is not literally for seeking benefits (as the Mu'tazila claim), because if it were, we would be intercessors for the Prophet when we ask Allah to increase his honor. Rather, intercession is for removing harm (isqat al-madar). The author then points out an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position: according to them, there is no punishment after repentance and no minor sin if one avoids major sins, so intercession would have no function unless it removes major sins. The author then lists four objections raised by the Mu'tazila, each with an answer. The first objection cites general verses negating intercession (e.g., 'no intercession will be accepted', 'the intercession of the intercessors will not benefit them'). The response: while granting the generality of time and conditions, the verses are specified to major sins to reconcile all evidences; moreover, 'absolute injustice' (zulm) is disbelief, and negating a helper does not negate an intercessor. The second objection cites verses that limit intercession to those whom Allah is pleased with (e.g., 'they intercede only for whom He approves'). The response: the immoral person (fasiq) is still pleasing from the aspect of faith, and the phrase 'those who repent' refers to repenting from polytheism, not from sins, because asking forgiveness for someone who repented from sins is pointless. The third objection about verses on eternal punishment is noted as already addressed. The fourth objection cites the consensus on supplicating 'Make us among the people of Muhammad's intercession.' The response: eligibility for his intercession, even if one is disobedient, is through obedience and faith (i.e., one must still be a believer). The author then reiterates the main point: text and consensus prove intercession, but the Mu'tazila restrict it to the obedient and repentant for raising degrees, while Ash'aris extend it to major sinners for removing evil deeds (hatt al-sayyi'at), either in the plains of judgment or after entering Hellfire. This is supported by the famous hadith 'I have stored my intercession for the major sinners of my community.' The author notes that the Mu'tazila consider punishment after repentance impossible, so intercession would be meaningless for them. He then presents a verse argument: the Quranic statement that intercession does not benefit disbelievers implies that it does benefit others (believers), otherwise specifying disbelievers would not increase their disappointment. Finally, the author discusses the definition of intercession: if it is defined as removing harm only (not increasing benefits), and since minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins, then intercession must be for removing major sins, which directly supports the Ash'ari position. The author notes that the Mu'tazila's counterargument (that intercession as seeking benefits would make us intercessors for the Prophet) is weak because it is based on a misunderstanding of the nature of intercession. The passage is cut off mid-sentence at 'when we ask' (hina nas'al).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verses denying intercession apply to all people, including believers.
Correction: The verses are specified to disbelievers (absolute wrongdoing) and do not negate intercession for believers with major sins.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Mu'tazila's use of verses that limit intercession to those 'He is pleased with'?
A) He argues the verses were abrogated.
B) He says the verses refer to disbelievers only.
C) He explains that the fasiq is pleasing from the aspect of faith.
D) He says the verses mean intercession is only for prophets.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objections are answered by specifying verses to disbelievers and interpreting 'pleased' as referring to faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Prophet's intercession can benefit you despite your major sins, or do you harbor despair that your sins are too great? How does this lesson challenge that despair?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that only those who are 'pleasing' receive intercession. The response says even the immoral person is pleasing from the aspect of faith. Do you rely on your obedience or on your faith for hope in intercession? What change must you make today to anchor your hope in your faith rather than your deeds alone?
?
The supplication 'O Allah, make us among the people of Muhammad's intercession' is used by the Mu'tazila to imply only righteous people hope for it. The Ash'ari response says eligibility is through obedience and faith. Are you actively seeking to be among those deserving intercession, or do you neglect your spiritual state? What one concrete act can you do today to increase your eligibility?

Proofs for Intercession for Major Sinners
Author presents hadith and Quranic proofs for intercession for major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key proof texts and logical arguments that establish intercession for major sinners, including the famous hadith and Quranic verses.

Core Idea
The hadith 'I have stored my intercession for the major sinners of my community' is the strongest proof.

Now that we have refuted the Mu'tazila objections, we turn to the positive proofs. The author cites the well-known hadith that intercession is stored up for major sinners, and also uses Quranic verses that imply intercession benefits believers but not disbelievers. We also examine the logical argument that if intercession is for removing harm, it necessarily applies to major sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its significance
The author mentions the famous hadith: 'I have stored my intercession for the people of major sins of my community.' Note its tawatur (mass transmission) and how it directly supports the Ash'ari position.
2
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Examine how verses like 'Ask forgiveness for your sin and for the believers' and the verse about disbelievers not benefiting from intercession imply intercession benefits believers, including sinners.
3
Understand the logical implication
The author builds on the definition from Lesson 1: if intercession is for removing harm, and minor sins are already expiated, then intercession must be for major sins.

Commentary

The third objection about verses on eternal punishment is noted as already addressed. The fourth objection cites the consensus on supplicating 'Make us among the people of Muhammad's intercession.' The response: eligibility for his intercession, even if one is disobedient, is through obedience and faith (i.e., one must still be a believer). The author then reiterates the main point: text and consensus prove intercession, but the Mu'tazila restrict it to the obedient and repentant for raising degrees, while Ash'aris extend it to major sinners for removing evil deeds (hatt al-sayyi'at), either in the plains of judgment or after entering Hellfire. This is supported by the famous hadith 'I have stored my intercession for the major sinners of my community.' The author notes that the Mu'tazila consider punishment after repentance impossible, so intercession would be meaningless for them. He then presents a verse argument: the Quranic statement that intercession does not benefit disbelievers implies that it does benefit others (believers), otherwise specifying disbelievers would not increase their disappointment. Finally, the author discusses the definition of intercession: if it is defined as removing harm only (not increasing benefits), and since minor sins are expiated by avoiding major sins, then intercession must be for removing major sins, which directly supports the Ash'ari position. The author notes that the Mu'tazila's counterargument (that intercession as seeking benefits would make us intercessors for the Prophet) is weak because it is based on a misunderstanding of the nature of intercession. The passage is cut off mid-sentence at 'when we ask' (hina nas'al).

Key Terms
ادخار الشفاعة
العرصات
الكبائر
استغفر
تخييب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession only benefits the righteous, not sinners.
Correction: The hadith and Quranic proofs show intercession is specifically for major sinners.

Checkpoint
Which hadith does the author cite as evidence that intercession is for major sinners?

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'I have stored my intercession for the major sinners' is the central proof for Ash'ari intercession doctrine.

?

Objection about an additional condition in intercession and its refutation
Objection and answer regarding the permissibility of an additional condition in intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that intercession may require an additional condition and how it is refuted by considering the intercessor's own intercession and lack of obedience.

Core Idea
Intercession does not require the intercessor to be superior or the benefits to depend solely on his request.

In this passage, a hypothetical objection is raised about intercession: could it be that intercession requires an additional condition, such as the intercessor being in a higher state or the benefit being obtained only through his request? The response refutes this by pointing out two possibilities that break the condition. Let us read the original text and its explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the proposal that intercession might involve an extra condition. The objector suggests it is permissible to consider an additional condition.
2
Identify the two examples
The objection gives two examples: (a) the intercessor being in a higher state than the one for whom intercession is made, or (b) the increase of benefits being obtained only through the intercessor's request.
3
Read the refutation
The refutation provides two counterexamples: (1) the intercessor may intercede for himself, so he is not higher; (2) the intercessor may not be obeyed, so the requested thing does not occur.
Notice that both counterexamples show the condition is not necessary.
4
Reflect on the logical force
The refutation demonstrates that the additional condition is not universally valid. Intercession can occur without the intercessor being superior or the benefit being solely due to his request.
This reinforces the original understanding of intercession.

Commentary

The objector suggests that intercession could involve an additional condition, such as the intercessor being superior to the person for whom he intercedes, or that the benefits are only obtained through the intercessor's request. The response refutes this by pointing out two possibilities: first, the intercessor may intercede for himself, in which case he is not higher; second, the intercessor may not be obeyed, so the requested benefit does not occur, let alone be granted due to his request. Objection and response on the literal meaning of shafāʿah
Objection and answer regarding the literal usage of 'shafāʿah' for requesting benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic objection to the technical definition of shafāʿah and the rational response maintaining its restricted literal meaning.

Core Idea
Literal intercession requires consistency; general benefit requests prove nothing.

The precise meaning of shafāʿah is debated. Some argue it is literally used for any request of benefit, citing poetry and historical documents. How does the scholar respond? He concedes the usage but argues that if it were literal, it would apply consistently across all contexts, which it does not. Thus the technical meaning remains restricted to intercession by a person of status.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the objector's evidence and the response's logic emerge.
2
Identify the two examples given by the objector
Find the poetic line and the historical decree. Notice that both use 'shafāʿah' for a simple request without a mediating person.
The poet says 'you do not come to him with an intercessor' meaning you come directly.
3
Understand the response's logic
The author says 'Yes' (نعم) to the existence of such usage, but then invokes a linguistic principle: if a word is literal (ḥaqīqah), it must apply consistently in all contexts where the meaning is intended. Since the technical contexts (Prophet's intercession) do not use 'shafāʿah' broadly, the literal meaning is restricted.
The response does not deny the examples; it reinterprets them as non-literal.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument protects the technical definition of intercession without rejecting common usage. It shows the importance of consistency in determining literal meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إطلاق الشفاعة على طلب المنافع مما لا سبيل إلى إنكاره كقول الشاعر: فذاك فتى إن تأته في صنيعه إلى ما له لم تأته بشفيع وكما في منشور دار الخلافة لسلطان محمود : وَلَيْنَاكَ كورة خراسان الدولة، وأمين الملة بشفاعة أبي حامد الإسفرائني . قلنا نعم، لكن لو كان حقيقة لاطرد فيما ذكرنا .
For if it is said: The application of the word 'shafāʿah' to the request of benefits is undeniable, as the poet says: 'That is a young man; if you come to him regarding his deed for what he has, you do not come to him with an intercessor,' and as in the decree of the caliphal court for Sultan Mahmud: 'We have appointed you over the province of Khurasan for the state and the trustee of the religion by the intercession of Abu Hamid al-Isfarā'īnī.' We say: Yes, but if it were the literal reality, it would apply consistently in what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response concerning the literal meaning of the term 'shafāʿah' (intercession). The objector argues that the word is literally used for simply requesting benefits, citing a poetic example and a historical decree. The response concedes that such usage exists, but counters that if 'shafāʿah' were literally (ḥaqīqah) applicable to any request, it would apply consistently in the specific cases mentioned earlier (presumably referring to the intercession of the Prophet for major sinners). Since it does not apply consistently, the literal meaning is more restricted. This is a linguistic argument about the technical definition of intercession.

Key Terms
شفاعة (shafāʿah)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqah)
إطلاق (iṭlāq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The literal meaning of intercession requires consistency and is restricted to mediation by a person of status; not every benefit request is intercession.

C) He says it is only metaphorical.
D) He says it is only used for prophets.

Key Takeaway
The literal meaning of shafāʿah is restricted to intercession by a person of status; general requests for benefits are not literal usage.

The Mu'tazila's Four Proofs Against Intercession for Major Sinners
Enumeration of four rational and scriptural proofs by the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four proofs that the Mu'tazila use to argue against the reality of intercession (shafāʿah) for major sinners, based on Qur'anic verses and consensus.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that intercession cannot benefit major sinners, citing clear Qur'anic texts and consensus.

The Mu'tazila, a rationalist theological school, denied that intercession (shafāʿah) could benefit those who commit major sins. They supported their position with four main proofs: Qur'anic verses that appear to negate intercession entirely, verses that restrict intercession to those God approves, verses threatening eternal punishment, and a consensus-based argument from the common prayer of Muslims. This lesson presents these four proofs as articulated by the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice that it lists four distinct proofs, each introduced by a number.
The passage is an enumeration; the author is presenting the Mu'tazila's arguments before refuting them.
2
Identify the four proofs
The passage clearly marks each proof with 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc. List them in your own words. The first uses verses that seem to deny intercession absolutely. The second uses verses that condition intercession on God's approval. The third uses verses on eternal punishment. The fourth uses a consensus-based prayer.
3
Analyze the scriptural evidence for each
For each proof, note the specific Qur'anic verses cited and how the Mu'tazila interpret them. For example, in the first proof, verses like 'no soul will suffice for another' are taken as general negations of intercession.
The Mu'tazila use these verses as rational evidence, not just proof texts.
4
Recognize the structure of the argument
The Mu'tazila combine rational and scriptural proofs. Notice how they use consensus and logical consistency (e.g., intercession would contradict eternal punishment). This sets up the author's subsequent refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - الآيات الدالة على نفي الشفاعة بالكلية، فيخص المطيع والتائب بالإجماع، فتبقى حجة فيما وراء ذلك، مثل قوله تعالى : واتقوا يوماً لا تجزي نفس عن نفس شيئاً ... الآية . والضمير في : لا يقبل منها شفاعة ) ولا تنفعها شفاعة ) للنفس المبهمة العامة . وكقوله تعالى : ومن قبل أن يأتي يوم لا بيع فيه ولا خلة ولا شفاعة . وكقوله تعالى : ما للظالمين من حميم ولا شفيع يطاع أي يجاب. يعني لا شفاعة أصلاً على طريقة قوله : ولا ترى الضب بها ينحجر. وكقوله تعالى : وما للظالمين من أنصار . الثاني ـ ما يشعر بنفي الشفاعة لصاحب الكبيرة منطوقاً كقوله تعالى : ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى . فإنه ليس بمرتضى . أو مفهوماً كقوله تعالى حكاية عن حملة العرش : ويستغفرون للذين آمنوا ، فاغفر للذين تابوا واتبعوا سبيلك . ولا فارق بين شفاعة الملائكة والأنبياء . الثالث - ما سبق من الآيات المشعرة بخلود الفساق. ولو كانت شفاعة لما كان خلود . الرابع - الإجماع على الدعاء بقولنا : اللهم اجعلنا من أهل شفاعة محمد . ولو خصت الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر، لكان ذلك دعاء بجعله منهم .
The Mu'tazila produced proofs: First - verses indicating the negation of intercession entirely, but they specify the obedient and the repentant by consensus, so the argument remains for what is beyond that, such as His saying: 'And fear a Day when no soul will suffice for another soul at all...' the verse. And the pronoun in 'No intercession will be accepted from it' and 'No intercession will benefit it' refers to the indefinite general soul. And like His saying: 'And before the coming of a Day in which there is no selling, no friendship, and no intercession.' And like His saying: 'For the wrongdoers there is no intimate friend nor intercessor to be obeyed' i.e., answered. Meaning no intercession at all, in the manner of His saying: 'And you will not see the lizard [?] in its hole.' And like His saying: 'And for the wrongdoers there are no helpers.' Second - what indicates the negation of intercession for the committer of a major sin, explicitly like His saying: 'And they do not intercede except for one whom He approves' - for he is not approved. Or implicitly like His saying, reporting from the bearers of the Throne: 'And they ask forgiveness for those who believe... so forgive those who repent and follow Your path.' And there is no distinction between the intercession of angels and prophets. Third - what has preceded of verses indicating the eternal punishment of transgressors. If there were intercession, there would be no eternity. Fourth - consensus on the prayer saying: 'O God, make us among the people of Muhammad's intercession.' If intercession were specific to the people of major sins, that would be a prayer to be made among them.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four proofs that the Mu'tazila use to argue against the reality of intercession (shafāʿah) for major sinners. The first proof cites verses that absolutely negate intercession, such as 'no soul will suffice for another' and 'no intercession will be accepted,' arguing that these are general and apply to all except the obedient and repentant by consensus. The second proof uses verses that restrict intercession to those whom God approves, implying the major sinner is not approved, and verses that mention forgiveness only for those who believe and repent. The third proof relies on verses indicating eternal punishment for sinners, which would be incompatible with intercession. The fourth proof is based on the consensus of Muslims praying to be included among those who receive the Prophet's intercession, which would be inappropriate if intercession were only for sinners. These are the main arguments the author will refute in subsequent passages.

D) The verse 'They do not intercede except for one whom He approves'.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use four proofs—two scriptural, one from punishment verses, and one from consensus—to argue that major sinners cannot benefit from intercession.

Refuting General Negation of Intercession
Refutation of the argument that verses negating intercession preclude it for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author restricts the negation of intercession to disbelievers and distinguishes it from the negation of help.

Core Idea
Global negation doesn't imply absolute negation; intercession remains for believers.

The Mu'tazilis argued that verses like 'There is no intercessor for the wrongdoers' prove intercession is impossible for any sinner. The author responds by first conceding the verses' generality but then showing they refer specifically to disbelievers. He also clarifies that negating 'help' is not the same as negating intercession, and that the logical scope matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. The author is responding to an opponent's proof.
2
Identify the two key distinctions
Notice two distinctions: (1) the verses are about disbelievers only; (2) negating help does not negate intercession because they are different actions.
3
Understand the logical distinction
The author mentions two logical scopes: 'generality of negation' vs 'negation of generality'. Relate this to the intercession debate.
This distinction saves the possibility of intercession for believers.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Ask how this refutation fits into the Sunni position on intercession: it affirms intercession for believers while denying it for disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول - بعد تسليم العموم في الأزمان والأحوال أنها تختص بالكفار جمعاً بین الأدلة على أن الظالم على الإطلاق هو الكافر، وأن نفي النصرة لا يستلزم نفي الشفاعة لأنها طلب على خضوع والنصرة ربما تنبىء مدافعة ومغالبة. هذا بعد تسليم كون الكلام لعموم السلب لا لسلب العموم . وقد سبق مثل ذلك .
And the answer to the first [proof] - after conceding generality in times and circumstances, it is that [the verses] are specific to disbelievers, combining the evidences that the absolute wrongdoer (ẓālim) is the disbeliever, and that the negation of help (nuṣrah) does not entail the negation of intercession because it is a request with humility, while help perhaps implies defense and overcoming. This after conceding that the speech is for the generality of negation (ʿumūm al-salb), not the negation of generality (salb al-ʿumūm). And similar has preceded.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the first Mu'tazili proof, which cited verses that seem to negate intercession absolutely. The author first concedes the general application of these verses, but then specifies that they refer exclusively to disbelievers (kuffār), not to sinful believers. He combines evidence that the term 'wrongdoer' (ẓālim) in these verses means the disbeliever. Moreover, he distinguishes between the negation of help (nuṣrah) and the negation of intercession: intercession is a humble request, whereas help involves defense and confrontation. Thus, verses negating help do not automatically negate intercession. Finally, he clarifies the logical scope: the verses are for the 'generality of negation' (i.e., no individual of a certain category has intercession), not the 'negation of generality' (i.e., it is not that all individuals lack intercession). This distinction allows for the possibility of intercession for some believers.

Key Terms
عموم السلب
سلب العموم
نصرة
ظالم
كافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating help in the Quran is the same as negating intercession.
Correction: Help (nuṣrah) implies defense and struggle, while intercession is a humble request; negating one does not negate the other.

Checkpoint
How does the author specify the verses that negate intercession?
A) He says they apply to all sinners.
B) He says they apply only to disbelievers.
C) He says they are abrogated.
D) He says they are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The verses negating intercession are specific to disbelievers, and the negation of help does not imply the negation of intercession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author distinguishes between help and intercession: help implies defense, intercession implies humility. In your relationship with Allah, do you more often approach Him demanding your 'rights' (like help) or begging with humility (like intercession)? Which one dominates your dua, and what will you change starting today?

Response to the Second Mu'tazili Proof: The Sinner's Approval
Refutation of the argument that the major sinner is not approved for intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the major sinner is not approved for intercession by distinguishing between approval in faith and dislike due to sin.

Core Idea
The sinner is approved in faith and deeds, though disliked for sin.

The Mu'tazila argued that the major sinner cannot receive intercession because God only approves of those whom He intercedes for. In this passage, the author denies that premise: the sinner (fāsiq) is indeed approved from the perspective of faith and righteous deeds, even if disliked for disobedience. This sets up a key distinction that underpins the Ash'arī position on intercession.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the author's argument.
Note that the text is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the author's move
The author denies the Mu'tazili premise: we do not concede that 'one whom He approves' excludes the sinner.
3
Examine the distinction
The author distinguishes between approval in terms of faith and righteous deeds versus dislike due to sin. The sinner is approved in one aspect, disliked in another.
This is a nuanced view compared to the disbeliever who is not approved at all.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The text ends before completing the response. Consider what might follow: likely an explanation of how the sinner is included among 'those who believe' in the relevant verse.
This missing part is why the source is marked for review.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني - بأنا لا نسلم أن من ارتضى لا يتناول الفاسق، فإنه مرتضى من جهة الإيمان والعمل الصالح، وإن كان مبغوضاً من جهة المعصية، بخلاف الكافر المتصف بمثل ،العدل أو الجور، فإنه ليس بمرتضى عند الله تعالى أصلاً لفوات أصل الحسنات وأساس الكمالات. ولا نسلم أن الذين ...

Unlike the disbeliever who is described with such as justice or injustice, for he is not approved at all before God due to the loss of the foundation of good deeds and the basis of perfections. And we do not concede that those who...

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the second Mu'tazili proof, which argued that intercession is only for those whom God approves, and the major sinner is not approved. The author denies this premise: the sinner (fāsiq) is indeed approved (murtadā) in terms of his faith (īmān) and righteous deeds, even though he is disliked (mabghūḍ) due to his sin. This distinguishes him from the disbeliever, who lacks any foundation of good deeds and is not approved in any way. The argument begins to address the second part of the proof regarding the verse about the angels asking forgiveness only for those who believe and repent, but the text is cut off. The author likely goes on to explain that the major sinner is included among 'those who believe' and therefore can receive intercession.

Misconception: The major sinner is completely disapproved by God and cannot receive intercession.
Correction: The author shows that the sinner is approved in terms of faith and good deeds, though disliked for sin, allowing for intercession.

Checkpoint

A) He is not approved at all.
B) He is approved in terms of faith and good deeds, though disliked for sin.
C) He is approved only if he repents.

Key Takeaway
The major sinner is approved by God in terms of faith and good deeds, enabling intercession despite sin.

First Objection and Answer on Intercession for Major Sinners
First objection using verse on no helpers for wrongdoers and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a Quranic verse denying help to wrongdoers disproves intercession is answered by distinguishing between the referent of 'wrongdoer' and the nature of help versus intercession.

Core Idea
A verse denying help to wrongdoers does not deny intercession; help and intercession are different categories.

This lesson examines the first objection against intercession for major sinners: a Quranic verse that says wrongdoers have no helpers. The author provides a nuanced answer that clarifies that 'wrongdoer' refers specifically to disbelievers, not sinning believers, and that the type of support denied (help) is different from intercession. By the end of this lesson, you will see how this objection is resolved through careful textual and logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to get the gist.
2
Identify the objection and the verse
Notice the verse cited and how it forms the objection against intercession. Ask yourself: why is this verse a problem?
The verse is Q. 5:72.
3
Follow the two-part answer
The answer makes two main points: (1) 'wrongdoer' means disbeliever; (2) help and intercession are different. Trace how the author argues each.
The logical distinction between 'generality of negation' and 'negation of generality' is a third subtle point.
4
Reflect on the logical distinction
Understand why the distinction between 'عموم السلب' and 'سلب العموم' matters. This is a key logical tool in kalām.
Refer back to the earlier discussion mentioned in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينحجر. وكقوله تعالى : وما للظالمين من أنصار (۱) . والجواب عن الأول - بعد تسليم العموم في الأزمان والأحوال أنها تختص بالكفار جمعاً بین الأدلة على أن الظالم على الإطلاق هو الكافر، وأن نفي النصرة لا يستلزم نفي الشفاعة لأنها طلب على خضوع والنصرة ربما تنبىء مدافعة ومغالبة. هذا بعد تسليم كون الكلام لعموم السلب لا لسلب العموم . وقد سبق مثل ذلك (۷) .
It breaks. And like His saying the Most High: 'And for the wrongdoers there are no helpers' (1). And the answer concerning the first - after conceding generality in times and conditions, that it is specific to disbelievers, gathering the evidences that the wrongdoer absolutely is the disbeliever, and that the negation of help does not necessitate the negation of intercession because it (intercession) is a request in humility, while help perhaps implies confrontation and overcoming. This after conceding that the statement is for the generality of negation, not the negation of generality. And similar has preceded (7).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the first objection-and-answer unit in a series of four objections against the possibility of intercession for major sinners. The objection cites the Quranic verse 'And for the wrongdoers there are no helpers' (Q. 5:72) to argue that wrongdoers (i.e., major sinners) cannot receive any aid, including intercession. The answer begins by conceding the verse's generality but specifies that 'wrongdoer' absolutely means disbeliever (kafir), not a sinning believer. Then it argues that the negation of 'help' (nusra) does not negate intercession (shafa'a), because nusra implies confrontation and overcoming, whereas shafa'a is a humble request. The answer also notes that the verse is understood as 'the generality of negation' (all individuals negated) rather than 'the negation of generality' (not all individuals have help), a logical distinction already discussed earlier. The author thus preserves the possibility of intercession for believers who commit major sins.

Key Terms
ظالم
نصرة
شفاعة
عموم السلب
سلب العموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And for the wrongdoers there are no helpers' proves that intercession is impossible for any wrongdoer, including sinning believers.
Correction: The verse refers to disbelievers, and 'help' (nusra) is different from intercession (shafa'a), which is a humble request rather than forceful aid.

Checkpoint
How does the answer distinguish between the negation of help (nusra) and intercession (shafa'a)?
A) By saying intercession is a request in humility while help implies confrontation.
B) By saying the verse only applies to disbelievers, not believers.
C) By saying intercession is only for believers, not disbelievers.
D) By saying the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The objection from the verse on wrongdoers is resolved by specifying that the verse refers to disbelievers and by distinguishing between help (nusra) and intercession (shafa'a) as different types of support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that appears to contradict a belief you hold, do you hastily discard the belief or do you, as this lesson shows, engage in precise linguistic analysis and gather all evidences? This lesson reveals that even subtle distinctions between terms like 'help' and 'intercession' can protect your Aqeedah. Will you now take the time to study such distinctions before jumping to conclusions?
?
The objector used a single verse to argue against intercession for major sinners. Have you ever used a partial proof—a verse or hadith taken out of context—to support a position without considering other texts? This lesson demonstrates the need to 'gather the evidences' as the scholars do. Commit today to seeking a comprehensive understanding of any Islamic issue before forming an opinion.
?
This lesson highlights the logical distinction between 'generality of negation' and 'negation of generality'—a nuance many overlook. In your daily reading of the Quran and Hadith, do you pay attention to such logical forms, or do you take statements at face value? Set a goal this week to scrutinize at least one verse for its precise logical structure, applying what you have learned here.

The Second Objection: Quranic Proofs Against Intercession for Major Sinners
Objection: major sinners excluded from intercession by explicit and implicit Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses explicit and implicit Quranic meanings to argue against intercession for major sinners.

Core Idea
The objector claims the Quran denies intercession for all but the approved — excluding major sinners.

This lesson presents the second objection against intercession for major sinners. The objector cites two Quranic arguments: an explicit verse stating intercession is only for whom Allah approves, and an implicit inference from the bearers of the Throne's prayer for believers who repent. The objector argues that major sinners are neither approved nor included in those who repent from shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage describing the objection. Notice it begins with 'الثاني - ما يشعر بنفي الشفاعة'.
The passage is in two parts: explicit and implicit proofs.
2
Identify the explicit proof
The objector cites the verse 'ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى' (21:28) and argues that the major sinner is not 'approved' (murtada).
This is a direct textual argument.
3
Identify the implicit proof
The objector then refers to the verse about the bearers of the Throne asking forgiveness for 'those who believe' and 'those who repent and follow Your way' (implied from 40:7-9). The objector argues that this excludes major sinners who have not repented from shirk.
The objector claims no difference between intercession of angels and prophets.
4
Summarize the objection
The objection combines both proofs to conclude that intercession is not for major sinners. Understand the logic before moving to the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - ما يشعر بنفي الشفاعة لصاحب الكبيرة منطوقاً كقوله تعالى : ﴿ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى) (۲) . فإنه ليس بمرتضى . أو مفهوماً كقوله تعالى حكاية عن حملة العرش : (ويستغفرون للذين آمنوا (۳) ، فاغفر للذين تابوا واتبعوا سبيلك ) (٤) . ولا فارق بين شفاعة الملائكة والأنبياء . وعن الثاني - بأنا لا نسلم أن من ارتضى لا يتناول الفاسق، فإنه مرتضى من جهة الإيمان والعمل الصالح، وإن كان مبغوضاً من جهة المعصية، بخلاف الكافر المتصف بمثل ،العدل أو الجور، فإنه ليس بمرتضى عند الله تعالى أصلاً لفوات أصل الحسنات وأساس الكمالات. ولا نسلم أن الذين تابوا لا يتناول الفاسق فإن المراد تابوا عن الشرك، إذ لا معنى لطلب مغفرة من تاب عن المعاصي وعمل صالحاً(۱) عندكم لكونه عبثاً أو طلباً لترك الظلم بمنع المستحق حقه. هذا بعد تسليم دلالة التخصيص بالوصف على نفي الحكم عما عداه .
Second: What indicates the negation of intercession for the possessor of a major sin explicitly, like His saying the Most High: 'And they will not intercede except for whom He approves' (2). For he (the sinner) is not approved. Or implicitly, like His saying the Most High, relating from the bearers of the Throne: 'And they ask forgiveness for those who believe' (3), 'so forgive those who repent and follow Your way' (4). And there is no difference between the intercession of angels and prophets. And the answer concerning the second: We do not concede that 'whom He approves' does not include the immoral believer (fasiq), for he is approved with regard to faith and righteous deeds, even if he is hated with regard to sin, unlike the disbeliever who is described with justice or injustice, for he is not approved by Allah the Most High at all due to the loss of the foundation of good deeds and the basis of perfections. And we do not concede that 'those who repent' does not include the immoral believer, for what is meant is repented from shirk (polytheism), since there is no meaning to seeking forgiveness for one who repents from sins and does righteous deeds, according to you, because it would be futile or a request to leave injustice by preventing the deserving party from his right. This after conceding the implication of specification by description for the negation of the ruling from what is other than it.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is the second objection-and-answer unit. The objection uses two Quranic proofs: the explicit meaning of 'And they will not intercede except for whom He approves' (Q. 21:28), arguing that a major sinner is not approved; and the implicit meaning of the verse about the bearers of the Throne asking forgiveness only for those who believe and repent, implying that those who repent from shirk, not from minor sins, are included. The objector claims there is no difference between intercession of angels and prophets. The answer denies both premises: First, 'whom He approves' includes the sinful believer because he is approved with respect to his faith and good deeds, even if his sin is hated; the disbeliever, by contrast, is not approved at all because he lacks the root of good deeds. Second, 'those who repent' in the verse refers to repentance from shirk, not from sins, because asking forgiveness for someone who repented from a sin and did good deeds would be futile (since forgiveness already obtained) or would be a request to leave injustice by preventing the deserving party from his right. The answer also concedes the logical principle that specification by a description (e.g., 'those who repent from shirk') does not negate the ruling from others (i.e., it does not prove that sinners cannot receive intercession).

Key Terms
فاسق
ارتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two types of Quranic proofs the objector uses?
A) Explicit and implicit
B) Literal and figurative
C) Abrogated and confirmed
D) General and specific

Key Takeaway
The objection relies on two Quranic arguments to deny intercession for major sinners, but the meanings of 'approval' and 'repentance' are contested.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the objector's reading of these verses is correct, where would you stand? Have you committed a major sin and still hope for intercession without true repentance? What must you change today to be among those who repent and follow His way?
?
The objector claims that intercession is only for those whom Allah approves. Are you certain that you are among the approved? What evidence from your life would you present to show you are not persisting in major sin?
?
If the angels ask forgiveness only for those who repent and follow the path, does your daily life reflect that you are among them? What one step can you take today to align your actions with that condition?

The Answer: Major Sinners Are Approved in Faith and Repentance Means Leaving Shirk
Answer: defending inclusion of major sinners in 'murtada' and 'taa'ib'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the answer rebuts the objection by distinguishing between approval in faith and hatred of sin, and by interpreting repentance as repentance from shirk.

Core Idea
The fasiq is approved in faith and righteous deeds, and 'those who repent' refers to leaving shirk, not sins.

This lesson presents the answer to the second objection. The answer denies the premise that 'whom He approves' excludes the fasiq, arguing that the fasiq is approved in faith and good deeds even if his sin is hated. It also reinterprets 'those who repent' as meaning repentance from shirk, not from minor sins, otherwise asking forgiveness would be futile. Finally, it concedes the logical principle of specification by description but denies it applies here.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic answer starting from 'وعن الثاني - بأنا لا نسلم'. Note the two main denials.
2
Analyze the first denial
The answer denies that 'whom He approves' excludes the fasiq. It explains that the fasiq is approved in terms of faith and righteous deeds, even though his sin is hated (مبغوض). The disbeliever is not approved at all because he lacks the foundation of good deeds.
This distinguishes between partial approval (for faith) and total disapproval (for shirk).
3
Analyze the second denial
The answer denies that 'those who repent' excludes the fasiq. It argues that 'repent' here means repentance from shirk, not from sins. If it meant repentance from sins, asking forgiveness for such a person would be futile or unjust.
The logic: if one repents from a sin and does good deeds, forgiveness is already obtained, so asking for it again is meaningless.
4
Consider the logical concession
The answer concedes the principle that specification by description implies negation of the ruling for what is other than the described. However, it argues that this principle does not apply here because the descriptions ('whom He approves' and 'those who repent') do not exclude the fasiq as argued.
This shows careful engagement with the objector's logical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The objection uses two Quranic proofs: the explicit meaning of 'And they will not intercede except for whom He approves' (Q. 21:28), arguing that a major sinner is not approved; and the implicit meaning of the verse about the bearers of the Throne asking forgiveness only for those who believe and repent, implying that those who repent from shirk, not from minor sins, are included.
