The Third Objection on Intercession for Major Sinners
Third objection against intercession and answer by referencing earlier discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to the objection that verses threatening eternal punishment contradict intercession for major sinners.

Core Idea
The eternity objection is answered by the prior proof that punishment for major sinners ceases.

This lesson presents the third objection against intercession for major sinners: if intercession were possible, why does the Qur'an threaten eternal punishment? The author's answer is brief and points to a previously established principle that the punishment of a major believer is not everlasting. Understanding this response requires recalling the earlier discussion on the cessation of punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic source passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is framed: it cites verses indicating eternity and argues that intercession would negate that eternity.
The objection uses the term 'khulud' (eternity) and 'fussaq' (immoral believers).
2
Identify the answer's basis
The answer simply refers to 'what has preceded in the issue of the cessation of punishment of the possessor of a major sin.' Understand that the author is relying on an earlier doctrinal resolution.
This is a typical method in scholastic texts: cross-reference to established positions.
3
Synthesize the argument
Explain in your own words how the answer resolves the objection. The key is that the punishment for major sinners is not eternal, so intercession does not conflict with the verses.
If the punishment is not everlasting, intercession can still occur without nullifying the threat of temporary punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - ما سبق (٥) من الآيات المشعرة بخلود الفساق. ولو كانت شفاعة لما كان خلود . وعن الثالث - بما سبق في مسألة انقطاع (۲) عذاب صاحب الكبيرة .
Third: What has preceded (5) of the verses indicating the eternity of the immoral believers. And if there were intercession, there would be no eternity. And the answer concerning the third: By what has preceded in the issue of the cessation of punishment of the possessor of a major sin (2).
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is the third objection-and-answer unit. The objection references earlier verses that suggest the eternal punishment of major sinners (fussāq), arguing that if intercession existed, eternity would not occur (i.e., intercession would remove the punishment). The answer is very brief, simply referring back to the earlier discussion on the cessation of punishment for the major sinner (possibly referring to the discussion on whether punishment ends or not). The author relies on a previously established argument that the punishment of a major sinner is not eternal, so intercession can still be effective. This answer assumes the reader is familiar with the earlier position on the non-eternity of punishment for sinful believers.

Key Terms
خلود
فساق
انقطاع عذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verses threatening eternal punishment prove that major sinners will never be saved, making intercession impossible.
Correction: The author argues that the punishment for major sinners is not literally eternal; it can cease, so intercession does not contradict the threat of 'eternity' which is understood as a very long period or temporary.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the third objection?
A) By providing a new verse.
B) By referring to a previous discussion on the cessation of punishment.
C) By denying that intercession exists.
D) By saying the verses are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The objection from eternity verses is answered by the earlier teaching that punishment for major sinners is not everlasting, so intercession is compatible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your repeated major sins make you unworthy of forgiveness because you think of them as eternally damning? How does this lesson's grounding in the cessation of punishment for believers change your view of God's mercy? What one step will you take this week to internalize that hope rather than despair?
?
The objection reads eternity verses literally, but the answer refers to a deeper principle. Is there a verse or teaching you have taken at face value that has actually limited your understanding of God's mercy? How can you seek out the foundational truths that put such verses in proper context?
?
This passage shows that theological debate often hinges on connecting previous insights. In your own faith journey, is there a foundational truth about God's justice and mercy that you have neglected? How will you rebuild your trust in that truth starting today?

The Supplication for Intercession: Objection and Answer
Fourth objection using supplication to be among those who receive intercession, and its detailed answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the common supplication to be among the people of intercession does not prove that intercession is for all believers, but rather it is a request for the faith and good deeds that lead to intercession.

Core Idea
Asking for intercession is asking for the cause, not the sin.

This lesson presents a fourth objection against the view that intercession is only for major sinners. The objection is based on the Muslim practice of supplicating to be among those who receive intercession. The answer provides a nuanced interpretation using an analogy from medicine, showing that the supplication is for the attributes that lead to intercession, not for the sins themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objection: the consensus on supplication 'O Allah, make us among the people of intercession' is used as evidence that intercession cannot be only for major sinners, because that would mean asking to be among them.
Notice that the objection relies on a literal reading of the supplication.
2
Understand the answer's key move
The answer reinterprets the supplication as 'make us among the people of intercession on the assumption of sins'—similar to saying 'make us among the people of forgiveness' without asking to sin.
This reframes the request as conditional, not a desire for sin.
3
Study the medical analogy
Analyze the analogy: treatment is for the sick, but asking to be among the people of treatment is a request for strength of constitution, not sickness. Apply this to intercession: asking for intercession is asking for faith and good deeds, the origin of intercession, not the sins.
The analogy clarifies that the supplication targets the cause, not the occasion.
4
See the resolution of related scenarios
Note that the answer also resolves the example of a person who swears by divorce to do something that makes him eligible for intercession: he is commanded to perform acts of obedience, not sins, confirming that the supplication requests good deeds.
This shows consistency with Islamic legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - الإجماع على الدعاء بقولنا : اللهم اجعلنا من أهل شفاعة محمد . ولو خصت (٦) الشفاعة لأهل الكبائر، لكان ذلك دعاء بجعله منهم . وعن الرابع - أن المراد اجعلنا من أهل الشفاعة على تقدير المعاصي كما في قولنا اجعلنا من أهل المغفرة وأهل التوبة. وتحقيقه أن المتصف بالصفات إذا اختص بكرامة منشأها بعض تلك الصفات دون البعض، لم يكن استدعاء أهلية تلك الكرامة الا استدعاء الصفة التي هي منشأ تلك الكرامة . ألا يرى أن المعالجة وإن لم تكن إلا للمريض لكن قولك: اللهم اجعلني من أهل العلاج ليس طلباً للمرض، بل لقوة المزاج. فكذا ههنا الشفاعة، وإن اختصت بأهل الكبائر، لكن منشأها الإيمان وبعض الحسنات التي تصير سبباً لرضى الشفيع عنه، وميله إليه . وبهذا يخرج الجواب عما قالوا أن من حلف بالطلاق أن يعمل ما يجعله أهلاً للشفاعة أنه يؤمر بالطاعات، لا المعاصي .
Fourth: The consensus on the supplication by our saying: 'O Allah, make us among the people of the intercession of Muhammad.' And if intercession were specific to the people of major sins, that would be a supplication to be made one of them. And the answer concerning the fourth: That what is meant is 'make us among the people of intercession' on the assumption of sins, as in our saying 'make us among the people of forgiveness and the people of repentance.' Its verification: That the one qualified with attributes, if he is singled out for a dignity whose origin is some of those attributes without others, then requesting eligibility for that dignity is nothing but requesting the attribute which is the origin of that dignity. Do you not see that treatment, even if it is only for the sick, yet your saying 'O Allah, make me among the people of treatment' is not a request for sickness, but for strength of constitution. So likewise here intercession, even if it is specific to the people of major sins, yet its origin is faith and some good deeds that become a cause for the satisfaction of the intercessor with him, and his inclination towards him. And by this emerges the answer to what they said: that if someone swore by divorce to do something that makes him eligible for intercession, he is commanded to do acts of obedience, not sins.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection-and-answer unit. The objection is based on the established practice of Muslims supplicating 'O Allah, make us among the people of the intercession of Muhammad.' If intercession were only for major sinners, then this supplication would be asking to be among major sinners. The answer provides a nuanced interpretation: the supplication means 'make us among the people of intercession on the condition that we commit sins' (i.e., even if we sin), similar to saying 'make us among the people of forgiveness' — which does not mean asking to sin, but to be forgiven when sinning. The author then gives a detailed analogy: just as a sick person who asks 'make me among the people of treatment' is not asking for sickness but for a strong constitution to be treated, so too asking for intercession is asking for the attributes that are the basis of intercession — namely, faith and good deeds — not for the sins that occasion it. The answer further notes that this resolves a related issue: if someone swears by divorce to do something that makes him eligible for intercession, he is commanded to do acts of obedience, not sins. Thus, the supplication is consistent with intercession being for major sinners, as it asks for the faith and good deeds that are the cause of intercession, not for the sins themselves.

Key Terms

دعاء
أهل شفاعة
منشأ
سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The supplication to be among the people of intercession proves that intercession is not only for major sinners.
Correction: The supplication is reinterpreted as a request for the faith and good deeds that are the origin of intercession, not for the sins that occasion it. It is conditional on the assumption of sins, similar to asking for forgiveness.

Checkpoint

B) The analogy of a student asking to be among the people of knowledge.

D) The analogy of a traveler asking to be among the people of the road.

Key Takeaway
The supplication to be among the people of intercession is not a request for sin but for faith and good deeds, which are the origin of intercession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you supplicate 'O Allah, make us among the people of intercession,' what are you actually asking for? Have you focused on acquiring the causes (faith and good deeds) or merely hoping for the result without effort?
?
The passage compares requesting intercession to requesting treatment. In your spiritual life, are you seeking the 'treatment' of faith and deeds, or are you expecting intercession without addressing the root causes in your soul?
?
How does this understanding of intercession change your attitude towards your own sins and your reliance on the Prophet's intercession? Will you now prioritize building the qualities that make intercession meaningful?

Definition and Enumeration of Major Sins
Defines major sins and provides enumerations from three Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of major sins (kaba'ir), the argument for their intrinsic distinction from minor sins, and the classical enumeration of the major sins.

Core Idea
Major sins are intrinsically defined by specific threats or lack of religious concern, not merely relative to other sins.

The concept of major sins (kaba'ir) is central to Islamic ethics. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid provides both a conceptual definition and a concrete list, arguing that major sins are distinct in essence—not merely in degree. Let's read the passage carefully to grasp the definitions, the Quranic argument, and the transmitted enumerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to understand the overall flow.
Notice the two definitions and the Quranic verse cited.
2
Identify the definitions and refutation
Find the two definitions of major sin and the refutation of the relativistic view. Understand why the author insists on intrinsic distinction.
The verse 'If you avoid the major sins...' is key to the argument.
3
Compare the three narrations
Look at the lists from Ibn Umar, Abu Hurairah, and Ali. Note which sins are common and which are added.
This shows how the enumeration was transmitted and expanded by different Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(خاتمة الكبيرة المعصية التي تشعر بعلة الاكتراث بالدين . وقيل : التي توعد عليها الشارع بخصوصها. وقيل الشرك(٣) ، والقتل(٤)، والقذف(٥)، والزنا (٦) والفرار من الزحف والسحر، وأكل مال اليتيم والعقوق والإلحاد في الحرم، وقد يراد أكل الربا والسرقة وشرب الخمر . ظاهر قوله تعالى : إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفر عنكم سياتكم) (۱). يدل على أن الكبائر متميزة عن الصغائر بالذات لا كما قيل أن كل سيئة فهي بالنسبة إلى ما فوقها صغيرة، وبالنسبة إلى ما تحتها كبيرة، لأنه لا حينئذ إجتناب الكبائر إلا بترك جميع المنهيات سوى واحدة هي دون الكل وأني للبشر ذلك. فمن ههنا ذهب بعضهم إلى تفسير الكبيرة بأنها التي تشعر بقلة الإكتراث بالدين أو التي توعد عليها الشارع .بخصوصها. وبعضهم إلى تعيين الكبائر، ففي رواية ابن عمر (رضي الله تعالى عنه أنها الشرك بالله ، وقتل النفس بغير حق، وقذف المحصنة، والزنا والفرار من الزحف، والسحر، وأكل مال اليتيم، وعقوق الوالدين المسلمين والإلحاد في الحرم . وزاد في رواية أبي هريرة : أكل الربا . وفي رواية على السرقة، وشرب الخمر.
(Conclusion on major sin: the disobedience that indicates lack of concern for religion. And it is said: that which the Lawgiver specifically threatened with punishment. And it is said: shirk (٣), murder (٤), slander (٥), adultery (٦), desertion from battle, sorcery, consuming the orphan's wealth, disobedience to parents, and heresy in the Sacred Sanctuary. And it may also include usury, theft, and drinking wine.) The apparent meaning of His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate for you your [minor] sins' (1) indicates that major sins are distinct from minor sins in essence, not as it is said that every sin is minor relative to what is above it and major relative to what is below it. For then avoiding major sins would only be possible by abandoning all prohibitions except one that is less than all, and that is impossible for humans. Hence some have gone to interpreting major sin as that which indicates lack of concern for religion, or that which the Lawgiver specifically threatened with punishment. And some have gone to specifying the major sins. In the narration of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): they are shirk with Allah, killing a soul without right, slandering a chaste woman, adultery, desertion from battle, sorcery, consuming the orphan's wealth, disobedience to Muslim parents, and heresy in the Sacred Sanctuary. In the narration of Abu Hurairah he added: usury. And in the narration of Ali: theft and drinking wine.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage begins with a conclusion (khatima) on major sins (kaba'ir). It first gives two definitions: a major sin is a transgression that shows lack of concern for religion, or it is a sin that the Lawgiver has specifically threatened with punishment. Then it lists a common enumeration including shirk, murder, slander, adultery, desertion from battle, sorcery, consuming orphan's wealth, disobedience to parents, heresy in the Sanctuary, and possibly usury, theft, and drinking wine. Next, it cites Quran 4:31 to argue that major sins are intrinsically distinct from minor sins, not merely relative. It refutes the relativistic view (that sins are major only in comparison) because it would make avoiding all major sins impossible—since one would have to avoid all sins except the smallest—which is beyond human capacity. It then notes two scholarly approaches: defining major sin by its indication of religious carelessness or by specific threat, and specifying the actual major sins. It provides the list from Ibn Umar (the first list) and additions from Abu Hurairah (usury) and Ali (theft and wine). The passage thus establishes both a conceptual definition and a concrete enumeration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A major sin is simply a sin that is greater than another sin; the classification is relative.
Correction: The passage argues that major sins are distinct in essence, not merely relative, because otherwise avoiding them would be impossible. The Quranic promise of expiation for minor sins upon avoiding major sins implies a real distinction.

Key Takeaway
Major sins are intrinsically defined by divine threat or lack of religious concern, and their enumeration is transmitted from the Companions with slight variations.

?

Defining Repentance as Regret and Its Motives
Defines repentance as regret and questions its validity under various motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential definition of repentance as regret for sin because it is disobedience, and the scholarly debate over the validity of repentance motivated by fear, hope, or other purposes, including repentance at the time of terminal illness.

Core Idea
Repentance is fundamentally regret for the sin itself, not just its consequences.

In this opening passage on repentance, the author sets out the core definition that distinguishes genuine repentance from mere emotional reactions. The text begins with a clear statement: repentance is regret for the sin because it is a sin. It then raises a series of questions about the motives that can accompany that regret—fear of Hell, hope of Paradise, or the ugliness of the act—and whether these affect the validity of repentance. The discussion also touches on the delicate case of repentance during a serious illness. This lesson unpacks these foundational points.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the first sentence carefully: الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية. Note that it specifies the reason: because it is disobedience.
This is the essential element.
2
Examine the motives debate
The text asks whether regret motivated by fear, hope, or the ugliness of sin with another purpose counts as repentance. Notice the hesitation: فيه تردد.
This indicates scholarly difference.
3
Consider terminal illness
The text specifically mentions repentance at a dangerous illness. Reflect on why this case is problematic: is it sincere or forced?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الندم على المعصية لكونها معصية. وهل الندم لخوف النار، أو طمع الجنة، ولقبح المعصية مع غرض آخر، وعند مرض مخوف توبة؟ فيه تردد، وقد يزاد قيد العزم على الترك في الاستقبال ويزاد على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار حتى لو سلب القدرة، لم يشترط العزم على الترك. والظاهر أنه للبيان دون الاحتراز ومعنى الندم الأسف والحزن، وتمنى كونه لم يفعل.
Regret for the disobedience because it is disobedience. And is regret for fear of the Fire, or hope for Paradise, and for the ugliness of disobedience with another purpose, and at a dangerous illness, repentance? There is hesitation regarding it. And the condition of resolve to abandon [the sin] in the future may be added, and [it is added] on the condition of occurrence and ability, so that if ability is removed, resolve to abandon is not required. And the apparent [view] is that it is for clarification not restriction. And the meaning of regret is sorrow and grief, and wishing that one had not done it.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is the fourteenth investigation on repentance (tawba). It defines repentance primarily as regret (nadam) for the disobedience because it is disobedience. It then discusses the motive for regret: whether it is valid if motivated by fear of Hell, hope of Paradise, or the ugliness of the sin itself along with another purpose, and whether repentance during a terminal illness is accepted; there is hesitation on these points. It then adds conditions: the condition of resolve ('azm) to abandon the sin in the future, but this condition applies only if the sin is possible to commit in the future and the person has the ability; if ability is removed (e.g., paralysis), then resolve is not required. The author notes that the apparent view is that these conditions are for clarification (bayan) not restriction (ihtiraz). Finally, it gives the lexical meaning of regret: sorrow, grief, and wishing it had not happened.

معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is only valid if motivated by fear of Hell or hope of Paradise.
Correction: The text defines repentance as regret for the sin itself, and it questions the validity of motives other than the sin being disobedience. The essential element is regret for the sin because it is a sin.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Repentance is fundamentally regret for the sin itself, and the validity of other motives is a matter of scholarly debate.

Conditions of Repentance: Resolve and Ability
Lists the additional conditions for repentance and clarifies their function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional conditions of repentance, particularly the requirement of resolve to abandon sin in the future and how it depends on ability, as well as the clarification that these conditions are explanatory, not restrictive.

Core Idea
The condition of resolve to abandon sin applies only if the sin is still possible and one has the ability.

After establishing the definition of repentance as regret, the author turns to additional conditions that some scholars require. The key condition is the sincere resolve ('azm) to abandon the sin in the future, but this condition is qualified: it only applies if the sin is conceivable (khutur) and one has the power (iqtidar) to commit it. If ability is removed, no such resolve is required. The author then clarifies that these conditions are meant to explain the concept, not to restrict it. Finally, the lexical meaning of regret is provided. This lesson unpacks these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the added condition
The text says 'قد يزاد قيد العزم على الترك في الاستقبال' — the condition of resolve to abandon in the future may be added. Note that it says 'may be added', indicating it's not unanimously accepted.
2
Understand the ability qualification
The condition is only on the assumption of occurrence (khutur) and ability (iqtidar). If ability is removed (e.g., paralysis), resolve is not required. Why? Because one cannot intend to leave what one cannot do.
This shows the condition is practical.
3
Grasp the clarification vs restriction
The author states 'الظاهر أنه للبيان دون الاحتراز' — the apparent view is that these conditions are for clarification, not restriction. This means they help explain what repentance entails, not that repentance is invalid without them.
This is a key theological point.
4
Note the lexical meaning
The passage ends by giving the linguistic meaning of regret: sorrow, grief, and wishing it had not happened. This reinforces the emotional aspect.

The Mu'tazila Argument on Punishment After Repentance and Its Refutation
Mu'tazila argument and Ash'ari dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that punishment after repentance is unjust and the Ash'ari dismissal of its weakness.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue penalty after repentance is injustice, but the Ash'ari say their reasoning is weak.

This lesson presents a specific Mu'tazila argument regarding divine justice after repentance. The Mu'tazila claim that punishing a repentant person is unjust, drawing an analogy with human apologies and the purpose of divine obligation. However, the Ash'ari author dismisses the argument as obviously weak, leaving the detailed refutation for later.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the passage carefully, noting the two reasons given for why punishment after repentance is unjust.
The passage is short, so take your time to grasp each reason.
2
Understand the analogy with human apology
Consider the analogy: if a person apologizes profusely, their sin is dropped. Think about why this analogy might fail when applied to God.
Human relationships are not perfectly analogous to divine justice.
3
Reflect on the purpose of taklīf
The second reason links obligation to reward, requiring removal of punishment. Ask yourself: does obligation always entail reward?
The Mu'tazila assume that taklīf's purpose is solely reward, which Ash'aris may challenge.
4
Note the Ash'ari response
The author simply says 'its weakness is apparent.' Consider what this tells you about the strength of the argument from an Ash'ari perspective.
The dismissal is terse; the full refutation comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى أن العقاب بعد التوبة ظلم. لأن من بالغ في الاعتذار إلى من أساء إليه، سقط ذنبه . ولأن التكليف باق وهو تعريض للثواب ولا يتصور إلا بسقوط العقاب. ولا طريق سوى التوبة . وضعفه ظاهر.
That punishment after repentance is injustice. Because the one who is profuse in apology to the one he has wronged, his sin falls away. And because the obligation (taklīf) remains, and it is an exposure to reward, and it is not conceivable except with the removal of punishment. And there is no way other than repentance. And its weakness is apparent.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazila proof that punishment after repentance is unjust, followed by a brief Ash'ari refutation. The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila claim: punishment after repentance is injustice (ẓulm). They give two reasons. First, by analogy with human affairs: if a person apologizes profusely to someone they have wronged, their sin is dropped. Second, the purpose of divine obligation (taklīf) is to expose one to reward, which can only happen if punishment is removed, and the only way to remove it is repentance. The author responds simply: 'And its weakness is apparent' (wa ḍuʿfuhu ẓāhir). This indicates that the Ash'aris do not accept these premises, though the detailed refutation is left for later passages.

Key Terms
توبة (tawba)
عقاب (ʿuqūba)
ظلم (ẓulm)
تكليف (taklīf)
اعتذار (iʿtidhār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Punishment after repentance is unjust because human apology removes guilt.
Correction: The Ash'ari argue that God is not bound by human conventions; repentance's effect on punishment is a matter of divine will, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument presented, why should punishment be removed after repentance?
A) Because God is merciful
B) Because the repentant has apologized and obligation requires reward
C) Because repentance is a virtuous act
D) Because the sinner deserves it

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that punishment after repentance is unjust, but the Ash'ari dismiss this as a weak argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin and repent, do you expect that God *must* forgive you and remove all punishment, or do you acknowledge that forgiveness is His right to grant or withhold? How does this lesson challenge your sense of entitlement before God?
?
Are you content with merely uttering words of repentance, or do you truly fear that your sin may still have consequences that only God's mercy can lift? What would change in your daily worship if you believed repentance alone does not guarantee the removal of punishment?
?
If the Ash'ari are correct that punishing after repentance is not necessarily unjust, how should that affect your hope in God's mercy and your fear of His justice? Are you prepared to stand before Him relying not on your repentance, but on His grace?

What Removes Punishment After Repentance? Three Views
Classifies views on removal of punishment after repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological views on what removes punishment after repentance, especially the Ash'ari emphasis on divine grace and the nature of repentance as worship.

Core Idea
Repentance is an act of worship, but it is God's pure grace that removes punishment.

This passage from the Maqasid presents a concise classification of different theological positions on a key soteriological question: what exactly removes the punishment due to sin after one repents? The Ash'ari answer, grounded in divine grace, is distinguished from Mu'tazilite views. Understanding this classification clarifies the role of grace versus human action in salvation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
The passage lists three positions: most Mu'tazila say repentance itself, some say abundance of reward, Ash'aris say pure grace. Find each.
2
Understand the Ash'ari nuance
The text adds that sound repentance is an act of worship with reward not nullified by later sin. Reflect on how this relates to the 'pure grace' position.
3
See the implications for repentance
Note that repenting again is a separate act of worship. This shows the value of repeated repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سقوط العقوبة عند أكثر المعتزلة بنفس التوبة . وعند بعضهم بكثرة ثوابها. وعندنا بمحض الكرم. والتوبة الصحيحة عبادة لا يبطل ثوابها بمعاودة الذنب. والتوبة ثانياً عبادة أخرى.
Then the removal of punishment: according to most Mu'tazila, by repentance itself; according to some of them, by the abundance of its reward; and according to us (Ash'aris), by pure grace. And sound repentance is an act of worship whose reward is not nullified by the recurrence of sin. And repenting a second time is another act of worship.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage classifies the different theological positions on what causes the punishment to be removed after repentance. The author presents three views: the majority of Mu'tazila hold that repentance itself (nafs al-tawba) removes punishment; a minority among them hold that it is the abundance of reward (kathrat thawābihā) that removes it; and the Ash'ari position is that it is by pure grace (maḥḍ al-karam) from God. The author then adds two further points about the nature of repentance: a sound repentance is an act of worship (ʿibāda) and its reward is not invalidated by later sin; if one sins again and repents again, that is a separate act of worship. This emphasizes the meritorious nature of repentance in the Ash'ari view.

Key Terms
سقوط العقوبة
كرم
عبادة
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view of pure grace denies any efficacy of repentance.
Correction: The passage shows that repentance is an act of worship with reward, but the removal of punishment is attributed to divine grace, not the repentance itself.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view as stated in this passage, what removes the punishment after repentance?
A) Repentance itself
B) Abundance of reward
C) Pure grace of God
D) The sinner's apology

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that while repentance is a meritorious act of worship, the removal of punishment depends solely on God's pure grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin again after repenting, do you feel your prior repentance was wasted? How does the statement that repentance’s reward is not nullified by recurrence of sin change your view of God’s grace?
?
If punishment is removed only by God’s pure grace—not your repentance itself—does that make you more humble or more careless? Honestly, which reaction rises in your heart, and what does that reveal about your trust in God?
?
You have likely repented many times. Do you treat each repentance as a separate act of worship, or do you grow weary? How can you cultivate gratitude for the fact that every new repentance is a fresh opportunity for worship, not a repetition of failure?

The Obligation of Repentance: Revelation or Reason?
Discussion of consensus on repentance and its two bases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus on the obligation of repentance, the different bases for it (revelation for Ash'aris, reason for Mu'tazila), and how this rational basis refutes the Bahshamiyya position on minor sins.

Core Idea
Repentance is obligatory by consensus, but the basis differs: scripture for Ash'aris, reason for Mu'tazila.

This passage from al-Maqasid begins by affirming the universal obligation of repentance. The author then clarifies the underlying justification from two theological perspectives: the Ash'ari reliance on divine command and the Mu'tazila rational argument. Additionally, the passage engages with a specific Mu'tazila sub-sect, the Bahshamiyya, concerning minor sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the consensus
Read the Arabic passage once to see the claim of no disagreement. Then note the two bases presented.
The consensus is stated at the very beginning.
2
Understand the two bases: sam' and 'aql
Notice how the Ash'ari basis is scriptural (sam'an) with Quranic verses, while the Mu'tazila basis is rational (ʿaqlan) based on repelling harm and regret as a rational requirement.
The word 'samʿan' and 'ʿaqlan' are technical terms in kalam.
3
Examine the argument against Bahshamiyya
The rational basis covers minor sins, which is used as an argument against the Bahshamiyya who held that repentance from minor sins is only scripture-based.
Bahshamiyya is a sub-sect of Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on why the difference matters
Consider how the basis of obligation affects theological reasoning about repentance and sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نزاع في وجوب التوبة . أما عندنا فسمعاً لقوله تعالى: {وتوبوا إلى الله جميعاً} {توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحاً} ونحو ذلك . وأما عند المعتزلة فعقلاً لما فيها من دفع ضرر العقاب. ولما أن الندم على القبيح من مقتضيات العقل الصحيح. وهذا يتناول الصغائر أيضاً، فيكون حجة على البهشمية القائلين بوجوب التوبة عن الصغائر سمعاً لا عقلاً، لسقوط عقوبتها . ثم المصرح في كلام المعتزلة أن وجوب التوبة على الفور حتى يلزمه بتأخير ساعة إثم آخر يجب التوبة عنه، وهلم جرا. حتى ذكروا أن بتأخير التوبة عن الكبيرة ساعة واحدة تكون له كبيرتان المعصية، وترك التوبة . وساعتين أربع الأوليان وترك التوبة عن كل منهما. وثلاث ساعات ثمان، وهكذا .
There is no disagreement concerning the obligation of repentance. As for us (Ash'aris), it is by transmitted evidence (samʿan) due to His saying, 'And turn in repentance to Allah all of you,' 'Turn to Allah with a sincere repentance,' and the like. As for the Mu'tazila, it is by rational evidence (ʿaqlan) because of what it contains of repelling the harm of punishment, and because regret for the ugly (al-qabīḥ) is among the requirements of sound reason. And this includes minor sins as well, so it is an argument against the Bahshamiyya who say the obligation of repentance for minor sins is by transmission not by reason, because their punishment is removed. Then it is explicitly stated in the speech of the Mu'tazila that the obligation of repentance is immediate (ʿalā al-fawr), such that by delaying it an hour another sin becomes incumbent upon him which must be repented from, and so on. Even they mentioned that by delaying repentance from a major sin one hour, he has two major sins: the sin itself and the abandonment of repentance. And two hours: four: the first two and the abandonment of repentance from each of them. And three hours: eight, etc.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation (wujūb) of repentance. The author first states that there is no disagreement that repentance is obligatory, then distinguishes the basis of obligation. For Ash'aris, it is known through revelation (samʿan), citing two Quranic verses. For Mu'tazila, it is known rationally (ʿaqlan) because repentance repels the harm of punishment and because regret for what is ugly (al-qabīḥ) is required by sound reason. The author notes that this rational basis covers minor sins as well, which serves as an argument against the Bahshamiyya (a Mu'tazila sub-sect) who held that repentance from minor sins is obligatory only by revelation, since their punishment is already removed. The passage then explains that the Mu'tazila hold the obligation to be immediate (fawr), meaning that any delay incurs an additional sin that also requires repentance. The author illustrates: a one-hour delay from a major sin results in two major sins (the original sin and the delay itself); two hours results in four; three hours in eight, etc. This demonstrates the strictness of the Mu'tazila position on the timing of repentance.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujūb)
سمع (samʿ)
عقل (ʿaql)
صغائر (ṣaghāʾir)
بهشمية (Bahshamiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny any rational obligation in repentance.
Correction: Ash'aris accept rational obligation in principle, but here they ground the specific obligation of repentance in scripture.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the obligation of repentance for the Ash'aris?
A) Reason (ʿaql)
B) Revelation (samʿ)
C) Consensus (ijmāʿ)
D) Intuition (ḥads)

Key Takeaway
Repentance is obligatory by consensus, but the source of obligation differs: scripture for Ash'aris and reason for Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you repent, do you feel you are fulfilling a command from Allah out of obedience to scripture, or are you truly repelled by the ugliness of the sin in your own mind? Which motive dominates your repentance, and what does that reveal about your reliance on revelation versus reason?
?
The lesson says repentance for minor sins is also obligatory by reason. Think of a ‘small’ sin you have committed recently and dismissed as trivial. Have you repented from it? If not, why haven’t you? What would it look like for you to treat that minor sin with the same seriousness as a major one?
?
The Bahshamiyya thought minor sins don't require rational repentance because their punishment is removed. Are there sins you assume are ‘too small’ to bother repenting from, as if they are automatically forgiven? Will you today make a list of such sins and sincerely repent from each one, knowing that your own reason demands it?

The Immediate Obligation of Repentance and the Multiplication of Sins
Explanation of Mu'tazila view on immediate repentance and multiplicative sin count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of immediate repentance (fawr) and how delaying repentance multiplies sins exponentially.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, delaying repentance even an hour creates an endless chain of new sins.

The second part of the passage delves into a specific Mu'tazila position: that repentance must be performed immediately. Any delay constitutes a new sin requiring its own repentance, leading to a geometric increase in the number of sins. This stark view highlights the seriousness of procrastination in repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on immediate obligation
Read the Arabic text about fawr and the example of delay. Try to understand the pattern described.
The example uses a major sin (kabīra).
2
Understand the meaning of fawr
Fawr means immediate obligation; any delay is itself sinful. Notice the consequence: each delay adds a new sin to repent from.
This is a strict view unique to the Mu'tazila.
3
Work through the one-hour delay example
Delaying one hour from a major sin results in two major sins: the original sin and the sin of delaying repentance.
The delay is considered a separate major sin.
4
See the pattern of multiplication
The pattern is geometric: each new hour adds a new sin of delay for each previous sin, doubling the count. Understand how it grows: 1 hour → 2 sins; 2 hours → 4; 3 → 8; etc.
The passage explicitly calculates up to three hours.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نزاع في وجوب التوبة . أما عندنا فسمعاً لقوله تعالى: {وتوبوا إلى الله جميعاً} {توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحاً} ونحو ذلك . وأما عند المعتزلة فعقلاً لما فيها من دفع ضرر العقاب. ولما أن الندم على القبيح من مقتضيات العقل الصحيح. وهذا يتناول الصغائر أيضاً، فيكون حجة على البهشمية القائلين بوجوب التوبة عن الصغائر سمعاً لا عقلاً، لسقوط عقوبتها . ثم المصرح في كلام المعتزلة أن وجوب التوبة على الفور حتى يلزمه بتأخير ساعة إثم آخر يجب التوبة عنه، وهلم جرا. حتى ذكروا أن بتأخير التوبة عن الكبيرة ساعة واحدة تكون له كبيرتان المعصية، وترك التوبة . وساعتين أربع الأوليان وترك التوبة عن كل منهما. وثلاث ساعات ثمان، وهكذا .
There is no disagreement concerning the obligation of repentance. As for us (Ash'aris), it is by transmitted evidence (samʿan) due to His saying, 'And turn in repentance to Allah all of you,' 'Turn to Allah with a sincere repentance,' and the like. As for the Mu'tazila, it is by rational evidence (ʿaqlan) because of what it contains of repelling the harm of punishment, and because regret for the ugly (al-qabīḥ) is among the requirements of sound reason. And this includes minor sins as well, so it is an argument against the Bahshamiyya who say the obligation of repentance for minor sins is by transmission not by reason, because their punishment is removed. Then it is explicitly stated in the speech of the Mu'tazila that the obligation of repentance is immediate (ʿalā al-fawr), such that by delaying it an hour another sin becomes incumbent upon him which must be repented from, and so on. Even they mentioned that by delaying repentance from a major sin one hour, he has two major sins: the sin itself and the abandonment of repentance. And two hours: four: the first two and the abandonment of repentance from each of them. And three hours: eight, etc.

This passage addresses the obligation (wujūb) of repentance. The author first states that there is no disagreement that repentance is obligatory, then distinguishes the basis of obligation. For Ash'aris, it is known through revelation (samʿan), citing two Quranic verses. For Mu'tazila, it is known rationally (ʿaqlan) because repentance repels the harm of punishment and because regret for what is ugly (al-qabīḥ) is required by sound reason. توبة (tawba)
إثم (ithm)

Misconception: That the Mu'tazila consider the delay sin to be minor.
Correction: The text explicitly calls both the original sin and the delay sin 'major sins' (kabīratān).

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view presented, what happens if one delays repentance from a major sin by one hour?
A) Only the original sin remains
B) The original sin is multiplied
C) Two major sins are incurred: the sin and the delay

The Ash'ari View on Acceptance of Repentance
Ash'ari denial of obligation on God and Imām al-Ḥaramayn's probabilistic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that acceptance of repentance is not obligatory upon God, and how Imām al-Ḥaramayn qualifies its establishment as based on probabilistic evidence.

Core Idea
God is not obliged to accept repentance; its acceptance is a promise based on probable proof.

The Ash'ari school maintains that God is under no obligation to accept repentance, since He is the sovereign Lord. Yet they affirm that repentance is commanded and accepted as a matter of divine promise, though the evidence for this is not definitively certain. This lesson introduces that foundational stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage first states the Ash'ari denial of obligation (lā wujūb), then raises a question about transmission (samʿan wa waʿdan) and answers it.
Observe how Imām al-Ḥaramayn enters the discussion.
3
Reflect on the significance of 'dalīl ẓannī'
Consider why the evidence is called probabilistic rather than definitive. What does this imply about the certainty of the promise?
This highlights a key Ash'ari methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قبول التوبة فلا يجب عندنا إذ لا وجوب على الله تعالى . وهل ثبت سمعاً ووعداً؟ قال إمام الحرمين نعم بدليل ظني، إذ لم يثبت في ذلك نص قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل. وعند المعتزلة يجب حتى قالوا : إن العقاب بعد التوبة ظلم . لكن بمقتضى الجود على رأي البغدادية، وبمقتضى العدل والحكمة على رأي الجمهور. واحتجوا بأن العاصي قد بذل وسعه في التلافي، فيسقط عقابه، كمن بالغ في الاعتذار إلى من أساء إليه سقط ذمه بالضرورة، وبأن التكليف باق، وهو تعريض للثواب ولا يتصور إلا بسقوط العقاب، فوجب أن يكون له مخلص من العقاب، وليس غير التوبة، فوجب أن يكون مخلصاً، وأكثر المقدمات مزخرف بل ربما يدعي القطع بأن من أساء إلى غيره، وانتهك حماته، ثم جاء معتدراً لا يجب في حكم العقل قبول اعتذاره بل الخيرة إلى دك الغير، إن شاء صفح، وإن شاء جازاه . وأما احتجاجنا بالإجماع على الابتهال إلى الله تعالى في وجوب قبول التوبة، وعلى وجوب شكره على ذلك، فربما يدفع بأن المسئول هو استجماعها بشرائط القبول فإن الأمر فيه خطير. ووجوب القبول لا ينافي وجوب الشكر لكونه إحساناً في نفسه كتربية الوالد لولده يجب شكرها مع وجوبها .
As for the acceptance of repentance, it is not obligatory in our view, since there is no obligation upon Allah the Exalted. And is it established by transmission (samʿan) and promise (waʿdan)? The Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: Yes, by a probabilistic evidence (dalīl ẓannī), since there is no definitive text (naṣṣ qāṭiʿ) on that which is not open to interpretation. According to the Mu'tazila, it is obligatory, to the extent that they said: punishment after repentance is injustice. But according to the view of the Baghdādiyya, it is by virtue of generosity (jūd), and according to the view of the majority (al-jumhūr), by virtue of justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma). They argued that the sinner has exerted his effort (badhala wasʿahu) in making amends (al-talāfī), so his punishment is dropped, like one who is profuse in apology to the one he has wronged, his blame falls away by necessity. And because the obligation (taklīf) remains, and it is an exposure to reward, and it is not conceivable except with the removal of punishment, so it is necessary that he have a way out (mukhliṣ) from punishment, and there is no way other than repentance, so it is necessary that it be a way out. And most of the premises are embellished (muzakhraf); rather, one may claim certainty that one who wrongs another and violates his sanctities then comes apologizing, it is not obligatory in rational judgment to accept his apology; rather, the choice is up to the other person: if he wishes he forgives, and if he wishes he punishes. As for our argument by consensus (ijmāʿ) on supplication to Allah for acceptance of repentance and on gratitude to Him for that, it may be rebutted by saying that what is asked for is the fulfillment of repentance with its conditions of acceptance, for the matter is serious. And the obligation of acceptance does not negate the obligation of gratitude, because it is a favor in itself, like the upbringing of a parent for his child: gratitude for it is obligatory along with its being obligatory.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the acceptance (qubūl) of repentance, specifically whether God is obliged to accept it. The Ash'ari position is that it is not obligatory upon God (lā wujūb ʿalā Allāh). The author cites Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) who says acceptance is established by transmitted evidence but only as a probabilistic (ẓannī) proof, not a definitive one (qāṭiʿ). The Mu'tazila hold that acceptance is obligatory, even to the point of claiming that punishment after repentance would be injustice. The passage distinguishes two Mu'tazila sub-sects: the Baghdādiyya base the obligation on divine generosity (jūd), while the majority base it on divine justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma). The Mu'tazila proofs are then presented: the sinner has exerted his effort in making amends, so punishment should drop, similar to an apology that removes blame by necessity; and because the purpose of obligation (taklīf) is exposure to reward, which requires removal of punishment, there must be a way out, which is repentance, so repentance must be effective. The author responds by calling most of these premises 'embellished' (muzakhraf) and argues that, rationally, accepting an apology from a wrongdoer is not obligatory; the wronged party has the choice to forgive or punish. The passage also mentions an Ash'ari proof from consensus (ijmāʿ) on supplicating for acceptance and thanking God for it, but this can be countered by noting that what is asked for is the fulfillment of repentance with its proper conditions, and the obligation of acceptance does not contradict the obligation of gratitude, as illustrated by the example of a parent's upbringing: the parent is obliged to raise the child, yet gratitude for it is still due.

Key Terms
قبول (qubūl)
إمام الحرمين (Imām al-Ḥaramayn)
دليل ظني (dalīl ẓannī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris reject the acceptance of repentance altogether.
Correction: Ash'aris deny that acceptance is obligatory on God, but they affirm it occurs by God's promise, though based on probabilistic evidence.

Checkpoint
According to Imām al-Ḥaramayn, the acceptance of repentance is established by what kind of evidence?
A) A definitive text (naṣṣ qāṭiʿ)
B) A probabilistic evidence (dalīl ẓannī)
C) Rational necessity
D) Consensus (ijmāʿ)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view is that God is not obliged to accept repentance, but He has promised acceptance, though the proof for that is probabilistic, not definitive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has no obligation to accept your repentance, how does that sobering truth affect the urgency and sincerity of your turn back to Him? Are you relying on His promise or taking His grace for granted?
?
When you sin, do you assume God will accept your repentance? How does knowing that the proof for acceptance is only probabilistic affect your conviction in turning back to Him and your hope in His mercy?
?
What would it mean for you if God did not accept your repentance? How does this teaching shape your humility and dependence on Him alone?

The Mu'tazila Position and Their Arguments for Obligatory Acceptance
Mu'tazila view that repentance acceptance is obligatory, with two sub-schools and two arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view that God must accept repentance as an obligation based on either generosity or justice, and their two main arguments.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that punishing after repentance would be unjust, so acceptance is rationally necessary.

The Mu'tazila held a strong rationalist stance: God is bound by justice and generosity, so He must accept repentance. They presented two arguments: one from analogy with human apology, and one from the purpose of divine obligation. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position and its two variants
Read the part where the Mu'tazila obligation is introduced, noting the two sub-schools: Baghdādiyya (generosity) and majority (justice and wisdom).
2
Examine the first argument: analogy with apology
The Mu'tazila argue that a sinner who makes amends is like someone who apologizes profusely; blame drops by necessity. Ask: is this analogy sound?
3
Examine the second argument: necessity of a way out
The second argument is that divine obligation (taklīf) aims at reward, which requires removal of punishment, so repentance must be a way out (mukhliṣ).
Notice the logical steps: obligation remains -> exposure to reward -> needs removal of punishment -> only repentance provides that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قبول التوبة فلا يجب عندنا إذ لا وجوب على الله تعالى . وهل ثبت سمعاً ووعداً؟ قال إمام الحرمين نعم بدليل ظني، إذ لم يثبت في ذلك نص قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل. وعند المعتزلة يجب حتى قالوا : إن العقاب بعد التوبة ظلم . لكن بمقتضى الجود على رأي البغدادية، وبمقتضى العدل والحكمة على رأي الجمهور. واحتجوا بأن العاصي قد بذل وسعه في التلافي، فيسقط عقابه، كمن بالغ في الاعتذار إلى من أساء إليه سقط ذمه بالضرورة، وبأن التكليف باق، وهو تعريض للثواب ولا يتصور إلا بسقوط العقاب، فوجب أن يكون له مخلص من العقاب، وليس غير التوبة، فوجب أن يكون مخلصاً، وأكثر المقدمات مزخرف بل ربما يدعي القطع بأن من أساء إلى غيره، وانتهك حماته، ثم جاء معتدراً لا يجب في حكم العقل قبول اعتذاره بل الخيرة إلى دك الغير، إن شاء صفح، وإن شاء جازاه . وأما احتجاجنا بالإجماع على الابتهال إلى الله تعالى في وجوب قبول التوبة، وعلى وجوب شكره على ذلك، فربما يدفع بأن المسئول هو استجماعها بشرائط القبول فإن الأمر فيه خطير. ووجوب القبول لا ينافي وجوب الشكر لكونه إحساناً في نفسه كتربية الوالد لولده يجب شكرها مع وجوبها .
As for the acceptance of repentance, it is not obligatory in our view, since there is no obligation upon Allah the Exalted. And is it established by transmission (samʿan) and promise (waʿdan)? The Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: Yes, by a probabilistic evidence (dalīl ẓannī), since there is no definitive text (naṣṣ qāṭiʿ) on that which is not open to interpretation. According to the Mu'tazila, it is obligatory, to the extent that they said: punishment after repentance is injustice. But according to the view of the Baghdādiyya, it is by virtue of generosity (jūd), and according to the view of the majority (al-jumhūr), by virtue of justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma). They argued that the sinner has exerted his effort (badhala wasʿahu) in making amends (al-talāfī), so his punishment is dropped, like one who is profuse in apology to the one he has wronged, his blame falls away by necessity. And because the obligation (taklīf) remains, and it is an exposure to reward, and it is not conceivable except with the removal of punishment, so it is necessary that he have a way out (mukhliṣ) from punishment, and there is no way other than repentance, so it is necessary that it be a way out. And most of the premises are embellished (muzakhraf); rather, one may claim certainty that one who wrongs another and violates his sanctities then comes apologizing, it is not obligatory in rational judgment to accept his apology; rather, the choice is up to the other person: if he wishes he forgives, and if he wishes he punishes. As for our argument by consensus (ijmāʿ) on supplication to Allah for acceptance of repentance and on gratitude to Him for that, it may be rebutted by saying that what is asked for is the fulfillment of repentance with its conditions of acceptance, for the matter is serious. And the obligation of acceptance does not negate the obligation of gratitude, because it is a favor in itself, like the upbringing of a parent for his child: gratitude for it is obligatory along with its being obligatory.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the acceptance (qubūl) of repentance, specifically whether God is obliged to accept it. The Ash'ari position is that it is not obligatory upon God (lā wujūb ʿalā Allāh). The author cites Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) who says acceptance is established by transmitted evidence but only as a probabilistic (ẓannī) proof, not a definitive one (qāṭiʿ). The Mu'tazila hold that acceptance is obligatory, even to the point of claiming that punishment after repentance would be injustice. The passage distinguishes two Mu'tazila sub-sects: the Baghdādiyya base the obligation on divine generosity (jūd), while the majority base it on divine justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma). The Mu'tazila proofs are then presented: the sinner has exerted his effort in making amends, so punishment should drop, similar to an apology that removes blame by necessity; and because the purpose of obligation (taklīf) is exposure to reward, which requires removal of punishment, there must be a way out, which is repentance, so repentance must be effective. The author responds by calling most of these premises 'embellished' (muzakhraf) and argues that, rationally, accepting an apology from a wrongdoer is not obligatory; the wronged party has the choice to forgive or punish. The passage also mentions an Ash'ari proof from consensus (ijmāʿ) on supplicating for acceptance and thanking God for it, but this can be countered by noting that what is asked for is the fulfillment of repentance with its proper conditions, and the obligation of acceptance does not contradict the obligation of gratitude, as illustrated by the example of a parent's upbringing: the parent is obliged to raise the child, yet gratitude for it is still due.

Key Terms
جود (jūd)
عدل (ʿadl)
حكمة (ḥikma)
تلافي (talāfī)
مخلص (mukhliṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila base the obligation of repentance on justice alone.
Correction: The Baghdādiyya base it on generosity (jūd), while the majority base it on justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the Baghdādiyya Mu'tazila base the obligation of repentance on which attribute?
A) Justice (ʿadl)
B) Generosity (jūd)
C) Wisdom (ḥikma)
D) Mercy (raḥma)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that God must accept repentance, either by generosity or by justice, and they argue that the sinner's effort to make amends and the purpose of divine obligation necessitate such acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that your repentance automatically obligates God to forgive you, as if He owes it to you? How does the Mu'tazila reasoning in this passage challenge or affirm your understanding of God's justice and mercy?
?
If you truly believed that punishment after repentance is 'injustice,' would you still feel the weight of your sins or take God's forgiveness for granted? What does this passage reveal about your heart's attitude toward repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that the purpose of divine obligation is reward, and therefore punishment must be removable. Do you live as if the only way out is repentance, or do you secretly hope for some other escape? What change must you make today based on this truth?

Ash'ari Responses to the Mu'tazila Arguments and the Consensus Proof
Ash'ari refutation of Mu'tazila arguments and discussion of consensus proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians respond to the Mu'tazila arguments for obligatory repentance, including the rejection of the analogy and the handling of the consensus argument.

Core Idea
Human analogies fail to prove obligation on God; even consensus on supplication does not entail divine obligation.

The Ash'ari scholars were not convinced by the Mu'tazila rational arguments. They pointed out that the analogy with human apology is flawed because the wronged person is not obligated to accept an apology. They also addressed their own argument from consensus, showing that it does not prove obligation. This lesson examines those rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari rejection of the analogy
The Ash'ari response dismisses the Mu'tazila premises as 'embellished' and argues that accepting an apology is not rationally obligatory; the wronged has full discretion.
Note the force of 'bal khīra ilā dhaaka al-ghayr'.
2
Read the consensus argument and its rebuttal
The Ash'ari had argued from consensus on supplication; the rebuttal is that what is asked for is repentance with its proper conditions, not acceptance per se.
3
Understand the parent-child analogy
The rebuttal uses an analogy: a parent is obliged to raise a child, yet gratitude for that upbringing is still obligatory. Obligation and gratitude can coexist.
This clarifies why thanking God for acceptance does not imply it was not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قبول التوبة فلا يجب عندنا إذ لا وجوب على الله تعالى . وهل ثبت سمعاً ووعداً؟ قال إمام الحرمين نعم بدليل ظني، إذ لم يثبت في ذلك نص قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل. وعند المعتزلة يجب حتى قالوا : إن العقاب بعد التوبة ظلم . لكن بمقتضى الجود على رأي البغدادية، وبمقتضى العدل والحكمة على رأي الجمهور. واحتجوا بأن العاصي قد بذل وسعه في التلافي، فيسقط عقابه، كمن بالغ في الاعتذار إلى من أساء إليه سقط ذمه بالضرورة، وبأن التكليف باق، وهو تعريض للثواب ولا يتصور إلا بسقوط العقاب، فوجب أن يكون له مخلص من العقاب، وليس غير التوبة، فوجب أن يكون مخلصاً، وأكثر المقدمات مزخرف بل ربما يدعي القطع بأن من أساء إلى غيره، وانتهك حماته، ثم جاء معتدراً لا يجب في حكم العقل قبول اعتذاره بل الخيرة إلى دك الغير، إن شاء صفح، وإن شاء جازاه . وأما احتجاجنا بالإجماع على الابتهال إلى الله تعالى في وجوب قبول التوبة، وعلى وجوب شكره على ذلك، فربما يدفع بأن المسئول هو استجماعها بشرائط القبول فإن الأمر فيه خطير. ووجوب القبول لا ينافي وجوب الشكر لكونه إحساناً في نفسه كتربية الوالد لولده يجب شكرها مع وجوبها .

But according to the view of the Baghdādiyya, it is by virtue of generosity (jūd), and according to the view of the majority (al-jumhūr), by virtue of justice (ʿadl) and wisdom (ḥikma). According to the Ash'ari response, what is the rational judgment on accepting an apology from a wrongdoer?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin and repent, do you approach Allah expecting acceptance as a right, or do you recognize that even the conditions of repentance are a mercy from Him? How does the Ash'ari insight that acceptance is a favor (iḥsān) change the way you ask for forgiveness tonight?
?
Think of a time you wronged someone deeply. Did you believe they were obligated to accept your apology? How does the Ash'ari claim that acceptance is not rationally obligatory challenge your sense of entitlement? In what way might this affect how you view Allah's forgiveness?
?
The parent analogy shows that gratitude is due even for obligatory acts. When was the last time you thanked Allah for something you considered routine or owed to you? How can you cultivate a heart that sees every divine action—even the expected ones—as a favor deserving of praise?

The Dispute on the Remover of Punishment: Repentance vs. Abundant Reward
Presents the Mu'tazila disagreement and refutes the abundant-reward view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazila views on what removes punishment and the argument against the abundant-reward view.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila split on whether repentance or its reward erases sin.

This lesson examines a key intra-Mu'tazila disagreement about the mechanism of forgiveness. Is it the act of repentance itself that removes punishment, or the abundant reward that God bestows for it? The passage presents both views and offers a refutation of the latter, laying the groundwork for the Ash'ari position of pure grace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views
Read the opening sentence to see the split among Mu'tazila: most say by repentance itself, some say by abundant reward.
2
Understand the argument for the abundant-reward view
The text gives a conditional justification: if repentance itself removed punishment, then even compelled repentance or repentance upon seeing the Fire would suffice, which is problematic.
This is a hypothetical argument from their opponents.
3
Analyze the refutation of the abundant-reward view
The passage then presents an argument from the majority Mu'tazila: if abundant reward were the cause, then repentance from any sin should equally remove punishment, but a counterexample shows this is false (repent from all sins then drink wine).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في مسقط العقوبة . فعند أكثر المعتزلة بنفس التوبة، وعند بعضهم بكثرة ثوابها، إذ لو كان بنفس التوبة لسقط بتوبة الملجأ ويندم العاصي عند معاينة النار . ورد بمنع الندم في صورة الإلجاء وبمنع كونه للقبح في صورة المعاينة. واحتج الأكثرون بأنه لو كان بكثرة الثواب لما اختصت التوبة عن معصية معينة بسقوط عقابها دون أخرى، لأن نسبة كثرة الثواب إلى الكل على السوية. ولما بقى فرق بين التوبة المتقدمة على المعصية والمتأخرة عنها في إسقاط عقابها كسائر الطاعات التي تسقط العقوبات بكثرة ثوابها واللازم باطل للقطع بأن من تاب عن المعاصي كلها، ثم شرب الخمر لا يسقط عنه عقاب الشرب . وأما عندنا فهو بمحض عفو الله تعالى وكرمه وتوبته الصحيحة عبادة يثاب عليها تفضلاً، ولا تبطل بمعاودة الذنب، ثم إذا تاب عنه ثانياً يكون عبادة أخرى.
Then they disagreed concerning the remover of punishment. According to most Mu'tazila, it is by repentance itself, and according to some of them, by the abundance of its reward, because if it were by repentance itself, it would be removed by the repentance of the compelled (tawbat al-muljaʾ) and the repentance of the sinner when he sees the Fire. And it was refuted by denying regret (al-nadam) in the case of compulsion and by denying that it is for the ugliness (al-qubḥ) in the case of seeing the Fire. And the majority argued: if it were by the abundance of reward, then repentance from a particular sin would not be specifically tied to the removal of its punishment without another, because the relation of abundant reward to all is equal. And there would remain no difference between repentance preceding a sin and repentance coming after it in removing its punishment, like other acts of obedience that remove punishments by the abundance of their reward. But the necessary consequence is false, because it is certain that if someone repents from all sins, then drinks wine, the punishment for drinking wine is not removed from him. As for us (Ash'aris), it is by pure pardon (ʿafw) and grace (karam) of Allah the Exalted, and sound repentance is an act of worship rewarded as a favor, and it is not nullified by the recurrence of sin; then if one repents a second time, it is another act of worship.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage returns to the disagreement on the remover of punishment (musqiṭ al-ʿuqūba). The two Mu'tazila positions are reiterated: most say repentance itself, some say the abundant reward of repentance. The passage then presents a refutation of the abundant-reward view. First, it states the argument for the abundant-reward view: if repentance itself were the cause, then even the repentance of a compelled person (muljaʾ) or someone who repents upon seeing the Fire (muʿāyana) would be effective, which is problematic. The refutation denies that such regret exists in the case of compulsion and denies that it is truly for ugliness (qubḥ) in the case of witnessing the Fire. Then the majority of Mu'tazila (those who hold repentance itself) argue against the abundant-reward view: if it were the cause, then repentance from a particular sin would not be specifically tied to removing its own punishment, because the abundant reward applies equally to all sins. Also, there would be no difference between repentance before committing a sin and repentance after, but in fact there is a difference (because repentance before sin is not relevant). They give a decisive counterexample: if a person repents from all sins but then drinks wine, the punishment for drinking wine is not removed, which contradicts the claim that abundant reward removes all punishments. The Ash'ari position is then reaffirmed: it is by pure pardon (ʿafw) and grace (karam) of God. The passage also reiterates that sound repentance is an act of worship rewarded as a favor (tafaḍḍulan), and that its reward is not invalidated by later sin, and that a second repentance is a separate act of worship.

Key Terms
مسقط (musqiṭ)
ملجأ (muljaʾ)
إلجاء (iljāʾ)
قبح (qubḥ)
معاينة (muʿāyana)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Mu'tazila hold the same view on the remover of punishment.
Correction: In fact, there is a split: most say repentance itself, some say abundant reward.

Checkpoint
What is the counterexample given against the view that abundant reward removes punishment?
A) Repentance from all sins then drinking wine
B) Repentance under compulsion
C) Repentance before sin
D) Repentance upon seeing the Fire

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila disagree internally on whether repentance itself or its abundant reward removes punishment, and a decisive counterexample refutes the abundant-reward view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever repented from a sin but still felt its weight in your heart? Why do you think God allows that feeling to remain? What does this lesson teach about the necessity of genuine regret versus merely piling up good deeds?
?
If you could 'erase' your sins by accumulating reward, would that make God's justice seem arbitrary? How does the example of the person who repents from all sins but then drinks wine expose the need for specific repentance for each sin?
?
Is your own repentance driven by fear of punishment, hope for reward, or genuine sorrow for the ugliness of sin? How does this lesson force you to examine the sincerity of your repentance?

The Ash'ari Position: Pure Grace and Repentance as Worship
Affirms the Ash'ari position of pure grace and describes repentance as worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that punishment is removed by God's pure grace, while repentance is an act of worship that is rewarded but not nullified by later sin.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is entirely God's grace, not a mechanical consequence of repentance.

After refuting the Mu'tazila positions, the text presents the Ash'ari affirmation: punishment is removed by God's pure pardon and grace. Repentance is not a cause but an act of worship that receives reward. This lesson clarifies that repentance does not automatically erase punishment, and that its reward is not invalidated by subsequent sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari view on the remover of punishment
Read the opening phrase: 'it is by pure pardon and grace of Allah.'
2
Understand the status of repentance
The text says sound repentance is an act of worship rewarded as a favor, and it is not nullified by repeating the sin.
3
Note the implication for repeated repentance
If one repents a second time, it is a separate act of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا في مسقط العقوبة . فعند أكثر المعتزلة بنفس التوبة، وعند بعضهم بكثرة ثوابها، إذ لو كان بنفس التوبة لسقط بتوبة الملجأ ويندم العاصي عند معاينة النار . ورد بمنع الندم في صورة الإلجاء وبمنع كونه للقبح في صورة المعاينة. واحتج الأكثرون بأنه لو كان بكثرة الثواب لما اختصت التوبة عن معصية معينة بسقوط عقابها دون أخرى، لأن نسبة كثرة الثواب إلى الكل على السوية. ولما بقى فرق بين التوبة المتقدمة على المعصية والمتأخرة عنها في إسقاط عقابها كسائر الطاعات التي تسقط العقوبات بكثرة ثوابها واللازم باطل للقطع بأن من تاب عن المعاصي كلها، ثم شرب الخمر لا يسقط عنه عقاب الشرب . وأما عندنا فهو بمحض عفو الله تعالى وكرمه وتوبته الصحيحة عبادة يثاب عليها تفضلاً، ولا تبطل بمعاودة الذنب، ثم إذا تاب عنه ثانياً يكون عبادة أخرى.
Then they disagreed concerning the remover of punishment. According to most Mu'tazila, it is by repentance itself, and according to some of them, by the abundance of its reward, because if it were by repentance itself, it would be removed by the repentance of the compelled (tawbat al-muljaʾ) and the repentance of the sinner when he sees the Fire. And it was refuted by denying regret (al-nadam) in the case of compulsion and by denying that it is for the ugliness (al-qubḥ) in the case of seeing the Fire. And the majority argued: if it were by the abundance of reward, then repentance from a particular sin would not be specifically tied to the removal of its punishment without another, because the relation of abundant reward to all is equal. And there would remain no difference between repentance preceding a sin and repentance coming after it in removing its punishment, like other acts of obedience that remove punishments by the abundance of their reward. But the necessary consequence is false, because it is certain that if someone repents from all sins, then drinks wine, the punishment for drinking wine is not removed from him. As for us (Ash'aris), it is by pure pardon (ʿafw) and grace (karam) of Allah the Exalted, and sound repentance is an act of worship rewarded as a favor, and it is not nullified by the recurrence of sin; then if one repents a second time, it is another act of worship.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage returns to the disagreement on the remover of punishment (musqiṭ al-ʿuqūba). The two Mu'tazila positions are reiterated: most say repentance itself, some say the abundant reward of repentance. The passage then presents a refutation of the abundant-reward view. First, it states the argument for the abundant-reward view: if repentance itself were the cause, then even the repentance of a compelled person (muljaʾ) or someone who repents upon seeing the Fire (muʿāyana) would be effective, which is problematic. The refutation denies that such regret exists in the case of compulsion and denies that it is truly for ugliness (qubḥ) in the case of witnessing the Fire. Then the majority of Mu'tazila (those who hold repentance itself) argue against the abundant-reward view: if it were the cause, then repentance from a particular sin would not be specifically tied to removing its own punishment, because the abundant reward applies equally to all sins. Also, there would be no difference between repentance before committing a sin and repentance after, but in fact there is a difference (because repentance before sin is not relevant). They give a decisive counterexample: if a person repents from all sins but then drinks wine, the punishment for drinking wine is not removed, which contradicts the claim that abundant reward removes all punishments. The Ash'ari position is then reaffirmed: it is by pure pardon (ʿafw) and grace (karam) of God. The passage also reiterates that sound repentance is an act of worship rewarded as a favor (tafaḍḍulan), and that its reward is not invalidated by later sin, and that a second repentance is a separate act of worship.

Key Terms
تفضل (tafaḍḍul)
عفو (ʿafw)
كرم (karam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view denies any value to repentance.
Correction: It affirms repentance as an act of worship rewarded as a favor, but not as a cause of forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view, what is the basis for the removal of punishment?
A) Repentance itself
B) Abundant reward of repentance
C) Pure pardon and grace of Allah
D) Both repentance and reward

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris affirm that punishment is removed solely by God's grace, while repentance is a rewarded act of worship that does not lose its reward if one sins again.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin and repent, do you truly believe that your repentance is simply an act of worship that Allah rewards out of His generosity, or do you secretly feel that your repentance 'earns' His forgiveness? How can you cultivate reliance on His pure grace rather than on your own actions?
?
If you have repeated a sin after repenting, do you feel that your previous repentance was wasted or that you have to start over? According to this lesson, each repentance is a separate act of worship. How does this change your view of Allah's mercy towards someone who struggles with a recurring sin?
?
In your daily life, are you more aware of your own efforts to repent and improve, or of Allah's unlimited grace that overlooks your shortcomings? What one practical step can you take today to shift your focus from your own repentance to Allah's pardon?
