Repentance: Regret Need Not Be Renewed
Refuting the claim that regret must be renewed each time sin is recalled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single sincere repentance is sufficient and why renewing regret upon recollection is not required.

Core Idea
A single regret completes repentance; recollection does not demand renewal.

This passage tackles an important question in Islamic theology: does remembering a past sin require one to feel regret all over again? Some scholars argued that failing to do so implies delight in sin, which would invalidate the original repentance. The author refutes this, showing that turning away without delight is possible, and thus a single repentance suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the repetition in the Arabic – possibly a scribal issue.
2
Identify the opposition's doubt
Find the doubt raised by al-Qāḍī and al-Jubbā'ī: that without renewed regret, one would be desiring the sin, invalidating repentance.
3
Understand the author's answer
Notice the author's simple denial: one may simply turn away without regret or delight. This refutes the necessary link.
The additional argument from al-Qāḍī is not answered explicitly, but the position implies it's not a sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ولا يلزم تجديد الندم كلما ذكر الذنب ولا يلزم تجديدها كلما ذكر الذنب خلافاً للقاضي والجبائي، ولا تعميمها. المعصية، لأنه قد أتى بما كلف به، وخرج عن عهدته خلافاً للقاضي منا. وأبي علي من المعتزلة . وشبهتهما أنه لو لم يندم كلما ذكرها، لكان مشتهياً لها، فرحاً بها، وذلك إبطال للندم (١) ، ورجوع إلى الإصرار. والجواب المنع، إذ ربما يضرب عنها صفحاً من غير ندم عليها، ولا اشتهاء لها وابتهاج بها ولو كان الأمر كما ذكر لزم أن لا تكون التوبة السابقة صحيحة. وقال القاضي: إنه إن لم يجدد ندماً كان ذلك معصية جديدة يجب الندم عليها، والتوبة الأولى مضت على صحتها، إذ العبادة الماضية لا ينقضها شيء بعد ثبوتها.
He said: It is not necessary to renew regret every time the sin is remembered, nor is it necessary to renew it every time the sin is remembered, contrary to al-Qāḍī and al-Jubbā'ī, nor to universalize it. Sin, because he has already performed what he was obligated to do and has discharged his responsibility, contrary to al-Qāḍī from us and Abū 'Alī from the Mu'tazila. Their doubt: that if he did not regret every time he remembered it, he would be desiring it, rejoicing in it, and that would be an invalidation of regret and a return to persistence. The answer: denial, for perhaps he turns away from it without regret for it, nor desire for it, nor delight in it. And if the matter were as they mentioned, it would entail that the previous repentance was not valid. And al-Qāḍī said: If he does not renew regret, that would be a new disobedience requiring regret, while the first repentance remains valid, for past acts of worship are not invalidated by anything after their establishment.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the view that repentance requires renewing regret every time the sin is recalled. The author begins by stating the position: it is not necessary to renew regret upon recollection. This is contrary to al-Qāḍī and al-Jubbā'ī. The author mentions the doubt of these two: if one does not regret each time, it would imply that he delights in the sin, which invalidates regret and indicates persistence. The answer is a simple denial: it is possible to turn away from the memory without delight. If their claim were necessary, then the original repentance would be invalid, which is false. Then al-Qāḍī adds a further argument: the failure to renew regret itself becomes a new sin. The author does not explicitly respond, but the implication from the earlier position is that it is not obligatory, so omission is not a sin. The lesson defends the completeness of a single sincere repentance.

إصرار
تجديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that repentance must be constantly reaffirmed every time the sin comes to mind.
Correction: The lesson clarifies that a single sincere repentance suffices; recollection does not require renewal of regret.

Checkpoint

A) Because regret itself is an act of worship that does not need repetition.
B) Because the person may simply ignore the memory without delight.
C) Because the sin is already forgiven.
D) Because al-Qāḍī and al-Jubbā'ī were mistaken.

Key Takeaway
A single sincere repentance is complete and does not require renewal of regret when the sin is remembered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific sin you have sincerely repented from but still remember. Do you secretly feel that your repentance is incomplete because you don't always feel regret when you recall it? ?
Have you ever felt guilty for not feeling guilty enough about a past sin? Al-Qadi argued that not renewing regret is itself a new sin. Have you, like that, added unnecessary guilt to yourself? How does the author's denial free you from that burden? Write down one way you will apply this freedom to your spiritual life.
?
If the memory of a past sin brings no regret, does that mean you are happy with it? The lesson says it is possible to turn away without desire or delight. Are there any sins from your past that you remember without regret? Instead of forcing regret, what can you do to ensure your heart is not attached to them? Commit to one concrete action this week.

Repentance from Some Sins: Valid Despite Persistence
Proving that repentance from some sins while persisting in others is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof and reasoning behind the validity of repenting from some sins while continuing others.

Core Idea
Partial repentance is valid; you don't need to quit all sins at once.

In this passage, the author defends the school's position that repentance from one sin is valid even if you persist in another. A strong proof is drawn from the case of a disbeliever who embraces Islam while continuing some sins. The objection of Abu Hashim and its elegant reply deepen your understanding of the nature of regret and ugliness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice how the author states the school's position, gives proof, then addresses an objection.
Pay attention to the structure: position, proof, objection, answer.
2
Identify the key arguments
Find the consensus proof (convert's repentance) and the definition of repentance. Then identify Abu Hashim's objection and the reply.
The reply distinguishes 'ugliness' from 'its ugliness'.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think how this applies to everyday life: you can repent of one sin while struggling with others. The lesson encourages continuous effort.
This supports the idea of incremental change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب أنه تصح التوبة عن بعض المعاصي مع الإصرار على بعض، خلافاً لأبي هاشم لنا الإجماع على أن الكافر إذا أسلم وتاب عن كفره، مع استدامة بعض المعاصي، صحت توبته وإسلامه ولم يعاقب إلا عقوبة تلك المعصية. وأيضاً ليست التوبة عن تلك المعاصي إلا الرجوع عنها والندم عليها، والعزم على أن يعاودها وقد وجدت وشبهة أبي هاشم الندم عليها يجب أن يكون لقبحها، وهو شامل للمعاصي كلها فلا يتحقق الندم على قبيح مع الإصرار على قبيح . وأجيب بأن الشامل للكل هو القبح لا قبحها.
The school: that repentance from some sins while persisting in others is valid, contrary to Abū Hāshim. For us, there is consensus that when a disbeliever becomes Muslim and repents from disbelief while continuing some sins, his repentance and Islam are valid, and he is punished only for that sin. Also, repentance from those sins is only the turning away from them, regret for them, and resolve not to return to them, and these exist. The doubt of Abū Hāshim: regret for them must be because of their ugliness, and ugliness is inclusive of all sins, so regret for an ugly act cannot be realized while persisting in an ugly act. And it was answered: that which is inclusive of all is ugliness, not its ugliness.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage states the position of the school (madhhab) regarding partial repentance. It affirms that repenting from some sins while persisting in others is valid, contrary to Abū Hāshim. The proof is consensus: a convert who repents from disbelief but continues some sins is considered a valid Muslim, punished only for those sins. Additionally, repentance consists of regret, turning away, and resolve—these can apply to specific sins. Abū Hāshim objects: regret must be for the ugliness (qubh) of the sin, and ugliness is universal, so one cannot regret one ugly act while committing another. The answer is that the universal element is ugliness itself (al-qubh), not 'its ugliness' (qubḥuhā) for each particular sin. Thus, one can regret a specific sin for its particular ugliness while persisting in another sin whose ugliness is not the object of regret. The lesson establishes the validity of partial repentance.

إصرار
قبح
إجماع
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is only valid if you stop all sins at once.
Correction: The consensus proof shows that a convert's repentance from disbelief is accepted even if he continues other sins, indicating that partial repentance is valid.

Checkpoint

B) The analogy between repentance and other acts of worship.
C) The statement of Abū Hāshim that ugliness is universal.
D) The claim that regret must be for all sins.

Key Takeaway
The consensus that a convert's repentance is valid demonstrates that partial repentance suffices; one need not abandon all sins simultaneously for repentance to be accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Verification: How Regret for Some Sins Is Possible
Explaining the role of motivations in specifying regret for particular sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motivations for regret can be specified to particular sins without contradicting the principle that regret is for ugliness.

Core Idea
Partial regret is possible because motivations can preponderate due to gravity or weak desire.

The author of al-Maqasid now engages in a detailed verification (tahqeeq) to resolve a potential tension: if all sins are ugly, why might one regret some but not others? Drawing on the author of al-Tajrid, he explains that while the basic motivation for regret is the same (ugliness), additional factors can tilt the balance for specific sins. This passage also distinguishes between what suffices for repentance in rights of God versus rights of servants.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the careful logical structure: the author first states the verification, then provides an analogy, then applies it to repentance conditions.
2
Understand the core verification
Identify the main claim: motivations for regret all share the basis of ugliness, but some preponderate due to additional factors (gravity of sin, weakness of desire), leading to regret for specific sins.
This resolves the apparent contradiction of regretting only some sins.
3
Examine the analogy with good acts
The author compares motivations for good acts: one can perform a good act for its goodness while omitting another because the motivation did not preponderate. This reinforces that regret for some sins does not negate its basis in ugliness.
The analogy is key to understanding the argument.
4
Note the distinction in repentance requirements
Observe how the passage divides sins: for rights of God, regret may suffice (fleeing battle, abandoning enjoining good) or need extra (drinking, missing prayer/zakat). For rights of people, restoration or apology is necessary.
This shows that the verification has practical consequences for repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق على ما ذكره صاحب التجريد هو أن الدواعي إلى الندم عن القبائح، وإن اشتركت في كون الندم على القبيح لقبحه . لكن يجوز أن تترجح بعض الدواعي بأمور تنضم إليه، كعظم المعصية أو قلة غلبة الهوى فيها فيبعثه ذلك الترجح على الندم عن البعض خاصة دون البعض الآخر، لانتفاء ترجيح الداعي بالنسبة إليه . ولا يلزم ذلك أن يكون الندم على البعض الذي تحقق منه الترجيح لاقبحه. إذ لا يخرج الداعي بهذا الترجيح عن الاشتراك في كونه داعياً إلى الندم على القبيح لقبحه. وهذا كما في الدواعي إلى الفعل لحسنه قد يترجح البعض فيخصص بعض الأفعال الحسنة بالوقوع . ولا يلزم من ترك البعض الآخر كون إيقاع هذا البعض لا لحسنه، بل لغرض غاية ما في الباب أنه حصل للداعي إلى هذا الفعل لحسنه رجحان لم يحصل للداعي إلى الفعل الآخر. وهذا ما قال أصحابنا أنه كما يجوز الإتيان بواجب لحسنه ترك واجب آخر، يجوز ترك قبيح لقبحه مع الإصرار على قبيح آخر . قال : ويكفي في الإجماع . وإن علمت الذنوب مفصلة، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة . قالوا : وفي حق الله تعالى قد يكفي الندم كما في الفرار عن الزحف، وترك الأمر بالمعروف. وقد يفتقر إلى زائد كما في الشرب، وترك الصلاة والزكاة في حق العبد، لا بد من تسليم حق العبد أو بدله إن كان الذنب ظلماً، كالغصب والقتل، ومن إرشاده إن كان إضلالاً، ومن الاعتذار إليه إن كان إيذاء كالغيبة . والتحقيق أن الزائد
And the verification, as mentioned by the author of al-Tajrīd, is that the motivations to regret ugly acts, although they share in being regret for the ugly because of its ugliness, it is possible that some motivations preponderate due to matters that join to it, like the gravity of the sin or the lack of the dominance of desire in it, so that preponderance impels him to regret some specifically without others, due to the absence of preponderance of the motivation with respect to the other. And that does not entail that the regret for the particular sin which has the preponderance is not for its ugliness. For the motivation by this preponderance does not exit from being shared in being a motivation to regret the ugly because of its ugliness. And this is like the motivations to do an act because of its goodness: some may preponderate and specify some good acts for occurrence. And the omission of others does not entail that the performance of this one is not for its goodness, but for some other purpose. The utmost in the matter is that the motivation to this act for its goodness has obtained a preponderance that did not obtain for the motivation to the other act. And this is what our companions said: just as it is permissible to perform an obligatory act for its goodness while leaving another obligatory act, it is permissible to leave an ugly act for its ugliness while persisting in another ugly act. He said: And it suffices, by consensus, even if the sins are known in detail, contrary to some Mu'tazila. They said: In the rights of God, regret may suffice, as in fleeing from battle and abandoning enjoining good. And it may need an extra, as in drinking, and abandoning prayer and zakāh. In the right of a servant, it is necessary to deliver the right or its equivalent if the sin was oppression, like usurpation and killing, and to guide him if it was misguidance, and to apologize to him if it was harm like backbiting. Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed verification (tahqeeq) of how one can regret some sins while persisting in others. The author follows the view of the author of al-Tajrid. The motivations (dawa'i) for regret are all based on the ugliness of sin, but additional factors like the gravity of the sin or weakness of desire can tip the balance for some sins, producing regret for those alone. This does not mean the regret is not for ugliness—it remains for ugliness, but the motivation for others lacks preponderance. An analogy is made with good actions: one may perform a good act for its goodness while omitting another because the motivation for the latter did not preponderate. This aligns with the school's position that one can perform one obligation and leave another. Then the author discusses what suffices in repentance: by consensus, regret alone suffices for sins against God that do not require reparation (like fleeing from battle or abandoning enjoining good), but acts like drinking, abandoning prayer, and zakāh require an extra (presumably making up missed duties). For violations against other people, restoration or compensation is necessary. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the key point is the verification that partial regret is possible without contradiction.

Correction: Regret can be specific because motivations preponderate due to additional factors, without negating that regret is for ugliness.

Checkpoint

D) The person chooses arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
Regret for some sins is valid when the motivation for regret preponderates for those sins over others, without compromising the principle that regret is for ugliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage distinguishes regret for the ugliness of sin and regret due to worldly loss. Can you honestly say your regret for your past sins is purely for God's sake, not because of consequences you suffered? How can you purify your regret to be solely for His displeasure?

Refuting Abu Hashim: Selective Regret Explained
Refutation of Abu Hashim's objection on selective regret.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how it is possible to regret some sins while persisting in others, based on differing motives.

Core Idea
Regret for some sins can coexist with persistence in others due to varying motivational strength.

Abu Hashim argued that since regret must be for ugliness itself, and ugliness is common to all sins, one cannot sincerely regret one sin while persisting in another. The author presents a refined answer: motives for regret can be given preponderance by factors like the sin's magnitude, making selective regret possible without compromising its basis in ugliness.

Guided Path
1
Understand Abu Hashim's objection
Read the objection: regret must be for the ugliness of sin, and ugliness is shared by all sins, so regret for one implies regret for all. Therefore selective repentance is impossible.
The objection is based on the principle that regret is for the attribute of ugliness, not the specific act.
2
Examine the author's initial answer
The brief reply: 'that which covers everything is ugliness, not the ugliness of it.' Notice the subtle distinction between the abstract quality of ugliness and the concrete ugliness of each sin.
3
Study the verification through motives
The author of al-Tajrid explains that although all motives to regret share the feature 'regret for ugliness', they can be given preponderance by additional factors like the sin's magnitude or weak passion. Thus one may have stronger motive to regret some sins and not others.
This is similar to why one may perform one good act while leaving another, both still done for their goodness.
4
Connect to the companions' principle
The passage ends with a statement from 'our companions': just as it is permissible to do one obligatory act while leaving another, it is permissible to leave one ugly act while persisting in another.
This shows the consistency of the verification with established Sunni doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من هذا والعزم على أن يعاودها وقد وجدت وشبهة أبي هاشم الندم عليها يجب أن يكون لقبحها، وهو شامل للمعاصي كلها فلا يتحقق الندم على قبيح مع الإصرار على قبيح . وأجيب بأن الشامل للكل هو القبح لاقبحها. والتحقيق على ما ذكره صاحب التجريد هو أن الدواعي إلى الندم عن القبائح، وإن اشتركت في كون الندم على القبيح لقبحه . لكن يجوز أن تترجح بعض الدواعي بأمور تنضم إليه، كعظم المعصية أو قلة غلبة الهوى فيها فيبعثه ذلك الترجح على الندم عن البعض خاصة دون البعض الآخر، لانتفاء ترجيح الداعي بالنسبة إليه . ولا يلزم ذلك أن يكون الندم على البعض الذي تحقق منه الترجيح ، لاقبحه. إذ لا يخرج الداعي بالنسبة إليه، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون الندم على البعض الذي تحقق منه الترجيح، لا قبحه. إذ لا يخرج الداعي بهذا الترجيح عن الاشتراك في كونه داعياً إلى الندم على القبيح لقبحه. وهذا كما في الدواعي إلى الفعل لحسنه قد يترجح البعض فيخصص بعض الأفعال الحسنة بالوقوع . ولا يلزم من ترك البعض الآخر كون إيقاع هذا البعض لا لحسنه، بل لغرض غاية ما في الباب أنه حصل للداعي إلى هذا الفعل لحسنه رجحان لم يحصل للداعي إلى الفعل الآخر. وهذا ما قال أصحابنا أنه كما يجوز الإتيان بواجب لحسنه ترك واجب آخر، يجوز ترك قبيح لقبحه مع الإصرار على قبيح آخر . قال : ويكفي في الإجماع . وإن علمت الذنوب مفصلة، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة . قالوا : وفي حق الله تعالى قد يكفي الندم كما في الفرار عن الزحف، وترك الأمر بالمعروف. وقد يفتقر إلى زائد كما في الشرب، وترك الصلاة والزكاة في حق العبد، لا بد من تسليم حق العبد أو بدله إن كان الذنب ظلماً، كالغصب والقتل، ومن إرشاده إن كان إضلالاً، ومن الاعتذار إليه إن كان إيذاء كالغيبة . والتحقيق أن الزائد واجب آخر. إلا أنه قد لا يصح الندم بدونه ، كرد المغصوب). يعني يكفي التوبة عن المعاصي كلها الإجمال، وإن علمت مفصلة لحصول الندم والعزم . وذهب بعض المعتزلة إلى أنه لا بد من الندم تفصيلا فيما علم مفصلاً . ورد بأنه مكلف بالتوبة في كل وقت مع امتناع اجتماع الذنوب الكثيرة في وقت واحد. فلو لم يكف الإجمال، لزم تكليف ما لا يطاق. قالوا : ثم إن كانت المعصية في خالص حق الله تعالى كالواجب، فقد يكفي الندم، كما في ارتكاب الفرار من الزحف وترك الأمر بالمعروف. وقد يفتقر إلى أمر زائد كتسليم النفس للحد في الشرب، وتسليم ما وجب في ترك الزكاة، ومثله في ترك الصلاة وإن تعلقت بحقوق العباد لزم مع الندم إيصال حق العبد، أو بدله إليه إن كان الذنب ظلماً كما في الغصب، والقتل العمد ولزم إرشاده إن كان الذنب إضلالاً له والاعتذار إليه إن كان إيذاء كما في الغيبة . ولا يلزم تفصيل ما اغتابه به إلا إذا بلغه على وجه أفحش . والتحقيق أن هذا الزائد واجب آخر خارج عن التوبة على ما قال إمام الحرمين أن القاتل إذا ندم من غير تسليم نفسه للقصاص، صحت توبته في حق الله تعالى، وكان منعه القصاص من مستحقه معصية متجددة تستدعي توبة. ولا يقدح في التوبة عن القتل . ثم قال : وربما لا يصح التوبة بدون الخروج من حق العبد كما في الغصب، فإنه لا يصح الندم عليه مع إدامة اليد على المغصوب ففرق بين القتل والغصب.
From this and the resolve to return to it [the sin] when it is found, and the doubt of Abu Hashim: regret for it must be because of its ugliness, and it covers all sins, so regret for an ugly act cannot be realized while persisting in another ugly act. And it was answered: that which covers everything is ugliness, not the ugliness of it. The verification according to what the author of al-Tajrid mentioned is that motives to regret ugly acts, even though they share in being regret for the ugly because of its ugliness, it is possible that some motives are given preponderance by matters that are joined to them, like the greatness of the sin or the scantiness of the dominance of passion in it, so that preponderance impels him to regret some sins specifically without others, due to the absence of preponderance of the motive with respect to the other. And it does not follow from that that the regret for the sin for which preponderance has been realized is not due to its ugliness, for the motive with respect to it does not depart [from being due to ugliness], and it does not follow from that that the regret for the sin for which preponderance has been realized is not due to its ugliness, for the motive with this preponderance does not depart from sharing in being a motive to regret the ugly because of its ugliness. This is like with motives to an act because of its goodness: some may be given preponderance, so some good acts are singled out for occurrence. And it does not follow from abandoning the others that performing this act is not due to its goodness, but for some purpose; the upshot is that there occurred for the motive to this act due to its goodness a preponderance that did not occur for the motive to the other act. And this is what our companions said: just as it is permissible to perform an obligatory act due to its goodness while leaving another obligatory act, it is permissible to leave an ugly act due to its ugliness while persisting in another ugly act. He said: And it suffices in consensus, even if sins are known in detail, contrary to some Mu'tazila. They said: And regarding the right of God Most High, regret may suffice as in fleeing from battle and abandoning commanding good. And it may require an extra [act] as in drinking [wine], and abandoning prayer and zakat; regarding the right of the servant, there must be restitution of the servant's right or its equivalent if the sin was oppression, like usurpation and killing, and guidance if it was misguidance, and apology if it was harm like backbiting. The verification is that the extra is another obligatory act, except that regret may not be valid without it, like returning the usurped property. That is, general repentance from all sins suffices, even if known in detail, because of the occurrence of regret and resolve. Some Mu'tazila held that detailed repentance is necessary for what is known in detail. And it was refuted that he is obligated to repent at all times while it is impossible for many sins to be gathered at one time. So if general repentance did not suffice, it would entail an impossible obligation. They said: Then if the sin is purely regarding the right of God Most High, like an obligation, regret may suffice, as in committing flight from battle and abandoning commanding good. And it may require an extra matter like surrendering oneself for the prescribed punishment in drinking, and surrendering what was obligatory in abandoning zakat, and similarly for abandoning prayer. And if it involves the rights of people, there must be, along with regret, delivery of the right of the servant or its equivalent to him if the sin was oppression as in usurpation and intentional killing, and there must be guidance if the sin was misguidance for him and apology if it was harm as in backbiting. And detailed mention of what he backbitten about is not necessary unless [the backbiting] reached him in an even more exaggerated manner. The verification is that this extra is another obligatory act outside of repentance, according to what Imam al-Haramayn said: that if a killer regrets without surrendering himself for retaliation, his repentance is valid regarding the right of God Most High, and his prevention of retaliation from its rightful claimant is a renewed sin requiring repentance, and it does not detract from repentance for the killing. Then he said: And perhaps repentance is not valid without exiting from the right of the servant as in usurpation, for regret for it is not valid while continuing to hold the usurped property. So he distinguished between killing and usurpation.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses a key objection raised by Abu Hashim (a Mu'tazili) regarding repentance from sins. The objection is that regret for a sin must be because of its ugliness (qabah), and since regret covers all sins equally, one cannot genuinely regret one sin while persisting in another. The author first gives a brief answer: what covers everything is ugliness itself, not the particular ugliness of each sin. Then he provides the thorough verification from the author of al-Tajrid. He explains that although all motives to regret ugly acts share the feature of being regret for ugliness, they can be given preponderance by additional factors like the magnitude of the sin or weakness of passion. Thus a person may have a stronger motive to regret certain sins and not others, without the regret for those sins becoming anything other than regret for ugliness. He draws an analogy with motives to perform good acts: one may do a good act because of its goodness while leaving another good act, without the performed act being any less good. So too, one may leave an ugly act because of its ugliness while persisting in another ugly act. Then the author turns to the consensus that general repentance (tawba ijmalia) suffices even if sins are known in detail, contrary to some Mu'tazila who demand detailed repentance. He supports this by noting that a person is obligated to repent at all times, and it is impossible to have many sins present simultaneously to regret each in detail; requiring detailed repentance would be an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq). He then discusses conditions: for sins purely against God (huquq Allah), regret may suffice, but sometimes an extra act like submitting to punishment is needed. For sins against people (huquq al-'ibad), restitution, guidance, or apology is required along with regret. He cites Imam al-Haramayn who distinguishes between killing and usurpation: in killing, regret alone suffices for God's right, but withholding retaliation is a new sin; while in usurpation, regret is impossible without actually returning the property.

Key Terms
ندم
قبح
داعي
إصرار
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person cannot truly repent from some sins while continuing in others.
Correction: The verification shows that due to varying motives, one may genuinely regret some sins for their ugliness while not yet regretting others, without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the verification, what makes it possible to regret some sins while persisting in others?
A) The sins are of different types
B) Motives for regret can be given preponderance by additional factors
C) Regret is not actually for ugliness
D) One can only regret one sin at a time

Key Takeaway
Regret for some sins while persisting in others is possible and valid because motives can vary in strength, yet remain grounded in the ugliness of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What sin in your life have you convinced yourself is 'not that bad' compared to others, allowing you to persist in it while claiming to regret bigger sins? What factor (like 'I'm not as bad as so-and-so') has given you false peace? Write down that sin and ask yourself: is my regret genuine, or just selective convenience?
?
If you were to face Allah tonight with a list of all your sins, which one would you be most ashamed of? Now: are you actively working to leave that sin? Or have you accepted it as a 'small fault'? This lesson says regret for some sins can coexist with persistence in others—are you using that as an excuse to not fully repent?
?
The passage mentions 'the scantiness of the dominance of passion' as a factor that can make regret easier. What passion (like pride, greed, lust) dominates in your life that you refuse to regret fully? Are you willing to admit that your lack of regret for that sin is a choice, not a necessity?

Unseen Matters
intermediate
10 min
Conditions of Repentance: General vs Detailed, Rights of God and People
Discussion of sufficiency of general repentance and conditions regarding rights of God and people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that general repentance suffices even for known sins, and the extra requirements when sins involve the rights of others.

Core Idea
General repentance suffices for all sins; but sins against others require restitution or apology in addition.

Having established the possibility of selective repentance, the author now addresses the scope and conditions of repentance. He affirms the Sunni consensus that general repentance (al-tawba al-ijmaliyya) is sufficient, even if the sins are known in detail, countering a Mu'tazilite view requiring detailed regret. He then distinguishes between sins purely against God and those involving the rights of others, outlining the extra acts needed for the latter.

Guided Path
1
Learn the consensus on general repentance
Read the statement: 'And it suffices in consensus' for general repentance even if sins are known in detail. Understand the proof: requiring detailed regret would be an impossible obligation since one cannot focus on many sins at once.
This is the Sunni position, opposed by some Mu'tazila who demand detailed repentance.
2
Identify the two categories of sin
The passage divides sins into those concerning the right of God (huquq Allah) and those concerning the rights of servants (huquq al-'ibad). Notice the subtypes within each.
For God's rights, regret may suffice, but sometimes an extra act like submitting to punishment is needed.
3
Understand the extra requirements for rights of others
For oppression like usurpation and killing, restitution or its equivalent is needed; for misguidance, guidance; for harm like backbiting, apology. Note the distinction between killing and usurpation made by Imam al-Haramayn.
In killing, repentance for God's right is valid even without surrendering for retaliation, but withholding retaliation is a new sin. In usurpation, repentance is invalid without returning the property.
4
Grasp the verification that the extra is a separate obligation
The author clarifies that the extra act is another obligatory act outside repentance, but sometimes repentance is not valid without it.
This preserves the meaning that repentance itself is a mental act (regret and resolve), but its conditions may include external actions.

Commentary
This passage addresses a key objection raised by Abu Hashim (a Mu'tazili) regarding repentance from sins. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance from a sin known in detail requires detailed regret for that specific sin.
Correction: The author affirms that general repentance covering all sins is sufficient, as detailed regret for many sins simultaneously is impossible.

Checkpoint
What extra act is required in repentance for sins like usurpation (ghasb)?
A) Only regret is needed
B) Restitution of the property or its equivalent
C) Surrendering for punishment
D) Detailed confession to the victim

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you committed against another person that you have never made right. Are you willing to take the concrete step of apologizing or making restitution, or have you convinced yourself that your private regret is enough?
?
The lesson shows that general repentance suffices for sins against God, but for sins against people, external action is required. Is there a relationship you have broken through gossip or backbiting that you have never personally apologized for? What is holding you back from doing so today?
?
If you died tonight, would the people you have wronged be able to say that you sought to restore their rights? What is one actionable step you can commit to this week to fulfill a right of another person that you have neglected?

The Obligation of Commanding Good and Forbidding Evil: Defining Its Scope
The author states the consensus and defines the scope of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of 'good' and 'evil' in the context of commanding good and forbidding evil, and why only wajib and haram acts are covered by the obligation.

Core Idea
Commanding good is only obligatory for wajib acts; for mandub it is recommended.

This lesson examines the consensus on the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. The author clarifies that the terms 'good' and 'evil' in this duty are specific to obligatory and forbidden acts respectively. Understanding this precision prevents confusion about what must be commanded versus what is merely recommended.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording sink in before interpreting.
The passage is concise; note the key terms.
2
Identify the key definitions
Notice how the author defines 'good' (ma'ruf) as wajib and 'evil' (munkar) as haram. This is the core doctrinal point.
3
Understand the exception
The author states that commanding mandub or forbidding makruh is not obligatory but recommended. Contrast this with the obligation for wajib and haram.
This clarifies the scope of the duty.
4
Reflect on the supporting verse
Read the Quranic verse (5:105) cited. Consider how it supports the obligation and the idea of personal responsibility.
The verse is truncated in the passage; you may look up the full verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الخامس عشر - قد أطبق الكتاب والسنة والإجماع على وجوب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. فالمراد بالمعروف الواجب، والمنكر الحرام وإلا فالأمر بالمندوب، أو النهي عن المكروه ليس بواجب، بل مندوب . وقوله تعالى: (عليكم أنفسكم لا يضركم من ضل إذا اهتديتم )
He said: The fifteenth chapter - The Book, the Sunnah, and consensus have affirmed the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil. What is meant by 'good' is the obligatory, and by 'evil' the forbidden; otherwise, commanding the recommended or forbidding the disliked is not obligatory, rather recommended. And His saying, Exalted: 'Upon yourselves; he who goes astray will not harm you if you are guided' (Quran 5:105).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage begins the fifteenth chapter (al-mabhas al-khamis 'ashar) on the topic of commanding good and forbidding evil (al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar). The author states that the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and the consensus (ijma') have all affirmed the obligation (wujub) of this duty. He then clarifies the definition: what is meant by 'good' (ma'ruf) here is specifically the obligatory (wajib), and what is meant by 'evil' (munkar) is specifically the forbidden (haram). This precision is important because if one were to command a recommended act (mandub) or forbid a disliked act (makruh), that is not obligatory but rather recommended (mandub). He then quotes the verse (Quran 5:105) which indicates that if one fulfills his duty of guiding others, the misguidance of others does not harm him, but the verse is cited to support the obligation. The passage is a concise statement of the basis and scope of this communal duty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That commanding good applies to all good deeds, including recommended acts.
Correction: The author specifies that commanding good is obligatory only for wajib acts; for mandub acts it is recommended but not obligatory.

Checkpoint

B) All obligatory acts

D) All acts

Key Takeaway
Commanding good and forbidding evil is obligatory only for wajib and haram acts; for mandub and makruh it is recommended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The verse promises that if you are guided, others' misguidance does not harm you. Do you truly believe that your duty is just to deliver the command, regardless of outcome? Or do you tie your success to people's reactions? Examine your heart: are you more concerned with being effective or being obedient?

Proofs from the Quran for Enjoining Good and Forbidding Evil
Opening statement and Quranic proofs for the obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for the obligation of commanding good and forbidding evil, and how this obligation is not conditional on the appearance of an infallible imam.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly commands enjoining good and forbidding evil for the Muslim community.

The author begins by stating that the proof for the obligation comes from the Book, Sunnah, and Consensus, and he immediately refutes the Rawafid claim that it depends on the Imam. He then cites two clear Quranic verses that establish this duty. This lesson focuses on the Quranic evidence, showing that Allah directly commands it.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first sentence which outlines the sources of proof and the refutation of the Rawafid position.
Notice that the author mentions three sources: Book, Sunnah, Ijma'.
2
Examine the two Quranic verses
Read the two verses cited: Surah Al-Imran 3:104 and Surah Luqman 31:17. Observe the phrasing and the command.
The first verse speaks about a community, the second is a direct command.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on how these verses establish a clear obligation for Muslims to enjoin good and forbid evil, without any condition of an imam's appearance.
This directly counters the Rawafid claim.

والدليل على وجوبهما من غير توقف على ظهور الإمام كما يزعم الروافض الكتاب والسنة والإجماع . أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى : ولتكن منكم أمة يدعون إلى الخير ويأمرون بالمعروف وينهون عن المنكر ) . وقوله تعالى : وأمر بالمعروف وانه عن المنكر . وأما السنة فلقوله (عليه السلام) مر بالمعروف، وانه عن المنكر، واصبر على ما أصابك» . وقوله (عليه السلام) لتأمرن بالمعروف ولتنهون عن المنكر، أو ليسلطن الله عليكم شراركم ، ثم يدعوا خياركم فلا يستجاب لكم . وقوله (عليه السلام) من رأى منكم منكراً فليغيره بيده. فإن لم يستطع، فبلسانه . فإن لم يستطع فبقلبه. وهذا أضعف الإيمان». وأما الإجماع فهو أن المسلمين في الصدر الأول وبعده كانوا يتواصون بذلك، ويوبخون تاركه مع الاقتدار عليه
And the proof for the obligation of both, without conditioning it upon the appearance of the Imam as the Rawafid claim, is the Book, the Sunnah, and the Consensus. As for the Book: His saying, the Exalted: 'Let there be from you a community inviting to good, enjoining what is right, and forbidding what is wrong' [Quran 3:104]. And as for the Sunnah: his saying, peace be upon him: 'Enjoin what is right, forbid what is wrong, and bear patiently whatever befalls you' [Hadith]. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'You must enjoin what is right and forbid what is wrong, or else Allah will set over you the worst of you, then the best of you will supplicate but will not be answered' [Hadith]. First Proof: Quran 3:85 on Accepting Only Islam as Religion
Author presents and defends the first proof using Quran 3:85, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof uses Quran 3:85 to argue for the synonymy of Iman and Islam, and how the objection is answered.

Core Idea
The verse 'only Islam accepted' proves Iman is not distinct from Islam.

This lesson examines the first well-known proof that Iman and Islam are synonymous. The proof relies on Quran 3:85, which states that only Islam is accepted as a religion. The argument shows that if Iman were different, it would not be accepted. An objection is raised and answered, clarifying the meaning of 'religion' (din) in the verse and the usage of the terms in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the flow of argument.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, response.
2
Understand the logical structure
The proof is a conditional: if Iman is other than Islam, then it would not be accepted. But it is accepted, so it is not other. The objection claims Iman might be different but not a 'religion'.
Identify the key terms: din, millah, tariqa.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that 'religion' in the verse means the established way from the Prophet, which Iman is also part of. He explains why 'religion of faith' is not common while 'religion of Islam' is.
This linguistic point strengthens the proof.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how this proof fits into the overall discussion of Iman and Islam synonymy.
This proof is one of two well-known proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمشهور من استدلال القوم وجهان : أحدهما - أن الإيمان لو كان غير الإسلام لم يقبل من مبتغيه لقوله تعالى: (ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام ديناً فلن يقبل منه ) واللازم باطل ،بالاتفاق، واعترض بأنه يجوز أن يكون غيره . لكن لا يكون ديناً غيره لكون الدين عبارة عن الطاعات على ما سبق. وقد عرفت ما فيه . بل المراد بالدين الملة والطريقة الثابتة من النبي عليه السلام والإيمان كذلك، وإن استمر في إطلاق أهل الشرع دين الإسلام ولم يسمع دين الإيمان، وذلك لاشتهار لفظ الإسلام في طريقة النبي واعتبار الإضافة إليه حتى صار بمنزلة اسم لدين محمد (عليه السلام). ولفظ الإيمان في فعل المؤمن من حيث الإضافة إليه، ولم يصر بمنزلة الاسم للدين ولهذا كثيراً ما يفتقر في الإيمان إلى ذكر المتعلق مثل : آمنوا بالله ورسوله . وغير ذلك، بخلاف الإسلام.
And the well-known from the proofs of the people are two aspects: One of them — that if faith were other than Islam, it would not be accepted from its seeker due to His saying, the Exalted: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' (3:85). And the consequent is false by consensus. And it was objected that it is possible that it be other than it, but it would not be a religion other than it because 'religion' (din) is an expression for acts of obedience, as previously. And you have known what is in that. Rather, what is meant by 'religion' is the millah (community) and the established way from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and likewise faith. And although the usage of the people of the Law continues as 'the religion of Islam,' and 'the religion of faith' is not heard, that is because the word 'Islam' is famous in the way of the Prophet and its attribution to him, until it became like a name for the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him). And the word 'faith' pertains to the act of the believer in terms of its attribution to him, and it did not become like a name for the religion; for this reason it often requires mention of the object, such as 'they believe in God and His Messenger' and so on, unlike Islam.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two well-known proofs (wajhān) used by those who argue for the synonymy of Īmān and Islām. The proof is based on Quran 3:85, which states that only Islām is accepted as a religion. The argument goes: if Īmān were different from Islām, then seeking Īmān alone would be seeking a religion other than Islām, and would thus be rejected — but the Qur'an says that anyone who seeks a religion other than Islām will not have it accepted. Since seeking Īmān is accepted (by consensus), Īmān cannot be other than Islām. An objection is raised: it is possible that Īmān is different but is not a 'religion' (dīn) because dīn here means acts of obedience, not the entire faith. The author responds by clarifying that dīn here means millah (community/faith) and the established way (ṭarīqa) from the Prophet, and Īmān is also that. He then explains why the phrase 'religion of Islam' is common but 'religion of faith' is not heard: because Islām has become a proper name for the Prophet's religion, while Īmān refers to the act of the believer and requires an object (e.g., Āmanū bi-llāh). Thus the linguistic usage does not undermine the proof.

Key Terms
الدين
الملة
الطريقة
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'only Islam accepted' means that Iman is a separate religion that is not accepted.
Correction: The verse means that any religion other than the established way from the Prophet (which includes Iman) is not accepted. Iman is part of that established way.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the first proof, and how is it answered?
A) The objection is that Iman is not a religion; the answer is that Iman is also a religion in the sense of millah.
B) The objection is that the verse is abrogated; the answer is that it is not.
C) The objection is that Islam is only outward; the answer is that Iman is inward.
D) The objection is that the verse is about disbelievers; the answer is about believers.

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses Quran 3:85 to show that Iman cannot be a separate religion because only one 'religion' (the established way of the Prophet) is accepted, and Iman is part of that way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever separated your inner faith (iman) from your outward submission (islam), treating them as independent? What would change in your daily life if you saw them as one inseparable reality – where every action is both an act of faith and submission?
?
The author says 'Islam' has become a name for the entire religion, while 'Iman' describes the believer's act. Does your faith remain merely an internal feeling, or does it manifest in concrete obedience? Commit to one specific act today that proves your iman is not just a label.
?
If you truly believed that no other 'religion' – no career, relationship, or worldly pursuit – will be accepted from you, what would you need to abandon or reprioritize? Ask Allah to make your submission exclusive to Him in every corner of your life.

Second Proof: Exception of Muslims from Believers (Quran 51:36)
Author presents second proof using Quran 51:36 with objection and partial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic exception in verse 51:36 is used as a proof for the synonymy of Īmān and Islām, and the objection that exception does not require conceptual unity.

Core Idea
Exception of Muslims from believers implies complete containment, supporting synonymy.

This lesson examines the second well-known proof for the synonymy of Īmān and Islām, based on Quran 51:36. The verse states that among the believers, only a house of Muslims was found. If Īmān and Islām were distinct, such an exception would be invalid. The author presents this argument along with an objection and part of his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse translation
Read the verse (51:36) in translation: 'So We brought out whoever was in it of the believers, and We found not therein other than a house of Muslims.' Notice the exception structure: 'Muslims' is excepted from 'believers'.
2
Understand the proof's logic
The proof argues that if Īmān and Islām were truly different, excepting Muslims from believers would be invalid, because exception requires the excepted to be a subset of the excepted-from. The verse does this, so they must be synonymous.
Exception is a linguistic tool that requires the excepted item to be part of the set from which it is excepted.
3
Examine the objection
The objection states that exception only requires coverage and inclusiveness (iḥāṭa wa shumūl), not conceptual unity. The excepted only needs to fall under the excepted-from category.
This challenges the need for synonymy; overlap might be sufficient.
4
Follow the author's partial response
The author responds by reiterating that the intended 'union' is the absence of separation (infikāk), not full identity. He then entertains an extreme objection about equality, but the passage cuts off mid-sentence.
The response is incomplete in this source chunk; further pages may complete it.
5
Reflect on the incomplete ending
Note that the passage ends abruptly. Consider how the author might complete the argument. This highlights the need for careful study of the full text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما — أنه لو كان غيره، لم يصح استثناء أحدهما من الآخر. واللازم باطل، لقوله تعالى : ( فأخرجنا من كان فيها من المؤمنين فما وجدنا فيها غير بيت من المسلمين ) أي فلم نجد ممن كان فيها من المؤمنين إلا أهل بيت من المسلمين. واعترض بأنه يكفي لصحة الاستثناء الإحاطة والشمول بحيث يدخل المستثنى تحت المستثنى منه . ولا يتوقف على اتحاد المفهوم، وقد عرفت أن المراد بالاتحاد عدم التغاير بمعنى الانفكاك، نعم لو قيل: إنه لا يتوقف على المساواة أيضاً، بل يصح مع كون المؤمن أعم كقولك: أخرجت العلماء فلم أترك إلا بعض النحاة، لكان شيئاً لا بالعكس على ما سبق إلى بعض الأوهام، ذهاباً إلى صحة قولنا : أخرجت العلماء
And the second of them — that if it were other than it, it would not be valid to except one from the other. And the consequent is false, due to His saying, the Exalted: 'So We brought out whoever was in it of the believers, and We found not therein other than a house of Muslims' (51:36), meaning: we did not find among those who were in it of the believers except the people of a house of Muslims. And it was objected that for the validity of exception, coverage and inclusiveness is sufficient, such that the excepted falls under the excepted-from. And it does not depend on unity of concept. And you have known that what is meant by 'union' is the absence of differentiation in the sense of separation. Yes, if it were said that it does not depend on equality either, but rather it is valid even if 'believer' is more general, like your saying: 'I expelled the scholars, so I left only some grammarians,' that would be something — not the reverse — as some false conceptions assume, going so far as to claim the validity of our saying: 'I expelled the scholars...'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage presents the second well-known proof for the synonymy of Īmān and Islām, based on Quran 51:36. The verse says that from among the believers, only a house of Muslims was found. If Īmān and Islām were truly different, using 'Muslims' as an exception from 'believers' would be invalid because exception requires that the excepted item be a subset of the excepted-from set. But the verse does exactly that: it excepts 'Muslims' from 'believers', implying that all Muslims are believers and vice versa. The objection is raised that exception does not require conceptual unity (iḥāṭa wa shumūl) — it only requires that the excepted be included under the excepted-from, not that they be synonymous. The author responds by reiterating that the intended 'union' is the absence of separation (infikāk), not full identity. He then entertains a further objection: even if 'believer' is more general than 'Muslim', the exception would still be valid, as in the example 'I expelled the scholars, so I left only some grammarians' (where grammarians are a subset of scholars). However, he notes that the actual verse does not work in reverse (i.e., excepting believers from Muslims would be problematic), but the objection attempts to claim it would be valid. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the main point is that the exception in the verse supports synonymy because if the two were separable, exception would be impossible.

Key Terms
الاستثناء
الإحاطة
الشمول
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think exception requires exact synonymy between the two terms.
Correction: The objection correctly notes that exception only requires the excepted to be included under the excepted-from, not synonymy. However, the author maintains that inseparability (lack of distinction) is still proven by the exception.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof's argument regarding Quran 51:36?
A) The verse proves that believers and Muslims are the same because it excepts Muslims from believers.
B) The verse shows that Muslims are better than believers.
C) The verse indicates that there were no believers except Muslims.
D) The verse uses exception incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
The exceptional relationship in Quran 51:36 supports the synonymy of Īmān and Islām because exception requires the excepted to be fully contained in the excepted-from.

First Proof for Obligation: Consensus of the Companions
Sanusi presents the consensus of the Companions as the first proof for the obligation of imamah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Companions' consensus on the need for an imam serves as the fundamental proof for the obligation of imamah.

Core Idea
The Companions' immediate consensus on appointing a leader proves imamah is a religious obligation.

The author begins with what he calls the 'mainstay' proof: the consensus of the Companions. Their actions after the Prophet's death—prioritizing the appointment of a leader over his burial—demonstrate that they considered imamah a binding duty. This lesson examines the historical event and the logical force of this consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's emphasis on 'the mainstay' and the narrative of Abu Bakr's sermon.
2
Identify the core argument
The author argues that the Companions' actions prove imamah is obligatory. Identify the two key evidences: their prioritization over burying the Prophet, and the fact that no one denied the need for an imam.
3
Analyze Abu Bakr's sermon
Abu Bakr's words distinguish between worship of the mortal Prophet and the eternal God. Then he declares 'there must be someone to undertake this matter.' This underscores the necessity of leadership for the community.
4
Reflect on the consensus
The Companions responded by agreeing to deliberate, but no one rejected the need for an imam. This unanimous acceptance is what constitutes the consensus (ijma') that makes imamah obligatory.
Note that this consensus is considered a mainstay proof in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - وهو العمدة إجماع الصحابة حتى جعلوا ذلك أهم الواجبات، واشتغلوا به عن دفن الرسول (لا) وكذا عقيب موت كل إمام . روي أنه لما توفي النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) خطب أبو بكر (رضي الله عنه) فقال: أيها الناس، من كان يعبد محمداً فإن محمداً قد مات. ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإنه حي لا يموت . لا بد لهذا الأمر ممن يقوم به، فانظروا وهاتوا آراءكم رحمكم الله . فتبادروا من كل جانب. وقالوا صدقت ولكن ننظر في هذا الأمر، ولم يقل أحد: إنه لا حاجة إلى الإمام.
The first—and it is the mainstay—is the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, such that they made that the most important of obligations, and occupied themselves with it instead of burying the Messenger (no). Likewise after the death of every imam. It is narrated that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) died, Abu Bakr (may God be pleased with him) gave a sermon and said: O people, whoever worshipped Muhammad, then Muhammad has died. And whoever worshipped the Lord of Muhammad, then He is alive and does not die. There must be for this matter someone to undertake it, so look and bring your opinions, may God have mercy on you. So they hastened from every side and said: You have spoken the truth, but let us look into this matter. And no one said that there is no need for an imam.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the obligation of appointing an imam, which is the consensus (ijma') of the Companions. The author describes this proof as 'the mainstay' (al-ʻumdah). He notes that the Companions considered appointing an imam so important that they prioritized it over burying the Prophet himself, and similarly after the death of every imam. He then narrates the historical event: after the Prophet's death, Abu Bakr addressed the people, reminding them that if they worshipped Muhammad, they must accept his mortality, but if they worshipped God, He is eternal. He then stressed the necessity of someone to lead the community. The Companions responded by agreeing to deliberate, but no one objected to the need for an imam. This unanimous acceptance by the Companions without any dissent demonstrates that the obligation of appointing an imam was a matter of consensus, thus itself a proof of its obligation.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة (consensus of Companions)
الأول - وهو العمدة (first and mainstay)
دفن الرسول (burying the Messenger)
أبو بكر (Abu Bakr)
لا بد لهذا الأمر ممن يقوم به (there must be someone to undertake this matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Companions were simply acting pragmatically, not establishing a religious obligation.
Correction: The passage shows they prioritized appointing an imam over burying the Prophet, indicating they viewed it as a duty (wajib), not just a pragmatic choice. Their consensus thus becomes a formal proof of obligation.

Checkpoint

A) 'Whoever worshipped Muhammad, Muhammad has died; whoever worshipped the Lord of Muhammad, He is alive and does not die.'
B) 'The imam is a grace from God.'
C) 'I have been appointed your leader.'
D) 'There is no need for an imam now.'

Key Takeaway
The Companions' unanimous prioritization of appointing an imam over burying the Prophet demonstrates that imamah is a religious obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions prioritized the appointment of a leader over the burial of the Prophet. What does this tell you about your own priorities? Are you more concerned with personal rituals or with ensuring that the leadership in your community is upright and legitimate? What concrete action can you take this week to strengthen the Muslim leadership you follow or to seek knowledge about the qualifications of a true imam?
?
Abu Bakr declared the necessity of a leader immediately after the Prophet's death. If your community were suddenly without a leader tomorrow, would you be prepared to identify and support a qualified candidate? What steps have you taken to understand the principles of legitimate Islamic leadership?
?
No Companion denied the need for an imam. Yet today, many Muslims are indifferent or even hostile to the concept of a unified leadership. In your heart, do you truly believe that imamah is a religious obligation, or do you treat it as an optional political matter? How will you change your attitude and actions based on the Companions' consensus?

Second Proof: Necessary Means to Absolute Commands
Second rational proof for the obligation of the imam, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the imam is obligatory as a necessary means to fulfill absolute divine commands, and the key distinction between restriction of the obligation and restriction of the obligated act.

Core Idea
The imam is obligatory as a necessary means to fulfill absolute commands.

This lesson presents the second proof for the obligation of the imamate. The Lawgiver has commanded certain duties that cannot be performed without an imam. According to a logical principle, if an absolute obligation depends on something possible, that thing becomes obligatory. The author then addresses a subtle objection by distinguishing between the obligation itself and the act commanded.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's premise
Identify what duties the Lawgiver has commanded that require an imam (hudud, guarding frontiers, etc.). Notice the phrase 'ما لا يتم إلا بالإمام'.
These are examples of collective obligations (fard kifayah) that need an imam's authority.
2
Understand the logical principle
The principle states: if an absolute obligation (wājib muṭlaq) cannot be fulfilled except through something (X), and X is possible, then X is obligatory. Apply this to the imam.
This principle was established earlier in the book.
3
Follow the objection
The objector argues: if the command to establish hudud is conditional on the imam, then it's not absolute, so it doesn't entail the imam's obligation. If not conditional, it's obvious.
The objection tries to weaken the proof by questioning the absoluteness of the command.
4
Grasp the author's response
The author distinguishes 'taqayyud al-wujūb' (restriction of the obligation) from 'taqayyud al-wājib' (restriction of the obligated act). The obligation is absolute, but the act depends on the imam—like prayer depending on purity.
This distinction saves the proof: the imam is a condition for performing the act, not for the obligation itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن الشارع أمر بإقامة الحدود ، وسد الثغور، وتجهيز الجيوش للجهاد، وكثير من الأمور المتعلقة بحفظ النظام، وحماية بيضة الإسلام، مما لا يتم إلا بالإمام، وما لا يتم الواجب المطلق إلا به وكان مقدوراً فهو واجب على ما مر في صدر الكتاب . لا يقال : الأمر بإقامة الحدود كقطع السارق مثلاً إن كان مشروطاً بوجود الإمام، لم يكن مطلقاً فلم يستلزم وجوبه ، كالأمر بالزكاة بالنسبة إلى تحصيل النصاب. وإن لم يكن مشروطاً به فظاهر لأنا نقول : فرق بين تقيد الوجوب، وتقيد الواجب، فههنا الوجوب مطلق، أي لم يقيد ولم يشترط بوجود الإمام والواجب - أعني المأمور به مشروط به و موقوف عليه. كوجوب الصلاة المشروطة بالطهارة. وأما في الزكاة فالوجوب مشروط بحصول النصاب، حتى إذا انتفى، فلا وجوب .
The second—that the Lawgiver commanded the establishment of hudud (prescribed punishments), guarding of frontiers, equipping armies for jihad, and many matters related to maintaining order and protecting the sanctuary of Islam, which cannot be accomplished except with an imam. And whatever an absolute obligation cannot be fulfilled except through something, and that thing is possible, then it is obligatory, as was mentioned in the beginning of the book. It cannot be said: The command to establish hudud, like cutting off the hand of a thief, for example, if it is conditioned upon the existence of an imam, then it is not absolute, so it does not entail his obligation, like the command to give zakat with respect to reaching the nisab. And if it is not conditioned upon it, then it is apparent. [But] we say: There is a difference between restriction of the obligation and restriction of the obligated act. Here the obligation is absolute, i.e., it is not restricted or conditioned upon the existence of the imam, while the obligated act—i.e., the commanded thing—is conditioned upon it and dependent upon it. Like the obligation of prayer conditioned upon purity. As for zakat, the obligation is conditioned upon the attainment of nisab, so if it is absent, there is no obligation.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (the second proof) for the obligation of the imamate. The argument proceeds as follows: The Lawgiver (al-shāriʻ) has commanded various duties such as establishing prescribed punishments (hudud), guarding frontiers, preparing armies for jihad, and other matters essential for public order and the protection of Islam. These duties cannot be performed except through the existence of an imam. Now, according to the principle established earlier in the book, if an absolute obligation cannot be fulfilled except through something, and that thing is possible, then that something becomes obligatory. Therefore, appointing an imam is obligatory. The author then anticipates an objection: the command to, say, cut off the hand of a thief is conditional upon the existence of an imam? If it is conditional, then the command is not absolute and does not entail the imam's obligation—similar to zakat, which is only obligatory upon reaching the nisab. If it is not conditional, then it is obvious that the command is absolute. The author responds by distinguishing between restriction of the obligation (taqayyud al-wujūb) and restriction of the obligated act (taqayyud al-wājib). In this case, the obligation to implement hudud is unconditional (absolute), but the performance of the act is dependent on the imam. For example, prayer is obligatory unconditionally, but it is conditioned on purity. In contrast, zakat's obligation is itself conditional upon reaching the nisab. Thus, the imam is a condition for the fulfillment of the obligated act, not for the obligation itself. Hence the imam becomes obligatory as a necessary means to fulfill the absolute duties commanded by the Lawgiver.

Key Terms
الشارع (Lawgiver)
إقامة الحدود (establishing hudud)
سد الثغور (guarding frontiers)
بيضة الإسلام (sanctuary of Islam)
الواجب المطلق (absolute obligation)
تقيد الوجوب (restriction of obligation)
تقيد الواجب (restriction of obligated act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the imam's obligation is conditional because the commands to establish hudud etc. are conditional upon his existence.
Correction: The commands are absolute in obligation; only the performance of the acts is conditioned on the imam. Thus the imam is a necessary means, not a condition of the obligation.

Checkpoint
How does the author distinguish the obligation of the imam from the obligation of zakat?
A) The imam's obligation is absolute, while zakat's obligation is conditional upon nisab.
B) Both are conditional upon the imam's existence.
C) The imam's obligation is conditional, but zakat's is absolute.
D) The imam is not obligatory, while zakat is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
The imam is obligatory as a necessary means to fulfill absolute commands, even though the acts themselves depend on the imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the establishment of justice, protection of the faith, and carrying out Allah's commands are your personal responsibilities, even if you cannot fulfill them alone? How are you actively supporting the leadership that makes these possible?
?
When you neglect supporting a just imam or system, are you not effectively abandoning absolute obligations that Allah has commanded? What will you change starting today to ensure you are part of the solution?
?
The lesson shows that some duties are unconditionally required even if the means are dependent on others. In your own life, have you excused yourself from a religious duty because the conditions weren't perfect? How does this distinction between obligation and its performance call you to reconsider?

Third Proof: Attraction of Benefits and Repulsion of Harms
Argues from benefits and harms that imam is obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of the imam is established through the universal rational principle of attracting benefits and repelling harms, supported by observable evidence from human societies and the animal kingdom.

Core Idea
The need for an imam is as obvious as the need for a leader in any organized group.

The author now presents the third proof for the obligation of the imam, a rational argument that appeals to universally observed facts. He begins by stating a general principle: anything that brings countless benefits and prevents clear harms is obligatory. Then he argues that appointing an imam does exactly that, and supports this with examples from everyday life and even from the behavior of bees. This passage grounds the necessity of leadership in empirical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the full English translation. Let the author's unfolding argument come through before analysis.
Notice the rhetorical force of the quotes and examples.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage contains a major premise (everything that attracts benefits and repels harms is obligatory) and a minor premise (appointing an imam does this). Locate these and see how the author defends the minor premise.
The minor premise is supported by observation and common sense.
3
Examine the scriptural and historical support
The author quotes 'what restrains the sultan is more than what restrains the Qur'an' and refers to historical tribulations when a protector is lost. Consider why these are relevant.
These show that even imperfect rulers are necessary for order.
4
Reflect on the animal example
The author uses bees as an illustration of natural leadership. Think about why this is a powerful analogy and what it implies about the universality of the need for a leader.
The bee example shows that this need is not limited to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أن في نصب الإمام استجلاب منافع لا تحصى، واستدفاع مضار لا يخفى، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو واجب، أما الصغرى فيكاد يلحق بالضروريات بل المشاهدات، ويعد من العيان الذي لا يحتاج إلى البيان . ولهذا اشتهر أن ما يزع السلطان أكثر مما يزع القرآن (وما يلتئم باللسان لا ينتظم بالبرهان).. وذلك لأن الاجتماع المؤدي إلى صلاح المعاش والمعاد لا يتم بدون سلطان قاهر يدرأ المفاسد ويحفظ المصالح ويمنع ما يتسارع إليه الطباع ويتنازع عليه الأطماع . وكفاك شاهداً ما يشاهد من استيلاء الفتن والابتلاء بالمحن لمجرد هلاك من يقوم بحماية الحوزة ورعاية البيضة، وإن لم يكن على ما ينبغي من الصلاح والسداد، ولم يخل عن شائبة شر وفساد ، ولهذا لا ينتظم أمر أدنى اجتماع كرفقة طريق بدون رئيس يصدرون عن رأيه ومقتضى أمره ونهيه . بل ربما يجري مثل هذا فيما بين الحيوانات العجم ، كالنحل لها عظيم يقوم مقام الرئيس ينتظم أمرها به ما دام فيها ، وإذا هلك انتشرت الأفراد انتشار الجراد وشاع فيما بينها الهلاك والفساد .
The third—that in appointing the imam there is attraction of innumerable benefits and repulsion of manifest harms, and everything that is such is obligatory. As for the minor premise, it is almost among necessities, indeed observations, and is counted among things evident that need no clarification. Hence it is famous that what restrains the sultan is more than what restrains the Qur'an (and what is united by the tongue is not ordered by the proof). This is because social cohesion leading to welfare in worldly life and the afterlife cannot be achieved without a coercing sultan who repels corruptions, preserves benefits, and prevents what natures rush to and ambitions contend over. Sufficient for you as a witness is what is observed of the prevalence of tribulations and affliction with calamities merely upon the destruction of one who undertakes protection of the domain and guardianship of the sanctuary, even if he was not as he should be in righteousness and rectitude, and was not free from a taint of evil and corruption. For this reason, the affair of the smallest gathering, like a company on a road, is not ordered without a leader from whose opinion and according to whose command and prohibition they take. Indeed, such may also occur among dumb animals, like bees—they have a great one who stands in the place of a leader; their affair is ordered by him as long as he is among them, and when he perishes, the individuals scatter like locusts and destruction and corruption spread among them.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the obligation of the imam, based on the principle that anything that attracts countless benefits and repels obvious harms is obligatory. The author asserts that the minor premise—that appointing an imam does bring such benefits and prevent harms—is so obvious that it is practically a necessity and a matter of direct observation, requiring no proof. He then quotes a famous saying: 'What restrains the sultan is more than what restrains the Qur'an,' meaning that a ruler's authority is more effective in preventing disorder than religious texts alone (since the latter require enforcement). Another saying follows: 'What is united by the tongue is not ordered by the proof'—verbal agreement alone does not ensure order without authority. The reasoning is that any society aiming at both worldly and religious welfare requires a coercive leader to repel evil and preserve good, given human tendencies to greed and conflict. The author points to historical evidence: when a protector is lost, even if imperfect, tribulations ensue. He then gives examples: even a small travelling party needs a leader; even bees have a queen (ʻaẓīm) that orders their affairs—upon her death, they scatter and perish. This empirical evidence from human and animal societies reinforces the rational necessity of a leader, thus making the imam obligatory.

Key Terms
نصب الإمام
استجلاب منافع
استدفاع مضار
سلطان قاهر
ما يزع السلطان أكثر مما يزع القرآن
الحيوانات العجم
النحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation of the imam is based solely on scriptural texts.
Correction: This proof shows it is also a rational necessity derived from observable benefits and harms, even found in animal societies.

Checkpoint
What example from the animal kingdom does the author give to illustrate the necessity of a leader?
A) Lions with a pride leader
B) Bees with a queen
C) Birds with a flock leader
D) Fish with a school leader

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that the imamate is obligatory through the rational principle of attracting benefits and repelling harms, supported by observable evidence from society and nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you experienced chaos due to lack of leadership in a group (family, work, community). How did that feel? Now, consider the imam as the leader of the Muslim community. Do you personally rely on the existence of a just imam for your spiritual and worldly order, or have you taken this necessity for granted? What will you do to strengthen your attachment to the concept of imamate?
?
The passage says that even an imperfect ruler prevents greater harm. Are you quick to criticize leaders in your life without acknowledging the benefit their presence provides? When you point out flaws in an imam or scholar, do you balance it with gratitude for the order they maintain? How can you cultivate a heart that appreciates leadership before criticizing it?
?
The analogy of bees shows that animals thrive under a leader and fall into destruction without one. Observe your own life: where have you resisted submitting to rightful authority (e.g., parents, teachers, scholars, imam)? What harm has your resistance caused? What would it look like for you to sincerely affirm the necessity of leadership in the Muslim community and align yourself with it?
