Refuting the Objection on the 30-Year Caliphate Hadith
Response to objection using distinction of voluntary vs. compelled abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the obligation of imamate against an objection based on the 30-year caliphate hadith, and why the community is not in error due to inability.

Core Idea
Error only follows if abandonment is voluntary; inability excuses the community.

The hadith stating that the caliphate lasts thirty years after the Prophet seems to imply that later rulers are not legitimate imams. This raises an objection: if the community has abandoned the duty of having an imam, they would be in sin. In this passage, the author responds by distinguishing between voluntary and compelled abandonment, and he also questions the certainty of the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Begin by reading the opening lines of the passage where the author presents the objection based on the hadith about the 30-year caliphate. Then notice the author's first response: 'قلنا'.
The objection assumes that later rulers are not true imams.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the phrase 'لوتركوه عن قدرة واختيار لا عجز واضطرار'. This is the core of the author's rebuttal: sin only occurs when one has ability and free choice.
The author introduces a condition that protects the community from blame.
3
Examine the status of the hadith
Note that the author mentions the hadith is solitary (ahad). This means it does not provide certain knowledge, so it can be interpreted flexibly.
The author uses this to weaken the force of the objection.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author preserves the obligation of imamate while avoiding the charge that the community fell into sin. The community is excused due to inability, and the hadith is not definitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العباسية، ولقوله : «الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة، ثم تصير ملكاً عضوضاً وقد تم ذلك بخلافة علي رضي الله تعالى عنه) فمعاوية ومن بعده ملوك وأمراء لا أئمة ولا خلفاء. واللازم منتف لأن ترك الواجب معصية وضلالة والأمة لا تجتمع على الضلالة . الكمال. العامة قلنا: إنما يلزم الضلالة لوتركوه عن قدرة واختيار لا عجز و اضطرار، والحديث مع أنه من باب الآحاد يحتمل الصرف إلى الخلافة على وجه وههنا بحث آخر، وهو أنه إذا لم يوجد إمام على شرائطه ، وبايع طائفة من أهل الحل والعقد قرشياً فيه بعض الشرائط ، من غير نفاذ الأحكامه، وطاعة من لأوامره وشوكة بها يتصرف في مصالح العباد، ويقدر على النصب والعزل لمن أراد ، هل يكون ذلك إتياناً بالواجب؟ وهل يجب على ذوي الشوكة العظيمة من ملوك الأطراف المتصفين بحسن السياسة والعدل والإنصاف أن يفوضوا الأمر إليه بالكلية ويكونوا لديه كسائر الرعية؟ وقد يتمسك بمثل قوله تعالى : أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولى الأمر منكم . وقوله ( صلى الله عليه وسلم ) : من مات ولم يعرف إمام زمانه مات ميتة جاهلية». فإن وجوب الطاعة والمعرفة يقتضي وجوب الحصول، وإما أنه لا يجب علينا عقلاً، ولا على الله أصلاً فلما مر من بطلان الأصلين.
The Abbasids? And for his saying: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years, then it becomes a biting kingship.' And that has been completed with the caliphate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). So Muawiyah and those after him are kings and rulers, not imams nor caliphs. And the necessary consequence is negated because abandoning the duty is sin and error, and the community does not agree on error. Al-Kamal. Al-Amma. We said: Error would only follow if they abandoned it out of ability and choice, not out of inability and compulsion. The hadith, even though it is solitary (ahad), can be interpreted as referring to the caliphate in a certain way. And here there is another inquiry: if there is no imam with his conditions, and a group of those who loose and bind pledge allegiance to a Qurayshite who has some of the conditions, without enforcement of his judgments and obedience to his commands and the power by which he manages the interests of the people and is able to appoint and dismiss whomever he wishes, is that a fulfillment of the duty? And is it incumbent upon those of great power among the kings of the regions who are characterized by good politics, justice, and fairness to delegate the entire affair to him and be like the rest of the subjects under him? And one may hold to such verses as 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' and his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever dies without knowing the imam of his time dies the death of ignorance.' For the obligation of obedience and knowledge necessitates the obligation of obtaining (the imam). But it is not obligatory upon us rationally, nor upon Allah at all, as has preceded in the invalidity of the two principles.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection concerning the hadith about the caliphate lasting thirty years. The objector claims that because the hadith says the caliphate after thirty years becomes kingship, and since after Ali the rulers were not true imams, the community would be in sin for abandoning the duty of having an imam. But the objector argues that the necessary consequence (that the community is in error) is negated because abandoning a duty is sin and the community does not agree on sin. The author responds (قلنا) that error would only follow if they abandoned the duty voluntarily and with ability, not if they were unable or compelled. The hadith, being a solitary narration (ahad), can be interpreted in a way that it refers to the caliphate in a specific sense. Then the author raises a further inquiry (بحث آخر): if there is no fully qualified imam, and a group of the people who loose and bind (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) pledge allegiance to a Qurayshite who possesses some but not all conditions, without full enforcement of judgments or obedience or power, does this count as fulfilling the duty? And is it obligatory for the powerful kings in the regions to submit entirely to him? The author then mentions that one might hold to the verse of obedience and the hadith about knowing the imam of one's time as proof that the obligation to have an imam is necessary. However, the author concludes that the obligation is not rational, nor obligatory upon God, as was established earlier when invalidating the two principles (i.e., the principle of rational obligation and the principle of obligation upon God). Thus, the author refutes the objection while leaving room for a practical solution.

Key Terms
اللازم
الضلالة
الحديث الآحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith of 30-year caliphate proves that there is no legitimate imam after Ali, and therefore the community has no obligation to install one.
Correction: The author clarifies that the hadith does not obligate sin on the community because they acted under inability, and the hadith itself is not certain.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when does abandoning the duty of having an imam lead to error?
A) If abandoned out of inability
B) If abandoned voluntarily with ability
C) If the community agrees on it
D) If the imam is not appointed by ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd

Key Takeaway
The community's failure to establish a fully qualified imam does not constitute sin if they lacked the ability, and the hadith cited is not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you excused your own failure to uphold an Islamic duty by claiming you were unable, when in truth you had the ability? Be honest—was your inability genuine, or a comfortable excuse? Today, identify one such area and commit to verifying whether you truly lack power, and if not, take one concrete step to begin fulfilling that duty.
?
This lesson teaches that the community is not sinful if they lack power. But what about when you have the power to speak against injustice but remain silent out of fear? Does this lesson give you comfort or convict you? Before this week ends, identify one situation where you can truthfully object to something wrong, and do it, trusting that ability brings responsibility.
?
Think of a person or group you have judged for not fulfilling an Islamic obligation. Did you consider whether they had the ability? How does this lesson change your view? As an act of accountability, reach out to that person or group this week, not to judge, but to understand their situation, and if they were unable, ask forgiveness from Allah for your judgment.

The Case of a Partially Qualified Imam and the Obligation of Submission
Raises a further inquiry about a partially qualified imam and concludes there is no obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scenario of a partially qualified imam and whether powerful rulers are obliged to submit to him, and the ultimate conclusion that the obligation is not rational nor upon God.

Core Idea
In the absence of a fully qualified imam, a lesser qualified one may suffice, but there is no rational or theological obligation to establish him.

After resolving the objection about the hadith, the author raises a practical scenario: if no fully qualified imam exists, but a group of elects a Qurayshite who has some but not all conditions, does this fulfill the duty? And are powerful kings obliged to hand over authority to him? The author concludes that there is no rational or divine obligation to do so.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Read the description of the partially qualified imam: a Qurayshite with some conditions but lacking full power and obedience.
2
Consider the two questions posed
Identify the two questions the author asks: does this appointment fulfill the duty? Are powerful kings required to submit entirely?
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
Look at the verse and hadith cited that seem to demand obedience and knowledge of the imam.
These proofs are used by opponents to argue for obligation.
4
Grasp the author's final verdict
Note the author's conclusion that the obligation is not rational nor upon God, referencing the invalidation of two principles.
This leaves the community free to manage with multiple just rulers.

?

?

?
The powerful kings in the passage are described as just and fair. Examine your own use of authority: do you delegate to others out of a sense of obligation to a theoretical ideal, or out of genuine benefit and justice? How can you apply the lesson's conclusion to free yourself from unnecessary obligations?

4 min
The argument that the imam is not obligatory upon God
Argument from necessary consequence of a manifest imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from necessary consequence used to deny that the imam is obligatory upon God.

Core Idea
If the imam were obligatory, every age would have a perfect imam; it doesn't, so it's not.

This lesson presents a key argument from those who deny that having an imam is obligatory upon God. The argument is a simple conditional: if it were obligatory, then every period of history would have a manifest, qualified imam. Since that is clearly not the case, the premise must be false. This sets the stage for the debate that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement (if...then...) and a denial of the consequent.
The argument is a form of modus tollens.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise is the conditional: if obligatory, then a manifest imam always exists. The second premise is the negation of the consequent: that such an imam does not exist in every age.
3
Understand the conclusion
The logical conclusion is that the imam is not obligatory upon God. This follows from the two premises.
The author calls the consequent 'clearly non-existent'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على عدم وجوبه على الله تعالى. أن الوجوب على الله في الجملة مذهبهم ، بأنه لو وجب على الله تعالى ، لما خلا زمان من الأزمنة من إمام ظاهر، قاهر جامع لشروط الإمامة قاطع لرسوم الضلالة، قائم بحماية بيضة الإسلام وإقامة الحدود وتنفيذ الأحكام. واللازم ظاهر الانتفاء. قال : احتج القائلون بوجوبه على الله بأنه لطف محصل للمعرفة مقرب من الطاعة، مبعد عن المعصية. ورد يمنع مقدمتي القياس ، كيف وفيه مفاسد تنشأ من اختلاف الآراء وميلها إلى الأباء عن امتثال الأكفاء. وأيضاً فعل الطاعة وترك المعصية مع عدم الإمام أشق، وأقرب الى الإخلاص. وأيضاً لا يصير لطفاً، بل خلقهم معصومين ألطف، والقول بأنه منفعة خالصة ولطف لا يحصل بالغير. وأيضاً اللطف في ظهوره وأنهم لا يحبونه . فإن قيل : مجرد الوجود لطف زاجر لخوف الظهور ، وتصرفه الظاهر لطف آخر فوته العباد بسوء اختيارهم ، حيث أضاعوه أخافوه وتركوا نصرته . قلنا فيكفي احتمال الوجود والحكم بأنه يوجد ولو بعد حين، فإن الخوف من وجود مرتب بمنزلة الخوف من ظهور مترقب، وينبغي أن يظهر للأولياء الذين قضوا في محبته وانتظاره الأعمار ، وبذلوا المهج والأموال، ونحن نقطع بانتفاء
And they argued for the non-obligation upon God Almighty: that obligation upon God is their doctrine in general, that if it were obligatory upon God, no age would be devoid of a manifest, overpowering imam, comprehensive of the conditions of imamate, cutting off the customs of error, upholding the protection of the citadel of Islam, establishing the hadd punishments, and executing the judgments. And the necessary consequence is clearly non-existent. He said: Those who affirm its obligation argued that it is a grace (lutf) attaining knowledge, bringing near to obedience, and distancing from disobedience. And it was rebutted by preventing the two premises of the syllogism. How so? For in it are corruptions arising from difference of opinions and their inclination to refuse compliance with equals. Also, performing obedience and abandoning disobedience without an imam is more difficult and closer to sincerity. Also, it does not become a grace; rather, creating them infallible is more graceful. And the statement that it is a pure benefit and a grace that is not obtained by other than him. Also, the grace is in its appearance, and they do not love him. If it is said: mere existence is a grace deterring by fear of appearance, and his manifest administration is another grace that the servants forfeited by their bad choice, when they neglected him, feared him, and abandoned his support. We said: The mere probability of existence suffices, and the judgment that it exists even if after a while; for fear of an expected existence is like fear of an anticipated appearance. And it should appear to the saints who spent their lives in his love and expectation, and sacrificed lives and wealth. And we are certain of its non-existence.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments regarding whether having an imam is obligatory upon God. First, the opponents argue that if it were obligatory, then every age would have a perfect imam, which is clearly not the case. This shows it is not obligatory. Then, the proponents argue that the imam is a grace (lutf) from God that leads to knowledge and obedience. The author refutes this by preventing the premises of the analogical argument, pointing out that such an imam could cause corruption due to differences of opinion and pride. Moreover, performing acts without an imam is more difficult and thus more sincere. Additionally, creating humans infallible would be an even greater grace, showing that the imam is not the only possible grace. The objector then raises a point: even the mere existence of an imam, even if hidden, is a deterrent grace, and his manifest governance is another grace that people lost by their own bad choices. The author replies that mere probability of existence suffices, and the fear of a possible imam is like fear of an anticipated one. But ultimately, the author declares that we are certain of the non-existence of such an imam (likely referring to a hidden imam), cutting off the argument.

Key Terms
الوجوب على الله
إمام ظاهر
شروط الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the necessary consequence that the author claims is clearly non-existent?
A) That no imam exists at all
B) That every age has a manifest, qualified imam
C) That the imam is from the Quraysh
D) That the imam must be infallible

Key Takeaway
The non-existence of a manifest, perfect imam in every age disproves the claim that such an imam is obligatory upon God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this argument is correct, what does it imply about your expectations of religious leadership? Are you demanding an impossible standard from leaders? How does that affect your attachment to the ideal of a perfect leader?
?
The argument relies on observable reality to deny a theological claim. Have you ever assumed something must exist simply because you think God should provide it? How does this argument challenge that assumption?
?
If the imam is not obligatory on God, what responsibility falls on the Muslim community instead? Are you personally fulfilling that responsibility, or are you waiting for a perfect leader who may never come?

The lutf argument and its rebuttals
The lutf argument and four rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lutf (grace) argument for the obligation of the imam and the multiple rebuttals offered against it.

Core Idea
The imam as grace is refuted by corruption, sincerity, infallibility, and dislike.

Proponents of the imam's obligation argue that an imam is a divine grace (lutf) that brings people closer to obedience and knowledge. The author responds with a series of rebuttals, challenging the premises of this argument and pointing out that an imam can cause harm, that obedience without an imam is more sincere, that creating infallible humans would be a greater grace, and that people often dislike the imam. These points aim to undermine the lutf argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the lutf argument
First, read the proponents' claim that the imam is a grace that leads to knowledge, obedience, and distance from sin.
2
Identify the four rebuttals
The author gives four distinct objections. Find each one and understand its logic.
They are: corruption, sincerity, infallibility, and dislike.
3
Evaluate the strength of each rebuttal
Consider whether each rebuttal successfully counters the lutf argument. Which is most effective?
This is an exercise in critical thinking.

Source Text

This passage presents a series of arguments regarding whether having an imam is obligatory upon God. First, the opponents argue that if it were obligatory, then every age would have a perfect imam, which is clearly not the case. This shows it is not obligatory. Then, the proponents argue that the imam is a grace (lutf) from God that leads to knowledge and obedience. The author refutes this by preventing the premises of the analogical argument, pointing out that such an imam could cause corruption due to differences of opinion and pride. Moreover, performing acts without an imam is more difficult and thus more sincere. Additionally, creating humans infallible would be an even greater grace, showing that the imam is not the only possible grace. The objector then raises a point: even the mere existence of an imam, even if hidden, is a deterrent grace, and his manifest governance is another grace that people lost by their own bad choices. The author replies that mere probability of existence suffices, and the fear of a possible imam is like fear of an anticipated one. But ultimately, the author declares that we are certain of the non-existence of such an imam (likely referring to a hidden imam), cutting off the argument.

Key Terms
لطف
مقدمتا القياس
فساد الآراء
خلق معصومين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of an imam is always a grace that benefits people.
Correction: The author argues that an imam can cause corruption, that obedience without him is more sincere, that creating infallible humans would be a greater grace, and that people may dislike the imam, undermining the claim of grace.

Checkpoint

A) The imam is always just
B) People dislike the imam
C) The imam is from the Quraysh
D) The imam is infallible

Key Takeaway
The lutf argument is refuted by four objections: corruption, sincerity, infallibility, and dislike, showing that the imam is not necessarily a divine grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult command from Allah, do you secretly wish you had a flawless leader to remove the struggle, forgetting that the hardship itself purifies your sincerity?
?
The source argues that obedience without an imam is more difficult and closer to sincerity. Have you ever used the absence of a perfect role model as an excuse to lower your standard of worship, rather than letting the difficulty refine your devotion to Allah alone?
?
The rebuff warns that an imam can cause corruption through difference of opinions. Where in your own heart do you cling to leaders or figures, knowing their fallibility, yet you still follow them blindly, compromising your obedience to the truth?

Objection and response: The hidden imam as grace
Objection about hidden imam and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that even a hidden imam's existence is a grace and the author's response that probability suffices but non-existence is certain.

Core Idea
The mere possibility of a hidden imam is not enough; we are certain of his non-existence.

The debate continues with an objection: even if the imam is not manifest, his mere existence (even hidden) serves as a deterrent grace, and his manifest actions are another grace lost by people's bad choices. The author responds by saying that the probability of existence is enough to create fear, but ultimately he asserts certainty that such an imam does not exist. This lesson analyzes the objection and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim: the hidden imam's existence is a grace because fear of his appearance deters sin. Also, his manifest governance would be another grace, but people forfeited it.
2
Analyze the response
The author gives two parts: (1) probability of existence suffices for deterrence; (2) we are certain of non-existence.
The final sentence is cut off but implies certainty.
3
Consider the implications
Why does the author assert certainty? What does this say about the overall argument?
This concludes the section on obligation.

And they argued for the non-obligation upon God Almighty: that obligation upon God is their doctrine in general, that if it were obligatory upon God, no age would be devoid of a manifest, overpowering imam, comprehensive of the conditions of imamate, cutting off the customs of error, upholding the protection of the citadel of Islam, establishing the hadd punishments, and executing the judgments. And the necessary consequence is clearly non-existent. He said: Those who affirm its obligation argued that it is a grace (lutf) attaining knowledge, bringing near to obedience, and distancing from disobedience. And it was rebutted by preventing the two premises of the syllogism. How so? For in it are corruptions arising from difference of opinions and their inclination to refuse compliance with equals. Also, performing obedience and abandoning disobedience without an imam is more difficult and closer to sincerity. Also, it does not become a grace; rather, creating them infallible is more graceful. And the statement that it is a pure benefit and a grace that is not obtained by other than him. Also, the grace is in its appearance, and they do not love him. If it is said: mere existence is a grace deterring by fear of appearance, and his manifest administration is another grace that the servants forfeited by their bad choice, when they neglected him, feared him, and abandoned his support. We said: The mere probability of existence suffices, and the judgment that it exists even if after a while; for fear of an expected existence is like fear of an anticipated appearance. And it should appear to the saints who spent their lives in his love and expectation, and sacrificed lives and wealth. And we are certain of its non-existence.

This passage presents a series of arguments regarding whether having an imam is obligatory upon God. Two Objections to the Imam as Lutf and Their Answers
Defense of the imam's existence as a lutf through responses to two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam defends the doctrine that the imam's existence, even without active rule, serves as a deterrent grace (lutf) through rational analogies.

Core Idea
The imam deters evil even when hidden, like a feared governor unknown to the villagers.

In this passage, the author responds to two objections that challenge the idea that the imam's existence is a grace (lutf). The first objection argues that existence without action cannot deter; the second asks why the imam does not appear to his loyal supporters. The author uses vivid analogies and common-sense reasoning to uphold the necessity of the imam's unseen deterrent role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the flow of objections and answers.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two main objections: (1) existence without action is not a grace, (2) the imam should appear to his supporters. See how each is introduced with 'فإن قيل'.
3
Study the first answer
Focus on the analogy of the hidden governor. Understand how fear of a potential authority deters evil just as fear of a hidden imam would.
The author distinguishes fear of something existent but hidden from fear of the non-existent.
4
Study the second answer
Examine the claim that the imam's non-appearance is a constant matter, like the impossibility of a sea of musk. Consider the counter-argument and its resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر، وإنما عدم من جهة العباد وسوء اختيارهم حيث أخافوه وتركوا نصرته ففوتوا اللطف على أنفسهم. ورد أولاً بأنا لا نسلم أن وجوده بدون التصرف لطف. فإن قيل : لأن المكلف إذا اعتقد وجوده كان دائماً يخاف ظهوره وتصرفه فيمتنع من القبائح . قلنا : مجرد الحكم بخلقه وإيجاده في وقت ما كاف في هذا المعنى ، فإن ساكن القرية إذا انزجر عن القبيح خوفاً من حاكم من قبل السلطان مختف في القرية، بحيث لا أثر له ، كذلك ينزجر خوفاً من حاكم علم أن السلطان يرسله إليها البتة متى شاء. وليس هذا خوفاً من المعدوم، بل من موجود مترقب، كما أن خوف الأول من ظهور مترقب، وثانياً بأنه ينبغي أن يظهر لأوليائه الذين يبذلون الأرواح والأموال على محبته، وليس عندهم منه إلا مجرد الاسم. فإن قيل : لعله ظهر لهم وأنتم عنه غافلون . قلنا: عدم ظهوره لهم من العاديات التي لا ارتياب فيها لعاقل . كعدم بحر من المسك وجبل من الياقوت، ولو سلم فالأولياء إذا عرفوا من أنفسهم أنه لم يظهر لهم توجه الإشكال عليهم .
Finally, and its non-existence is due to the servants and their bad choice, because they feared him and abandoned his support, thus they lost the grace for themselves. And it is responded firstly that we do not accept that his existence without action is a grace. For it is said: Because the responsible person, if he believes in his existence, will always fear his appearance and his action, and thus refrain from evil deeds. We say: Mere judgment of his creation and bringing into existence at some time is sufficient for this meaning. For the inhabitant of a village, if he is deterred from evil out of fear of a governor sent by the sultan hiding in the village with no trace, he is similarly deterred out of fear of a governor whom he knows the sultan will definitely send whenever he wishes. And this is not fear of something non-existent, but of something existent expected, just as the first fear is of an expected appearance. And secondly, he should appear to his friends who sacrifice their lives and wealth for his love, while they have nothing from him except the mere name. Then it is said: Perhaps he appeared to them while you are unaware of him. We say: His not appearing to them is among the constant matters about which no reasonable person has doubt. Like the non-existence of a sea of musk and a mountain of rubies. And even if it is conceded, then the friends, if they know from themselves that he has not appeared to them, the problem applies to them.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a sequence of objection-and-answer pairs defending the doctrine that the existence of an imam (even if not actively ruling) is a divine grace (lutf) that deters people from evil. The author begins with an objector's point: the imam's non-existence is due to people's bad choices—they feared him and failed to support him, thus losing the grace. The first response is a direct refusal: we do not accept that mere existence without action is a grace. Then a new objection is raised: if a responsible person believes the imam exists, he will constantly fear his appearance and action, and thus avoid evil. The answer uses a vivid analogy: a villager who knows a hidden governor appointed by the sultan will be deterred from evil out of fear of the governor's potential action, even if the governor has no current trace. This fear is of something existent and expected, not non-existent. Then the author adds a second point: the imam should appear to his loyal supporters who sacrifice everything for him, yet they have only his name. The next objection counters: perhaps he did appear to them, but you are unaware. The answer flatly denies this, comparing his non-appearance to the impossibility of a sea of musk or a mountain of rubies existing unnoticed. Even if conceded, the supporters themselves would recognize he had not appeared, and the objection would then apply to them. The argument uses rational analogies and common sense to uphold the necessity of the imam's existence as a deterrent grace.

لطف
المكلف
القبيح
الأولياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imam's existence must involve direct action to be a grace.
Correction: The analogy shows that mere potential deterrence from the expectation of his appearance suffices, even without visible action.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to show that fear of a hidden authority deters evil?
A) A king who openly rules
B) A village inhabitant fearing a hidden governor sent by the sultan
C) A prisoner fearing the warden
D) A student fearing a strict teacher

Key Takeaway
The imam's existence deters evil even without visible action, as shown by rational analogies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the imam's existence is a grace meant to deter you from sin, have you been using that grace or ignoring it? What concrete step can you take today to let the awareness of his existence guide your choices?

Refuting the Fitna Objection to Imamate Obligation
Presentation and refutation of the Khawarij claim that appointing an imam causes fitna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical theologians refuted the argument that appointing an imam necessarily leads to civil strife, and why the obligation remains on the community.

Core Idea
The harm of not having an imam far outweighs the potential harm of appointing one.

The Khawarij and others argued that appointing an imam invites conflict because people have divergent opinions and desires, so it cannot be obligatory—it should even be impermissible. In this passage, the author presents and refutes that claim. The refutation shows that mechanisms like preferring the most learned and the pledge of allegiance prevent strife, and more importantly, the fitna of dispute is negligible compared to the anarchy of having no imam. We will explore the argument, its exception, and the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how the Khawarij argument is presented and then answered.
Notice the careful logical structure: opponent's claim, exception, refutation, and clarification.
2
Identify the Khawarij argument
Find the lines where the Khawarij claim that appointing an imam causes fitna. Understand their reasoning: conflicting inclinations and opinions lead to civil strife, so it cannot be obligatory.
The argument uses the principle that whatever causes fitna is not obligatory, and indeed should be impermissible.
3
Study the exception they concede
Notice the exception: they say it could be permissible if there is unanimous agreement, or one uniquely qualified person, or someone clearly preponderant. This shows they do not fully reject appointment, just its obligation.
This exception is important because it weakens their absolute denial.
4
Analyze the refutation
The answer gives two main points: first, criteria for preponderance (most learned, then most pious, then oldest) and that a pledge of allegiance by even one person blocks opposition. Second, the fitna of appointment is negligible compared to the far greater evils of having no imam.
This comparative harm argument is the core of the refutation.
5
Grasp the scope of obligation
The passage ends by clarifying that the debate is about obligation on the community, not on God, the Prophet, or the previous imam. Understand why this distinction matters.
This prevents misunderstanding that the refutation rejects all forms of imamate obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : احتجت الخوارج القائلون بعدم وجوب نصب الإمام احتجوا بأن في نصبه إثارة الفتنة، لأن الأهواء متخالفة والآراء متباينة، فيميل كل حزب إلى واحد ، وتهيج الفتن وتقوم الحروب. وما هذا شأنه لا يجب بل كان ينبغي أن لا يجوز إلا ان احتمال الاتفاق على الواحد أو تعينه وتفرده باستجماع الشرائط أو ترجحه من بعض الجهات منع الامتناع، وأوجب الجواز. والجواب أن اعتبار الترجح كما قيل يقدم الأعلم ، ثم الأورع ، ثم الأسن، أو انعقاد الأمر، وانسداد طريق المخالفة بمجرد بيعة البعض ولو واحداً يدفع الفتنة، مع أن فتنة النزاع في تعيين الإمام بالنسبة إلى مفاسد عدم الإمام ملحقة بالعدم . لا يقال : الاحتجاج المذكور على تقدير تمامه لا ينفي الوجوب على الله، ولا على النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) بالنص ولا على الإمام السابق بالاستخلاف، لأنا نقول : المقصود نفي ما يراه الجمهور من الوجوب على العباد إذا لم ينصب النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ولم يستخلف الإمام السابق
He said: The Khawarij argued that those who deny the obligation of appointing an imam argued that in his appointment there is incitement of fitna, because inclinations are conflicting and opinions are divergent, so each party inclines to one, and fitnas are stirred and wars arise. And what is of this sort is not obligatory, rather it should not be permissible, except that the possibility of agreement upon one, or his designation and uniqueness in possessing all conditions, or his preponderance from some aspects, prevents impossibility and necessitates permissibility. The answer: Consideration of preponderance, as it is said, gives precedence to the most learned, then the most pious, then the oldest, or the consolidation of the matter, and blocking the way of opposition by the mere pledge of allegiance of some, even one, repels fitna. Moreover, the fitna of dispute over the appointment of the imam, in comparison to the evils of the absence of an imam, is attached to non-existence. It cannot be said: The aforementioned argument, even if complete, does not negate the obligation upon God, nor upon the Prophet (peace be upon him) by designation, nor upon the previous imam by appointment of a successor. For we say: The intent is to negate what the majority hold of obligation upon the servants if the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not appoint and the previous imam did not appoint a successor.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an argument against the obligation of appointing an imam. The author introduces the Khawarij and others who deny the obligation. Their reasoning is that appointing an imam incites fitna (civil strife) because people have conflicting desires and opinions, each faction favoring a different candidate, leading to wars. Therefore, such an act cannot be obligatory—rather it should be impermissible. However, they concede exceptions: if everyone agrees on one person, or if one person is uniquely qualified, or if someone is clearly preponderant, then appointment becomes possible (permissible). The refutation answers that considering preponderance (e.g., giving precedence to the most learned, then most pious, then oldest) and the fact that allegiance of even a few (or one) blocks the way of opposition and repels fitna. Moreover, the fitna of dispute over appointing an imam is negligible compared to the far greater evils that result from having no imam at all. The passage then addresses a potential objection: this argument does not negate the obligation on God, on the Prophet by explicit text, or on the previous imam to appoint a successor. The author clarifies that the debate is about the obligation on the community (the servants) when the Prophet has not designated anyone and the previous imam has not appointed a successor. Thus, the argument is directed against the majority view that it is obligatory on the community to appoint an imam.

Key Terms
الخوارج
الفتنة
نصب الإمام
بيعة
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij argument completely negates any obligation to appoint an imam in all circumstances.
Correction: The refutation shows that the argument only applies to the community when no previous designation exists, and even then, the potential fitna is outweighed by the greater harm of having no imam.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation against the claim that appointing an imam causes fitna?
A) Fitna is actually desirable
B) The fitna of appointment is less harmful than the fitna of having no imam
C) Appointing an imam is always permissible and never obligatory
D) People will never disagree on an imam

Key Takeaway
The potential fitna of appointing an imam is far less harmful than the certain fitna of having no imam, so the obligation remains on the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided taking on a leadership role in your community or family because you feared it would cause conflict or fitna? How does this lesson challenge that fear and reframe the harm of leaving a vacuum?
?
When you see disagreements in the Muslim community about who should lead, do you hold back from supporting anyone because you want to avoid 'fitna'? What does this lesson teach you about the greater fitna of having no leadership at all, and how might you now engage differently?
?
Think of a time when you criticized a leader because you saw their faults. Did your criticism help the community, or did it create division? How does the principle of preferring the most learned and pious—even if imperfect—guide your attitude towards obedience and support?

Basic Conditions of the Imam
Enumeration of seven conditions for the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven conditions for the imam according to the majority, starting from taklif to ijtihad and correctness of opinion.

Core Idea
The imam must be a free, male, just, adult Muslim with bravery, ijtihad, and sound judgment.

The author begins a new chapter enumerating the necessary qualifications for the imam. He first lists four obvious conditions – legal responsibility, freedom, masculinity, and justice – then mentions three more added by the majority: bravery, ijtihad, and correctness of opinion. This lesson unpacks each condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the list of conditions.
The passage is a list of conditions for the imam.
2
Identify the four basic conditions
Identify taklif, hurriya, dhukura, adala. Why are these considered obvious?
3
Understand the three added conditions
Shaja'a, ijtihad, isabat al-ra'y. What practical purpose does each serve?
The passage explains the rationale for each.
4
Reflect on the overall list
Consider why these conditions are necessary for the role of imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني التكليف والحرية والذكورة والعدالة وذلك ظاهر. وزاد الجمهور الشجاعة ليقيم الحدود، ويقاوم الخصوم، والاجتهاد ليقوم بمصالح الدين، وإصابة الرأي ليقوم الأمور. وكونه قريشياً لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم ) الأئمة من قريش) . الولاية من قريش، قدموا قريشاً ولا تقدموها». و خالفت الخوارج وأكثر المعتزلة لقوله ) : «أطيعوا ولو أمر عليكم عبد حبشي أجدع .
He said: The second chapter: Legal responsibility (taklif), freedom, masculinity, and justice, and that is apparent. And the majority added bravery to carry out the prescribed punishments and to resist opponents, and ijtihad to manage religious interests, and correctness of opinion to manage affairs. And that he be from Quraysh because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' The authority is from Quraysh; give precedence to Quraysh and do not precede them. And the Khawarij and most of the Mu'tazila disagreed because of his saying: 'Obey even if a black Abyssinian slave with a mutilated nose is appointed over you.'
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a new section (mabḥath thānī) enumerating the conditions required for an imam. The author lists four basic conditions: taklif (legal responsibility, i.e., being adult and sane), hurriya (freedom, i.e., not a slave), dhukūra (masculinity), and ʿadāla (justice). He notes that these are obvious. Then he mentions that the majority (al-jumhūr) add three more conditions: shajāʿa (bravery) to carry out prescribed punishments (ḥudūd) and resist opponents; ijtihād (independent legal reasoning) to manage religious affairs; and iṣābat al-raʾy (correctness of opinion) to administer matters properly. Additionally, the imam must be from the Quraysh tribe, citing the hadith: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' The phrase 'al-wilāya min Quraysh' and the instruction 'qaddimū Qurayshan wa lā tuqaddimūhā' emphasize Quraysh precedence. Finally, the author notes that the Khawarij and most Muʿtazila disagreed with the Quraysh condition, relying on another hadith: 'Obey even if a black Abyssinian slave with a mutilated nose is appointed over you.' This indicates they accept any qualified person regardless of lineage.

Key Terms

الحرية

العدالة
الشجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only basic conditions like justice matter; bravery is optional.
Correction: The majority include bravery as essential to execute punishments and defend the community.

Key Takeaway
The imam must possess seven key qualities: legal responsibility, freedom, masculinity, justice, bravery, ijtihad, and sound judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The imam must have 'ijtihad' to manage religious interests. You may not be an imam, but you are responsible for your own religious practice. Where have you lazily followed others without thinking critically about your faith? Will you commit to studying one Islamic issue with depth this week?
?
Sound judgment is not just for leaders—you also make decisions that affect others. Think of a recent decision you made that betrayed good judgment due to haste or desire. Repent and resolve a concrete step to seek counsel and think more carefully next time.

The Quraysh Condition and Its Dispute
Presentation of Quraysh condition and sectarian disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the requirement that the imam be from Quraysh and the disagreement of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The imam must be from Quraysh, but some groups reject this based on a different hadith.

After listing the general conditions, the author addresses a specific requirement: the imam must be from the Quraysh tribe. He cites a hadith to support this, then notes that the Khawarij and most Mu'tazila disagree, relying on another hadith. This lesson explores the lineage condition and the sectarian difference.

The Fourth Argument: Imamate as Divine Appointment vs. Community Choice
Objection and reply regarding imamate as divine appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that imamate must be by explicit divine appointment and the reply that consensus (ijmāʿ) transforms community choice into divine appointment, making the imam a deputy of both God and the community.

Core Idea
Community choice, validated by consensus, makes the imam God's deputy, not just the community's.

The fourth argument in the discussion of imamate raises a key objection: if the imam is a successor of God and the Prophet, how can mere human election appoint him? The author responds by invoking the force of consensus (ijmāʿ), which transforms community choice into divine appointment. This passage shows how Islamic legal theory uses collective agreement to bridge the gap between divine authority and human governance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The objection states that imamate as succession from God and the Messenger requires their explicit appointment, so community choice cannot produce a true caliph.
Notice the term 'khilāfa' – it implies representation of God and the Messenger.
2
Identify the reply's core argument
The author invokes consensus (ijmāʿ) as a proof from the Lawgiver. When consensus establishes that the community's chosen one is the caliph of God and His Messenger, the objection falls away.
Consensus here functions as a mechanism that makes the community's choice equivalent to divine appointment.
3
Analyze the supporting analogy
The author uses the analogy of a witness's testimony, a judge's ruling, and a mufti's fatwa: though issued by humans, they are considered God's judgment. Similarly, the imam is a deputy of God, even if chosen by the community.
This analogy reinforces that human acts can carry divine authority.
4
Reflect on the dual deputyship
The passage concludes that the imam is a deputy of God and also a deputy of the community. This dual role reconciles divine appointment with community choice.
This balances theological and political legitimacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن الإمامة خلافة الله (۲) ورسوله فتتوقف على استخلافهما بوسط أو لا بوسط . والثابت باختيار الأمة لا يكون خلافة منهما ، بل من الأمة، ورد بأنه لما قام الدليل من قبل الشارع وهو الإجماع على أن من اختاره الأمة خليفة الله ورسوله، كان خليفة سقط ما ذكرتم ألا ترى أن الوجوب بشهادة الشاهد (۳) وقضاء القاضي، وفتوى المفتي حكم الله لا حكمهم على أن الإمام وإن كان نائباً لله فهو نائب للأمة أيضاً .
The fourth: that the imamate is the caliphate (khilāfa) of God and His Messenger, so it depends on their appointment as caliphs, either through an intermediary or without. And what is established by the choice of the community is not a caliphate from them, but from the community. The reply: because when the proof from the Lawgiver – which is consensus (ijmāʿ) – established that whoever the community chooses is the caliph of God and His Messenger, he becomes caliph, and what you mentioned falls away. Do you not see that the obligation by the testimony of the witness, the judgment of the judge, and the fatwa of the mufti is God's judgment, not their judgment? Moreover, the imam, even if he is a deputy of God, is also a deputy of the community.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer regarding the nature of the imamate. The objection states that since the imamate is a succession from God and the Messenger, it must depend on explicit appointment by them, either directly or through an intermediary. Therefore, an imam chosen merely by the community's election cannot be a true caliph; he would be a representative of the community, not of God. The author replies by invoking consensus (ijmāʿ): when the Lawgiver (the Prophet) has established through consensus that the one chosen by the community is indeed the caliph of God and His Messenger, then he becomes such. The objection falls away. The author supports this by analogy: just as a witness's testimony, a judge's ruling, and a mufti's fatwa are considered God's judgment despite being issued by humans, so too the imam, though chosen by the community, is both a deputy of God and a deputy of the community. Thus, election by the community does not negate divine appointment; rather, the community's choice is the mechanism by which God's appointment is realized.

خلافة
اختيار الأمة
إجماع
نائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Election by the community makes the imam a representative only of the community, not of God.
Correction: The author explains that when consensus (ijmāʿ) validates the community's choice, the imam becomes both a deputy of God and a deputy of the community, as established by the Lawgiver.

Checkpoint

A) By arguing that the community's choice is invalid without explicit divine text.
B) By citing consensus (ijmāʿ) that such an imam is indeed the caliph of God and His Messenger.
C) By stating that the imam is only a deputy of the community, not of God.
D) By claiming that the imamate does not require divine appointment.

Key Takeaway
The imam, though chosen by the community, is validated as God's deputy through the mechanism of consensus, making him a deputy of both God and the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose a leader to follow—whether in religious or worldly matters—do you base your choice on personal preference or on evidence that the leader is truly a deputy of God? How can you know that the one you follow is chosen by divine will as validated by consensus of the righteous?
?
The imam is a deputy of God and also of the community. In your own life, whose authority do you truly submit to: God's, your own desires, or the community's? Are you willing to let go of your own opinion when the community, based on divine proof, agrees on a matter that you find difficult?
?
If consensus (ijmāʿ) is a proof from the Lawgiver, then obedience to the community's chosen leader is obedience to God. Ask yourself: Is there any authority in your life that you resist, even though the consensus of trusted scholars validates it? What would it take for you to accept that resistance as a form of disobedience to God?

The Fifth Argument: Temporal Void in Imam Leadership
Contrasts objection and reply on temporal void in imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that election can lead to a time without an imam and the reply that appointment is possible when the existence of an imam is unknown.

Core Idea
Election's risk of temporal void is answered by appointment when no imam is known.

In this passage, the author presents a fifth objection against the doctrine of election for the imamate. The objection envisions a scenario where two towns each elect a qualified person with unknown precedence, leading to a potential void. The reply argues that an imam can still be appointed when the existence of a valid imam is unknown, and that factors can be weighed to give preference. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise argumentative style.
Pay attention to the key terms like khalw al-zamān (temporal void) and tarjīḥ (weighing).
2
Identify the objection scenario
The objection describes a situation where two towns each elect a qualified imam, and it is unknown which election occurred first. Consider why this creates a problem.
The problem is that we cannot judge both valid (due to possible simultaneity) nor both invalid (due to possible precedence), nor determine the correct one.
3
Understand the consequences
Because no valid imam can be identified, no new imam can be appointed either, as any new appointment might be a second imam. This would leave a time without any valid imam, which is deemed invalid by consensus.
This is the core of the objection: election leads to an imamless period.
4
Examine the reply
The author replies simply: an imam may be appointed when the existence of a valid imam is unknown. Moreover, preference (tarjīḥ) can be given through various considerations, so a temporal void is not inevitable.
Consider how this reply preserves the possibility of maintaining an imam even in complex scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - أن القول بالاختيار يؤدي إلى خلو الزمان عن الإمام، وهو باطل بالاتفاق، وذلك فيما إذا عقد أهل بلدتين لمستعدين، ولم يعلم السبق. فإنه لا يمكن الحكم بصحتهما لاحتمال المقارنة ولا بفسادهما لاحتمال السبق ، ولا بتعين الصحيح لعدم الوقوف، وحينئذ لا يمكن نصب إمام آخر لاحتمال كونه ثانياً، ورد بأنه ينصب إمام بعدم العلم بوجود الإمام على أنه يمكن الترجيح بجهاته .
The fifth: that the doctrine of election leads to a time without an imam, which is false by agreement. This occurs when the people of two towns contract allegiance to two qualified persons, and the precedence is unknown. For it is impossible to judge both as valid due to the possibility of simultaneity, nor both as invalid due to the possibility of precedence, nor to determine the correct one due to the lack of knowledge. Then no other imam can be appointed due to the possibility that he would be a second. The reply: an imam is appointed due to the lack of knowledge of the existence of an imam, and it is possible to weigh the factors (tarjīḥ) through considerations.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection against the doctrine of election (ikhtiyār) for the imamate. The objection argues that if imams are chosen by election, a scenario arises where two different groups in two towns each elect a qualified person, and it is unknown which election occurred first. In such a case, we cannot rule both elections valid (because they might have occurred simultaneously) nor both invalid (because one might have preceded), nor can we determine the correct imam due to lack of knowledge of the precedence. Consequently, no imam can be appointed because any new appointment might be a second, and a time without an imam would result, which is unanimously rejected. The author replies simply: an imam may be appointed when the existence of a valid imam is unknown, and moreover, it is possible to give preference (tarjīḥ) through various considerations (e.g., qualifications, location). Thus the feared temporal void does not necessarily occur.

Key Terms
اختيار
خلو الزمان
عدة إمام
ترجيح
سبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any conflict in election inevitably leads to an imam-less period.
Correction: The author shows that appointment can still occur when no valid imam is known, and tarjīḥ can resolve conflicts.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem raised in the fifth argument against election?
A) That elected imams are always corrupt.
B) That simultaneous elections by two towns could leave no valid imam and prevent new appointments.
C) That election contradicts the Quran.
D) That the imam must be from Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
The objection of temporal void in election is rebutted by allowing imam appointment when no valid imam is known, with preference given through tarjīḥ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you cannot determine the correct course of action among multiple options, do you tend to delay indefinitely (like the objection's void) or do you act based on the best available weight (tarjīḥ)? What is one decision you have postponed because of uncertainty that you should now act on?
?
Consider a time when you avoided committing to a leader or a path because you were unsure who was right. How does the reply's principle—'appoint an imam when no valid imam is known'—challenge your tendency to wait for perfect clarity? What step of trust are you being called to take today?
?
The objection argues that uncertainty leads to a void. Is there an area of your faith where you have used uncertainty as an excuse to remain inactive? If so, what would it look like to 'appoint' a direction even without full knowledge, trusting that Allah will guide you through tarjīḥ (weighing factors)?

The Sixth Argument: The Prophet's Practice of Appointing Successors
Objection and answer from the Prophet's practice of appointing deputies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection based on the Prophet's meticulousness in appointing deputies and the response that delegation to community choice constitutes a form of appointment.

Core Idea
The Prophet's delegation to community choice is itself a form of succession.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against the Sunni position on the imamate: if the Prophet appointed deputies for even short absences and explained minor religious matters, how could he leave the imamate unaddressed? The response reframes the entire question by arguing that delegating the choice to the community is a kind of appointment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the rhetorical flow: the objection builds on the Prophet's established practice, and the answer shifts the frame.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the key terms like استخلاف and تفويض.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection argues by analogy: the Prophet never left even a short absence without a deputy, so how could he leave the permanent absence of death without appointing a successor? Also, he explained even trivial matters, so how could he omit explaining the imamate?
This is a strong rhetorical argument that seems to demand an explicit appointment.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer has two parts: first, calling the objection 'mere improbability' (استبعاد), not a logical proof. Second, redefining delegation to the choice of ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd as a form of appointment and explanation, analogous to many other religious matters.
Notice how the answer turns the tables: the Prophet did provide for the imamate by establishing the method of election.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس - أن سيرة النبي ) وطريقته على أنه كان لا يترك الاستخلاف على المدينة وغيرها من البلاد في غيبة مدة قليلة، ولا البيان في أدنى ما يحتاج إليه من الفرائض والسنن والآداب حتى في أمر قضاء الحاجة ومسح ذلك، فكيف يترك الاستخلاف في غيبة الوفاة والبيان فيما هو أساس المهمات؟ والجواب أن ذلك مجرد استبعاد على أن التفويض إلى اختيار أهل (٤) الحل والعقد واجتهاد أرباب أولي الألباب نوع استخلاف وبيان كما في كثير من فروع الإيمان .
The sixth: that the conduct (sīra) of the Prophet and his way was that he would never leave without appointing a successor over Medina and other towns during an absence of a short period, nor would he omit explanation of the least needed matters of obligations, sunnas, and etiquettes, even in the matter of relieving oneself and wiping that. How then would he leave without appointing a successor for the absence of death and without explanation of what is the foundation of important matters? The answer: that is mere improbability; moreover, delegating to the choice of the people of loosing and binding (ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd) and the ijtihād of the possessors of intellects is a kind of appointment and explanation, as in many branches of faith.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth objection based on the Prophet's established practice (sīra). The objection argues that the Prophet was meticulous in appointing deputies for brief absences from Medina and other towns, and he provided detailed explanations even for minor religious matters such as purification. By analogy, it is inconceivable that he would neglect to appoint a successor for the permanent absence of death and fail to clarify the matter of the imamate, which is the foundation of all important affairs. The author answers that this argument is merely an improbability (istiʿbād), not a logical necessity. Furthermore, the delegation of the choice of the imam to the people of loosing and binding and to the ijtihād of the qualified scholars is itself a form of appointment and explanation, just as in many other branches of faith (e.g., appointment of judges, scholars). Thus, the Prophet did effectively provide for the imamate by establishing the method of election.

Key Terms
سيرة
استخلاف
تفويض
أهل الحل والعقد
استبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's failure to explicitly name a successor means he left the imamate completely unaddressed.
Correction: The author argues that delegating the choice to the community is itself a form of appointment and explanation, so the imamate was not left unaddressed.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that the Prophet's practice requires him to have explicitly named a successor?
A) By stating that the Prophet did explicitly name Ali as his successor.
B) By arguing that the argument is merely an improbability and that delegation to election is itself a form of appointment.
C) By claiming that the imamate is not an important matter.
D) By denying that the Prophet ever appointed deputies.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's delegation of the choice of imam to the community is itself a form of succession and explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the most important decisions in your life—like leadership, marriage, or career—do you rely on explicit instructions from others, or are you willing to trust that God has placed the choice in your hands through principles and consultation? How does this lesson challenge your instinct to demand clear-cut answers?
?
If you were among the early Muslims, would you have felt dissatisfied with the Prophet's delegation of the imamate to the community? Are you willing to accept that divine guidance can come through collective ijtihād, or do you secretly wish for a more direct command?
?
This lesson refutes the argument that the Prophet's silence means negligence. Think of an area in your own life where you have demanded explicit guidance from God (a sign, a specific answer) and felt let down. Could it be that He has already guided you through the principles and the people He placed around you? Will you now actively look for that form of guidance?

The Seventh and Eighth Objections: Paternal Care and Perfected Religion
Presents two final objections: analogy with a caring father and the Quranic claim of religion's completion, with a shared answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh and eighth objections raised by proponents of explicit designation and why the author considers them already answered by earlier rebuttals.

Core Idea
The Prophet's care and the Quran's perfection do not force explicit designation—the earlier rebuttals suffice.

In this final part of the discussion, Sanusi presents two more objections that attempt to prove the necessity of explicit appointment of an imam. The first compares the Prophet to a caring father who would not leave his children without a guardian. The second invokes the Quranic verse stating that the religion has been perfected. Both are answered with a reference to the earlier responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the Arabic for the seventh and eighth objections. Identify the core analogy (compassionate father leaving a will) and the scriptural proof (Quran 5:3).
Notice how each objection builds on different premises: one from analogy, one from divine statement.
2
Understand the shared answer
The author does not give a new refutation but simply says 'the answer is like what preceded.' Ask yourself why these objections are considered already addressed by the earlier arguments.
This shows the author's confidence that the previous points cover all such objections.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these objections fit into the broader debate about whether the imam must be explicitly designated or can be elected by the community.
If the earlier rebuttals suffice, then explicit textual appointment is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع - أن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم ) كان لأمته بمنزلة الأب الشفيق لأولاده الصغار، وهو لا يترك الوصية في الأولاد إلى واحد يصلح لذلك فكذا النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في حق الأمة. الثامن - قوله تعالى : اليوم أكملت لكم دينكم ) ولا خفاء في أن الإمامة من معظمات أمر الدين ، فيكون قد بينها وأكملها إما في كتابه وإما على لسان نبيه . والجواب عنهما بمثل ما سبق .
The seventh: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was for his community like a compassionate father to his young children, and he does not leave the testament (waṣiyya) regarding the children to someone suitable for that; so likewise the Prophet (peace be upon him) in regard to the community. The eighth: His saying, the Exalted: 'Today I have perfected for you your religion' – and there is no doubt that the imamate is among the greatest matters of the religion, so He must have clarified it and perfected it, either in His Book or on the tongue of His Prophet. The answer for both is like what preceded.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh and eighth objections with a single shared answer. The seventh objection draws an analogy: the Prophet is like a compassionate father who would certainly appoint a guardian for his young children before his death; similarly, he must have appointed a successor for the community. The eighth objection cites the Quranic verse (5:3) which states that God has perfected the religion, implying that the imamate, being a major component of the religion, must have been clarified; otherwise the religion would be incomplete. The author answers both objections concisely: the answer is the same as what preceded, i.e., the same responses given in the earlier arguments (for example, that election by the community is a valid form of appointment, that delegation to ahl al-ḥall wa-l-ʿaqd constitutes clarification, etc.). Thus, these objections do not introduce new points that require a separate refutation beyond what has already been said.

Key Terms
أب شفيق
وصية
إكمال الدين
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that these objections prove that explicit textual designation of the imam is necessary and that without it the religion would be imperfect.
Correction: The author maintains that the earlier rebuttals suffice; the objections do not require new refutation. The perfection of religion does not mean every detail must be explicitly spelled out—some matters can be settled by community consensus or reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the shared answer to the seventh and eighth objections?
A) That these objections are irrelevant.
B) That the imamate is not a major matter.
C) That the answer is the same as what preceded in earlier arguments.
D) That the Prophet did explicitly appoint a successor.

Key Takeaway
The final objections from paternal analogy and religious perfection do not require new refutations because the earlier responses already neutralize them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet is called a compassionate father who would not neglect his children. Do you truly believe that he cared for you personally? If so, why do you sometimes act as if you need no guidance from his teachings or the community of scholars? Will you commit to seeking that guidance actively?
?
The verse 'Today I have perfected your religion' is used to demand explicit texts for everything. But are you willing to accept that God's perfection includes leaving some matters to collective reasoning? Examine your heart: do you trust God's plan even when it is not spelled out in every detail?
?
Both objections seem reasonable, yet the author dismisses them with a reference to earlier arguments. When have you been convinced by a seemingly strong argument that later turned out to be flawed? How will you train yourself to test your assumptions before accepting them?

General Principles of Imamate Dissolution
Sanusi states the general rule and lists initial causes of dissolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general statement that the imamate contract dissolves by anything that nullifies its purpose, and the initial list of causes including apostasy, madness, illness, resignation, and being overpowered.

Core Idea
The imamate contract dissolves when its purpose is destroyed.

This lesson introduces the conclusion (khatima) on the dissolution of the imamate contract. The author begins with a concise statement that the contract is dissolved by whatever nullifies its purpose. He then lists several causes and notes a disagreement that will be elaborated later. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the concise style of Sanusi here.
2
Identify the listed causes
Make note of the causes mentioned: apostasy, madness, some illnesses, resignation due to a reason, and being overpowered. Also note the mention of disagreement about two matters.
3
Reflect on the purpose of the imamate
Consider what the 'purpose of the imamate' might be. The passage implies that anything that nullifies this purpose dissolves the contract.
This will be expanded in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : خاتمة (عقد الإمامة ينحل بما يخل بمقصودها كالردة والجنون، وبعض الأمراض، وبخلعه نفسه بسبب وبالغلبة عليه إذا صار إماماً بالغلبة. واختلف في خلعه نفسه بلا سبب وفي انعزاله بالفسق . ينحل عقد الإمامة بما يزول به مقصود الإمامة كالردة والجنون المطبق وصيرورته أسيراً لا يرجى خلاصه ، وكذا بالمرض الذي ينسيه العلوم وبالعمى، والصمم والخرس، وكذا بخلعه نفسه لعجزه عن القيام بمصالح المسلمين، وإن لم يكن ظاهراً، بل استشعره في نفسه. وعليه يحمل خلع الحسن (رضي الله تعالى عنه) نفسه . وأما خلعه لنفسه بلا سبب ففيه خلاف وكذا في إنعزاله بالفسق . والأكثرون على أنه لا ينعزل . وهو المختار من مذهب الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه وأبي حنيفة، وعن محمد (رضي الله تعالى عنهما) روايتان . ويستحق العزل بالاتفاق. ومن صار إماماً بالقهر والغلبة ينعزل بأن يقهره آخر ويغلبه.وأما القاضي فينعزل بالفسق على الأظهر.
He said: Conclusion (khatima): The imamate contract is dissolved by what nullifies its purpose, such as apostasy, madness, some illnesses, his resignation due to a reason, and by being overpowered if he became imam by overpowering. And there is disagreement regarding his resignation without a cause and regarding his removal due to sin. The imamate contract is dissolved by whatever removes the purpose of the imamate, such as apostasy, continuous madness, his becoming a captive with no hope of release, likewise illness that makes him forget knowledge, blindness, deafness, muteness, and likewise his resignation due to his inability to manage the interests of the Muslims, even if it is not apparent but he perceives it in himself. Upon this is based the resignation of al-Ḥasan (may God be pleased with him). As for his resignation without a cause, there is disagreement about it, and likewise regarding his removal due to sin. The majority hold that he is not removed. And that is the chosen view in the school of al-Shāfiʿī (may God be pleased with him) and Abū Ḥanīfa, and from Muḥammad (may God be pleased with them both) there are two narrations. And he deserves removal by agreement. And whoever becomes imam by force and overpowering is removed by being overpowered by another. As for the judge, he is removed due to sin according to the most apparent view.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section (khatima) on the dissolution of the imamate contract. It begins by stating that the contract dissolves through anything that nullifies its purpose: apostasy, madness, some illnesses, resignation due to a valid reason, and being overpowered if the imam originally came to power by force. It then notes a disagreement about resignation without a cause and removal due to sin (fisq). The text then provides a more detailed list of conditions that remove the purpose of the imamate: apostasy, continuous madness, being captured with no hope of release, illnesses that cause forgetfulness, blindness, deafness, muteness, and resignation due to inability to manage Muslim interests even if only self-perceived. The example of al-Ḥasan's resignation is cited as falling under this. Then it addresses the disputed cases: resignation without cause and removal due to sin. The majority (al-aktharūn) hold that the imam is not removed by sin, and this is the adopted view in the schools of al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa, though there are two narrations from Muḥammad (al-Shaybānī). However, the imam deserves removal (i.e., it is recommended but not automatic) by agreement. Finally, it states that an imam who came to power by force is removed if another overpowers him, and a judge (qāḍī) is removed due to sin according to the most apparent view. The passage thus distinguishes between automatic dissolution and contested causes.

Key Terms
عقد الإمامة
انحلال
ردة
جنون
خلع
غلبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a cause of dissolution?
A) Apostasy
B) Madness
C) Death
D) Being overpowered

Key Takeaway
The imamate contract dissolves whenever its purpose is nullified, and the initial list includes apostasy, madness, illness, resignation with cause, and being overpowered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own relationship with authority figures in your life—parents, teachers, or leaders. If they were to lose the purpose for which you follow them (e.g., justice, guidance), would you feel released from your obligation? Have you ever stayed in a situation where the purpose was already nullified, out of habit or fear?
?
The imamate dissolves when its purpose is nullified. What is the ultimate purpose of your own life’s commitments—your marriage, your job, your friendships? Are you actively ensuring that those relationships serve their intended purpose, or have you allowed them to drift into purposelessness?
?
Scholars disagree on whether an imam can resign without cause or be removed for sin. How do you handle religious rulings that have legitimate scholarly disagreement? Do you demand a single clear answer, or can you live with ambiguity while still taking a position? And what does that say about your trust in God's guidance through scholarly diversity?

Automatic Causes of Imamate Dissolution
Sanusi enumerates the specific causes that automatically dissolve the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed list of conditions that automatically dissolve the imamate contract, including apostasy, continuous madness, captivity, incapacitating illnesses, and resignation due to inability.

Core Idea
The imamate ends automatically when the imam lacks the capacity to lead.

This lesson expands on the general principle by enumerating specific conditions that remove the purpose of the imamate. The author mentions apostasy, continuous madness, captivity, diseases that cause forgetfulness, blindness, deafness, muteness, and resignation when the imam knows he cannot fulfil his duties. The example of Ḥasan's resignation illustrates the last point.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed list
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the conditions: apostasy, continuous madness, captivity, illness that causes forgetfulness, blindness, deafness, muteness, and resignation due to inability.
2
Understand the rationale
Each condition nullifies the purpose of the imamate, which is to manage the affairs of the Muslims. For example, a blind or deaf imam cannot fulfil judicial duties.
3
Examine the example of Ḥasan
The author cites Ḥasan's resignation as an example of resignation due to perceived inability, showing that it is valid even if not manifest.
This distinguishes it from resignation without cause.

Source Text

?

?

?
Sanusi mentions illness that makes the imam forget knowledge. How does this apply to your own learning? Do you guard your knowledge by consistent review, or do you let it fade? Are you responsible for safeguarding the knowledge you have been given?

Disputed Grounds for Imamate Dissolution
Sanusi presents the disputed cases and the majority view on removal by sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether an imam can be removed for resigning without cause or committing sin, and the rules for removal by force and for judges.

Core Idea
Not all sins cause removal; the majority hold the imam is not removed by sin.

Having covered the automatic causes, the author now turns to two disputed cases: resignation without a valid reason and removal due to sin (fisq). He presents the majority view that the imam is not removed by sin, citing the schools of al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa. He also discusses the case of an imam who came to power by force and the special rule for judges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on disagreements
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two disputed issues: resignation without cause and removal due to sin.
2
Understand the majority position
The passage states that the majority (al-aktharūn) hold that the imam is not removed by sin. This is the chosen view in the Shāfiʿī and Ḥanafī schools.
3
Learn the exception for judges
The passage concludes by stating that a judge (qāḍī) is removed due to sin according to the most apparent view, unlike the imam.
4
Examine the rule for the forceful imam
An imam who came to power by force is removed if another overpowers him. This is a specific condition.

Source Text

?

?
Think of a time you felt justified in wanting someone's removal. Knowing that the majority hold that an imam is not removed by sin, does that reveal anything about your own need for control or perfection? What would it take for you to accept a flawed leader?
